Comprehensive Collection of Ancient and Modern Books, Medical Section, Volume Five Hundred and One

General Discussion

Book of Changes

Without Falsehood Hexagram

Nine in the fifth position: If one suffers from the malady of without falsehood, do not use medicine; there will be good fortune.
[Cheng's Commentary: When a person is ill, medicine and stones are used to expel the evil and nourish the right. If the vital energy is harmonious and there is no illness, then to attack and treat it will instead harm the right nature; thus, not using medicine brings good fortune. Good fortune refers to the illness disappearing by itself.]

The image states: The medicine of without falsehood cannot be tried.
[Cheng's Commentary: If a person has delusions, they must rectify the situation. If there is truly no delusion, but medicine is used to treat it, it becomes delusion again; how can it be used? Hence, it cannot be tried. To try means to use temporarily, somewhat like tasting it. The essence is that if there is already no delusion and one still uses medicine, it becomes delusion again and brings about illness. In the Great Complete Book, Zhang of the Xi family says: Without delusion yet ill, and without delusion yet medicated, it becomes delusion and causes illness again. This is why the medicine of without falsehood cannot be tried. Confucius said: "I do not dare to taste it; the sage does not try the medicine of delusion thus.”]

Book of Documents

On the Command

If the medicine does not cause drowsiness or dizziness, then the illness will not heal.[1p]
The Commentary by Confucius states: "When taking medicine, one must experience extreme dizziness; only then can the illness be cured." The commentary explains that dizziness leads to a dazed state of mind. The "Dialectics" mentions that consuming medicine that is toxic, especially in the regions between Dongqi and Haidai, is referred to as 'dizziness' or 'light-headedness.' Guo Pu notes that 'dizziness' is a common expression. Thus, the act of the medicine attacking the illness first causes a state of confusion, and only then can healing occur. When it is said that dizziness is extreme, it indicates that the medicine works only when the mind is deeply affected.

In the "Book of Rites," it states: "When a ruler is ill and takes medicine, a minister must first taste it. If a parent is ill and takes medicine, a child must first taste it. A doctor must not administer medicine without a lineage of three generations." The Great Compilation by the Yanling Fang clan asserts: "In ordinary times, when the ruler is not engaged in affairs, the servants taste the dishes, and the crown prince personally observes; this is to maintain caution. Thus, during illness, it is even more critical not to neglect this practice. If a medical skill is not passed down from father to son, it will not be refined. If the skill is not refined, how can its medicine be taken? The Zhou official's minister teaches based on experience, which is why this is stated. However, what is mentioned in the classics merely describes the norm. As for those who acquire knowledge not through inheritance but through personal understanding, they may still be accepted within three generations. Therefore, the Zhou officials' physicians only regard the perfectly comprehensive method as the best; if the knowledge is not passed to the right person, it is not accepted, even after three generations. Hence, Confucius remarked that those without constancy should not be allowed to serve as shamans or doctors." The "Collected Commentaries" by Zheng states: "One must assess their own capacity; a doctor should not administer medicine unless it has been tried on three generations; cautiousness must be observed."[2p]
Confucius said: When a person is ill, they must take medicine. If a father and son pass down their knowledge through three generations, they can avoid mistakes and maintain balance. Furthermore, it is said: the three generations refer to the "Yellow Emperor's Acupuncture," the "Shennong's Materia Medica," and the "Sunu's Pulse Formula." Without studying these three ancient texts, one should not take their medicine.

Lü of Lantian said: Confucius emphasized caution regarding diseases, warfare, and calamities. Disease is a serious matter. If one is not careful in the face of danger, they invite disaster, especially in the case of illnesses affecting one's ruler or parent. If a medicine makes one dizzy or fails to cure an illness, then the medicine aimed at curing the illness is never without toxicity. Preferences or aversions may lead to harmful outcomes, measuring doses may be inappropriate, and treating cold with heat or vice versa can have counterproductive effects. Mild misuse could worsen an ailment, and severe misuse could lead to death. As a subject or child, one must not use medicine without careful trial; there is no greater unfilial or disloyal act than that. This is why the heir is blamed for his ruler's assassination due to his failure to properly test the medicine.

Lü of Shanyin said: The knowledge passed down through three generations contains insights that cannot be easily discerned. The world says: the old practitioner provides experience, while the young one offers divination.[3p]
Zhou Li

Tian Guan

There are two upper physicians, four lower physicians, two officials, two historians, and twenty apprentices.

[Commentary: Zheng Kangcheng said: The physician is the leader among doctors. Chen Hongfu said: The use of a person’s body, akin to that of heaven and earth, requires balance; excess leads to harm, and deficiency needs to be compensated, which is the way of heaven. Without an understanding of the principles of creation and the changes of fullness and emptiness, how could one know the specific conditions of a person's vital energy? This is indeed crucial to the smallest detail. How could later generations of mediocre physicians comprehend this matter, thus establishing Zhou with the gentlemen to oversee it?]

[Xue Pingzhong said: From the cook to the butcher, cooks are interconnected in their duties, thus the position of food physician can also be aligned with them. It is special to place them among the physicians, truly reflecting the way a ruler protects and nurtures. However, the former kings did not want to indulge themselves at the expense of the people, which is why they appointed physicians ahead of food physicians; to manage diseases affecting the populace. After establishing food physicians, the roles of disease physicians, wound physicians, and animal physicians followed in sequence. Indeed, kindness originates from oneself and extends to the people, and concern for living beings truly arises from each sip and morsel.]

The administration of medical affairs gathers poisonous substances for the purposes of medicine.[4p]
Liu Zhizhong said: The physician, who is in charge of medical affairs, must gather toxic medications, understand the suitability for different regions, know the appropriate times for collection, have methods for processing, and protocols for treatment. Only with complete knowledge can one participate in medical matters.

In any state with diseases, those with ulcers should be treated separately by physicians, and at the end of each year, their medical affairs should be reviewed to determine food provisions. Full marks are considered the highest; a loss of one in ten is acceptable, two in ten is a warning, three in ten is significant, and four in ten is deemed inferior.

Wang said: With adequate provisions recognized, the capable should encourage the incapable to strive.

Shi stated: Assessing losses and prioritizing efficiency, physicians fear their shortcomings deeply.

The two physicians overseeing dietary matters are responsible for the six types of food, drink, and culinary presentations under the king's regime, ensuring the meals of gentlemen are consistently exemplary.

Zheng Jieqing remarked: In ancient times, the historians, music officials, and those in charge of medicine and divination all dedicated themselves to their professions, refraining from overlapping roles to maintain precision. A physician does not exceed three generations in practice without proper medication.[5p]
Those who serve above with their skills find it particularly difficult in medicine; it is vital to be cautious concerning diseases. How could the ancient kings dare to rely on a single person to bear the skills of two or three? Therefore, beneath the physician, there are specialists for ailments, and dietary management does not overlap with treating illnesses; beneath the ailment physician, there are ulcer specialists, who examine internal conditions without overlapping with external signs; beneath the ulcer physician, there are veterinarians, treating animals without overlapping with humans. One must pursue excellence without wishing for superfluousness; this illustrates the benevolence of the ancient kings.

Among the physicians of ailments, there are eight specialists, responsible for the health of all people’s diseases by regulating the five flavors, five grains, and five medicines to nurture their ailments.

According to Zheng Kangcheng, the five medicines include herbs, trees, insects, stones, and grains. "Nurture" is akin to "treat." Diseases arise from the balance of qi, so one must attack what is excessive and nurture what is deficient. As the Wang family stated, "The Suwen says, when the form is insufficient, warm it with qi; when the essence is insufficient, replenish it with flavors." Flavors nurture the essence, grains nurture the form, and medicines treat the illnesses. Nurturing the essence is fundamental, nurturing the form is secondary, and treating diseases is the last priority. Lü's teachings suggest first using the five flavors and five grains, then following up with five medicines, acknowledging that treating with medicine comes last. Yet, is it only physicians who practice this?

Through the five qi, five sounds, and five colors, one discerns between life and death.[6p]
Interpretation: Jia states that internal illnesses are not visible to others; however, by observing a person's sounds and color, one can discern the severity of their condition. Wang further explains that by looking at their breath, listening to their sounds, and watching their color, one can determine life or death based on five indicators, and using food and medicine to treat the illness also follows this five-fold principle. Thus, changes in the body and movements of the internal organs can be regarded similarly. When people suffer from illness, they should be treated based on their specific conditions, and at the end of the year, each should document these for the physician.

Interpretation: Jia notes that for illnesses, there are eight skilled practitioners who specialize in treating them, necessitating a division in treatment. The eight practitioners responsible for treating ulcers and wounds manage the blessings, medications, cuts, and cauterizations involved in healing.

Interpretation: It is my humble understanding that medical practices rely on blessings, which are perhaps reasonable. In contemporary times, some use techniques like sealing ulcers based on this traditional method. If the blessings do not help, medication is used; if the medicine is ineffective, cutting away the bad blood is necessary; if cutting does not heal, additional medication is required to remove the harmful tissue. Each method has varying degrees of applicability, hence it is said they are comparable.

Spring and Autumn Annals, Four Commentaries

Xǔ Shìzǐ Resists Taking Medicine.[7p]
"Spring and Autumn Annals": In the 19th year of Duke Zhao's reign, in the summer of the fifth month on the day of Wuchan, the heir of Xu made an attempt to assassinate his lord, Mai. "Zuo Zhuan": Duke Dao of Xu fell ill and took the medicine that the crown prince had prescribed, and ultimately the crown prince fled to Jin, where it was recorded: he assassinated his lord. A gentleman said: One should dedicate their heart and strength to serving their lord; it is permissible to forego medicine. Hu’s commentary on the Zuo tradition states that Duke Dao of Xu was ill and took the medicine prescribed by the heir; the record states he assassinated his lord because he did not try the medicine first. In ancient times, a physician would not administer medicine across three generations; one would not take medicine administered by others. The things the Master was cautious about were three, with illness being one of them. When Ji Kangzi sent medicine, he said: "Qiu has not yet understood, therefore I dare not try it." If the lord is ill and drinks medicine, the minister must try it first; if the father is ill and drinks medicine, the son must try it first. This highlights the importance of caution. If one hastily uses medicine without choosing a proper physician, and advances medicine untested to the lord, this reflects a neglect of the lordly and paternal respect, showing a lack of caution.

[In the comprehensive account, Zhang says: The reason a medication might lead to death is manifold; thus, we speak of illness. Today's treatments for malaria involve arsenic and often lead to cures, however, improper preparation has led to many deaths. The death of Duke Dao must have been of this sort. Commentary states: According to the records, Duke Xu consumed the prescribed medicine and passed away, indeed not an assassination, but blaming the heir for not leaving behind the medicinal concerns suggests that one should trust the physician rather than administer it themselves. A filial son should perform with dedication and prayers only; the dosing of medicines is not something to be learned by experience. The heir of Xu served the nation, and the country was not without physicians; thus, hastily advancing medicine brought guilt akin to assassination, primarily to instruct on distant prevention.][8p]
The Yellow Emperor's Classic of Medicine

On the Treatise of Regulation of the Four Qi

The sage does not treat an already existing illness, but rather addresses the disease before it manifests; he does not remedy disorder after it has arisen, but instead seeks to prevent chaos beforehand. If one only administers medicine after the disease has developed, or intervenes after chaos has broken out, it is like digging a well when one is thirsty or forging weapons after a battle; is that not too late?[9p]
On the Discussion of Vitality and Its Communication to Heaven

Chronic illness leads to transformation; if the upper and lower do not align, a skilled physician should not intervene. Therefore, when yang accumulates, illness can lead to death, and the yang energy becomes isolated. Isolation must be resolved, and if not promptly treated, it can lead to decline.

On the True Words of the Golden Coffer

A good pulse practitioner carefully examines the five internal organs and six bowels, observing their interplay: one goes against, one follows, noting the correspondence of yin and yang, surface and interior, and the balance of male and female. This is understood in the heart and aligns with essence. One should not teach the unworthy nor impart the untrue; this is called attaining the Way.

On the Great Discussion of Yin and Yang Correspondences and Symbols

In treating illness, one must seek the root cause. Therefore, if treatment does not conform to heavenly principles or does not follow earthly rationale, calamity will ensue. Hence, when an evil wind arrives, it comes swiftly like wind and rain. A skilled healer addresses the skin first, then the flesh, followed by the tendons, then the six bowels, and finally the five internal organs, as treating the five organs can be a matter of life or death.

Thus, an adept needle practitioner draws yin from yang and vice versa; they treat the right side with knowledge of the left and the left side with understanding of the right; they recognize the other by knowing the self, the exterior by understanding the interior, and they discern excess and deficiency. By observing subtle signs and avoiding misdiagnosis, they use treatment effectively without peril.

A skilled diagnostician examines color and takes the pulse, first distinguishing yin from yang, clarifying purity and turbidity to determine affected portions. They observe respiration, listen to sounds, and identify areas of suffering, assess balance and proportion, and discern the primary illness by their pulse examination, skillfully diagnosing symptoms without error, thus ensuring accuracy in treatment.[10p]
Discussion of Yin and Yang

To differentiate from Yang is to know the location of illness; to differentiate from Yin is to know the time of life and death. To differentiate from Yang is to know the contraindications of illness; to differentiate from Yin is to know the time of life and death.

Discussion of the Spiritual Orchid's Secret Classics

The true way lies in the subtle, with endless transformations; who knows its origin? How perplexing! The dissipating element is elusive, who knows its essence? In a timely manner, who acts wisely? In the nebulous calculations, life emerges from minute details; the minutiae arise from measurements. Hundreds and thousands can lead to great benefits; extending it greatly, its form is accomplished. The Yellow Emperor said: "Well said! I have heard of the path of essence and light, the great saint's undertaking, which clarifies the great way. It is not through fasting and selecting auspicious days that one can dare to accept it." The Emperor then chose auspicious days with good omens and stored the Spiritual Orchid in his chambers to pass it on and preserve it.

Discussion of the Five Organs

In treating illness, one must first examine the lower parts, match the pulse, observe the spirit and intentions alongside the illness. Those bound by spirits and deities cannot discuss ultimate virtue. Those averse to needles and stones cannot discuss ultimate skill. When illness does not permit treatment, it must remain untreated, for attempts to treat it will be in vain.[11p]
Discussion on the Transformation of Essence and Qi

The Yellow Emperor asked: "I have heard that in ancient times, curing illnesses relied solely on the transformation of essence and qi, and could be done through blessings. In today's world, diseases are treated with poisonous medicines for internal issues, and needles and stones for external troubles. Sometimes one recovers, sometimes not; why is this?" Qi Bo replied: "In ancient times, people lived among beasts, moved to avoid the cold, and stayed still to escape the heat. Internally, they were free from emotional burdens, and externally, they were not burdened by social roles. This was a harmonious era, and thus, evil forces could not penetrate deeply. Therefore, poisonous medicines could not treat internal issues, and needles and stones could not address external troubles; hence, they could transform essence and bless as required. However, in today's world, it is not the same. Inner worries and external pains prevail, and people have lost harmony with the seasons, going against the natural flow of cold and heat. Harmful winds frequently arrive, and weakening evils come morning and night, penetrating the five organs and bones, injuring the skin and voids. Hence, minor ailments become severe, and major ailments lead to death; thus, blessings can no longer be effective."

The Emperor said: "I wish to observe the conditions of patients, to see life and death, to resolve doubts, and to understand the essentials, like the light of the sun and moon. Can this be conveyed to me?" Qi Bo replied: "The colors and pulses are what the sovereigns value, and they were passed down by the earlier masters. In ancient times, practitioners used to study the color and pulse to connect with the divine, aligning with the five elements—metal, wood, water, fire, earth—and the four seasons, eight winds, and the six harmonies, never straying from the norm. They would adapt their understanding to observe the intricacies and grasp the essentials. To know the essentials, the colors and pulses are key."[12p]
Color responds to the sun, and pulse responds to the moon; one consistently seeks the essence, and that essence is revealed. The changes in color reflect the seasonal energies; this is what the Supreme Deity values, aligning with the divine. Thus, one distances from death and approaches life, cultivating the path of existence, leading to the title of Sage King.

In ancient times, illness was treated by understanding its root. Boiling herbal liquids for ten days could dispel eight winds and five blockages. If illness persisted for ten days, one would then use the branches of herbal plants as aids. When the root cause was addressed, the harmful energies yielded.

However, in the later ages, treatment deviated from the four seasons, lacking an understanding of the sun and moon, and misjudging the trends of illness. When the symptoms were already established, they would seek to treat superficially with needles and internally with herbal liquids, using coarse methods rashly, believing they could attack the illness. As a result, the illness would persist, and new ailments would arise.

The Emperor said: "I wish to hear the essential principles!" Qi Bo replied: "The essence of treatment lies in ensuring no neglect of color and pulse. If one uses it without confusion, they achieve great results. Following or going against the natural order, if the root cause isn’t addressed, the spirit is lost and the state falls. By discarding the old and embracing the new, one can attain the status of a true human."

The Emperor said: "I have heard its essence from my teacher. My teacher spoke of color and pulse, which is what I know." Qi Bo replied: "The pinnacle of treatment lies in unity." The Emperor asked: "What do you mean by unity?" Qi Bo said: "Unity arises from understanding. The Emperor asked: "How can this be achieved?" Qi Bo said: "Close the doors and block the windows; inquire deeply about the patient's situation, to align with their intention. Those who gain the spirit thrive, while those who lose the spirit perish." The Emperor said: "Excellent."[13p]
Discussion on Decoctions and Fermented Liquor

The Emperor said: In ancient times, sages created decoctions and fermented liquors but did not use them; why is that? Qi Bo replied: Since ancient times, sages made these drinks as preparations. In ancient times, decoctions were made but not consumed. During the Middle Ages, the virtues declined slightly, and evil energies arrived, hence they were consumed for complete healing.

The Emperor asked: In today’s world, is it unnecessary to do so? Qi Bo replied: In the present age, one must directly attack poison with medicines for internal issues, while using needles and stones to treat external afflictions.

The Emperor asked: What about when the body is weak and blood is exhausted, and yet the treatment does not take effect? Qi Bo replied: This is because the spirit is not responsive. The Emperor asked: What does it mean that the spirit is unresponsive? Qi Bo replied: It refers to the methods of needles and stones. If the spirit does not advance and one's will is not directed toward healing, the illness cannot be cured.

The Emperor said: When an illness first manifests, it is extremely subtle and refined; it must first be lodged within the skin. Today, skilled doctors all say that when an illness is fully manifested, it is termed a reverse condition, hence needles and stones cannot treat it, nor can good medicines reach it. Nowadays, skilled practitioners have their methods and adhere to their practices, relatives and friends hear sounds daily, and they see colors every day, yet the illness does not heal; what flaw remains unaddressed? Qi Bo replied: The illness is the root, and the practitioner is merely the branch. Without grasping the root, the evil energy will not subside. This is what it means.[14p]
On Pain Discourse

The Yellow Emperor said: I have heard that those who articulate the heavenly truths must have their evidence in human experience; those who speak of the ancients must align with the present; those who discuss humanity must have a profound understanding of themselves. If so, then the Way is clear and the essential truths become apparent, which is what is called clarity.

On the Essentials of the Ultimate Truth

Qi Bo said: The emergence of diseases comes from various sources; some arise from the root, some from the superficial, and some from the inner Qi. Some are derived from the root, some from the superficial, some from the inner Qi, and some from a combination of superficial and root causes. There are those that arise from oppositional forces and those that are derived from harmonies. Opposition is in contrast to harmony; what is harmonious can become oppositional. Therefore, it is said knowing the superficial and the root will ensure no peril in their application. Understanding opposition and harmony allows for righteous practice without inquiries. This is what is meant. Not knowing this is insufficient for diagnosis and leads to disruptions in the meridians. Hence, the essence states: Rough practitioners laugh it off as knowable. Words on heat have not yet ceased, and cold diseases begin anew. Similar emitted energies of different forms confound diagnoses and disrupt the meridians. This is what is meant. The Emperor said: All diseases arise from wind, cold, heat, dampness, dryness, and fire; these transform and change. The classics say that the excessive should be drained, and the deficient should be supplemented. I bestow this to the practitioners, but they still have yet to achieve perfection. I wish to ensure that the essential Way is practiced; let the drums and gongs respond harmoniously, just like removing thorns and clearing dirt. Is there a skillful and divine method that can be shared? Qi Bo said: Careful observation of the condition of the disease must not neglect the critical energies. This is what is meant.[15p]
The Treatise on Medical Learning

The Yellow Emperor sat in the Hall of Illumination and summoned Lei Gong to ask him, "Do you know the way of medicine?" Lei Gong replied, "I can recite and somewhat understand, understand but cannot distinguish, distinguish but cannot clarify, clarify but cannot reveal. This is sufficient to treat advisors, but insufficient for lords and kings. I wish to receive the heavenly measure, to align with the four seasons and the interplay of yin and yang, to differentiate the stars and the light of the sun and moon, to clarify the teachings, so that future generations may understand them more fully." He connected with the teachings of Shen Nong and sought to convey this profound education, casting doubts on the two emperors. The Emperor said, "Good. Don’t lose this understanding. All of this represents the interplay of yin and yang, the inner and outer principles, the complement of male and female, mutually responsive. Thus, those who understand the heavens above and the geography below, and the concerns of humanity in the midst can sustain this knowledge and teach the populace, without fear of danger. The teachings of medicine can be passed down for future generations and can become a treasure." Lei Gong said, "Please allow me to receive this path, to recite and understand!" The Emperor replied, "If you were to receive the transmission without understanding the true way, you would confuse the teachings. I will convey to you the essence of the true path, addressing ailments of the five organs and the consumption of muscles and bones. Your lack of clarity and discrimination means that the scholars of the world have exhausted their learning."[16p]
Discussion on Composure

The Yellow Emperor was seated, and he summoned Lord Lei to ask him: "You, who have received teachings and recited texts, if you can survey and understand various studies, and classify and integrate principles to enlighten me, what then do you have to say about the internal organs—the heart, liver, spleen, lungs, and kidneys; the gallbladder and intestines; the brain, marrow, tears, and saliva; the crying and sorrow, and the source of water's flow—these are all part of human existence, governing their ailments and mistakes. You should strive to clarify these matters; this knowledge could lead to completeness. If you cannot, then you will be resented by the world."

Lord Lei replied: "I will recite the numerous chapters of the 'Pulse Classic,' both upper and lower. There are different categories; yet I still cannot achieve completeness, so how can I clarify?"

The Emperor said: "What you are capable of treating, your knowledge is already extensive, even if it is flawed. It is akin to a swan flying, also soaring into the sky. The way of sages in treating illness follows the laws and measures, makes comparisons and classifications, transforming what is obscure, following from the top to the bottom; must one necessarily adhere to the texts?"[17p]
On the Five Excesses

The Yellow Emperor said: "Ah, it is far! It is like gazing into a deep abyss, or welcoming floating clouds. While the deep abyss can still be measured, the floating clouds have no known boundaries. The sage’s way serves as a standard for all people. When discussing intents and aspirations, there must be principles. Following the texts and adhering to the numbers, in accordance with medical matters, guides the people well. There are five excesses and four virtues, do you know of them? The Thunder Lord, bowing twice, said: 'I am young and inexperienced, foolish and confused, not aware of the five excesses and four virtues, their classifications and names, drawing only on vague texts, and my heart has no response.' The Emperor said: 'For everyone who has not diagnosed the illness, one must first ask whether one has gone from noble to humble; even if not possessed by evil, the illness arises from within, termed as loss of essence. When the five energies linger, illnesses may converge; the physician diagnoses it, but it is not in the organs, nor does it change the bodily form; if doubt arises, the name of the illness is unknown.'"[18p]
The body daily weakens, the vital energy is depleted and lacks essence, deep-rooted illnesses leave one breathless, leading to occasional fright. In deep sickness, one loses externally from defense and internally from nourishment. A skilled practitioner might misjudge without understanding the illness. This is also a mistake in treatment.

Anyone wishing to diagnose an illness must inquire about diet, living conditions, sudden pleasure, and sudden sorrow; joyous beginnings followed by sorrow harm the vital energy. When essence is exhausted, the body is ruined. Sudden anger injures Yin, sudden joy injures Yang. When the vital energy rises, the meridians lose their form. A foolish doctor treats without discerning whether to tonify or drain, oblivious to the condition of the illness; essence and vitality are daily lost, while pathogenic energies intertwine. This is a second mistake in treatment.

A skilled pulse reader must compare, categorize, and understand steadily. A practitioner who doesn’t know yet wishes to excel is not worthy of respect in diagnosis. This is a third mistake in treatment.

Diagnosis has three constants, requiring inquiry into wealth and poverty, noble injuries, and the desires of kings and lords.[19p]
Therefore, valuing the release from power, though one is not involved in evil, if the spirit is internally wounded, the body must perish. Being wealthy initially and then becoming poor, though not due to evil, leads to skin burning and sinews bending, resulting in weakness and contractions. If a physician cannot be strict and cannot engage the spirit, being externally weak and disordered will lead to the loss of normalcy; if the condition cannot change, then medical affairs will not succeed. This is one of the four missteps in treatment.

In all diagnoses, one must know the beginning and the end. Understanding the remaining traces, taking pulses and asking names, one must align with male and female. Separation and blockage, worry, fear, joy, and anger lead to the emptiness of the five organs and the departure of blood and qi. If the skilled cannot understand, then what technique can they speak of? Experiences of wealth that lead to great injuries cut sinews and sever pulses; if the body recovers, it causes unremitting harm, leading to the failure of vital connections, leaving insufficient return of yang, causing accumulation of pus and cold. If a novice treats it, hastily needling yin and yang, the body will disintegrate, limbs will cramp, and death will be certain. If the physician cannot be clear, does not inquire about the cause, and only speaks of the day of death, this is also a sign of a novice. This is the fifth misstep in treatment.[20p]
All five of these are due to a lack of understanding of the techniques and the matters of humanity. Therefore, it is said: When a sage treats illness, they must comprehend the principles of heaven and earth, the yin and yang, the seasonal changes, the functions of the five organs and six intestines, as well as the relationship between male and female. They must know the methods of needling, moxibustion, stones, and the principles of poisons. They should approach human affairs calmly, to clarify the canonical way. Rich and poor, high and low, each has its own patterns and principles. By inquiring into the reasons for youth and age, courage and fear, one must examine the divisions to understand the origins of illness. In discerning the eight regular and nine irregular conditions, the diagnosis must be accurate.

The way to treat illness considers inner energy as a treasure. By seeking its principles, if one cannot find them, the problem lies in the external and internal. By adhering to data in treatment, one should not deviate from the principles of healing. Those who can apply this technique throughout their lives will remain safe. If one does not understand the principles, the five organs may become heated, leading to sores in the six intestines; misdiagnosing the illness indicates a failure to maintain normalcy. Careful adherence to this method, in conjunction with the classics, aligns upper and lower texts and assesses yin and yang. By discerning the exceptional and the constant within the five elements, one can make decisions in a clear manner. Observing beginnings and endings allows for the ability to navigate freely.[21p]
Discussion on the Four Losses

The Yellow Emperor was in the Bright Hall, and Thunder Lord was in attendance. The Yellow Emperor said: "The teachings you have received are numerous, please speak about the meanings of gains and losses. What leads to gain? What leads to loss?" Thunder Lord replied: "Following the classics and receiving teachings, all speak of complete fulfillment. If there are mistakes at that time, I would like to hear their explanations." The Emperor said: "You are young, and your wisdom may not yet be sufficient! Are you going to mix things together? The twelve primary channels and the three hundred sixty-five collateral channels are all well known and practiced by skilled practitioners. The reasons for not achieving completeness are due to undivided spirit, unregulated will, and discrepancies between the external and internal, thus leading to doubts and dangers at times. Not understanding the principles of yin and yang, the reversals and followings, is one mistake in treatment. Not completing the training under the teacher and recklessly practicing mixed techniques, misleading others with false statements, changing names for personal gain, misusing filiform needles ultimately brings personal blame. This is the second mistake in treatment."[22p]
It is inappropriate to be concerned about the dwelling of the rich or poor, the thickness or thinness of seating, the warmth or coldness of form, and to be indifferent to the suitability of food and drink, the bravery or cowardice of others, and not to recognize comparison and classification, which leads to confusion and lacks clarity. This reflects three failures in practice.

In diagnosing ailments, one should not neglect to inquire about their origins, the troubles arising from failures in diet, and the excesses in daily living, which may result in harm from toxins. If one does not address these issues first, but merely takes the pulse, how can any disease be correctly identified? Speaking recklessly and being constrained by trivial concerns also represents a failure in practice.

Thus, when people in the world speak, they may leap thousands of miles away, lacking clarity in discussion and failing to diagnose human matters correctly. The principles of healing rely on calmness and preservation.

Holding the pulse, if one does not discern the five pulses, the onset of various diseases will be blamed on self-reproach, leaving the teacher to bear the blame. Therefore, practice cannot adhere to reason and abandons techniques in the marketplace. Treating conditions whimsically may sometimes bring relief, but it is born out of foolishness. Alas! In the depths of obscurity, who knows the path! The great way aligns with heaven and earth, corresponding to the four seas. You do not understand the way's directives, calling clarity obscure.[23p]
On the Rise and Fall

The Emperor said: Diagnosis has ten degrees, including measuring the pulse, assessing the organs, evaluating the flesh, examining the tendons, and discerning the points. When the essence of yin and yang is exhausted, illness manifests naturally. The pulse is irregular, and yin disperses while yang prevails. When the pulse is faint and lacking, the diagnosis is inconsistent. Diagnosis must encompass both the upper and lower states, considering the people, the ruler, and the ministers. If one does not receive teaching completely, one’s skills remain unclear. Failing to discern what is contrary or in agreement leads to erroneous actions. Holding onto the feminine while losing the masculine, favoring yin over yang without understanding their union, results in unclear diagnoses. The teachings must be transmitted to future generations, reflecting upon themselves. When yin is extremely deficient, the heavenly qi is severed; when yang is overly abundant, the earthly qi is insufficient.

The synchrony of yin and yang is where the wise person acts. In this synchrony, yang qi arrives first, followed by yin qi. Therefore, the sage upholds the way of diagnosis by prioritizing the sequence of yin and yang. The dynamic of the extraordinary and the ordinary culminates in sixty beginnings. The subtle matters of diagnosis align with the changes in yin and yang, reflecting the emotions contained within five principles, examining the essence of emptiness and fullness, and establishing the required degrees—this is sufficient for diagnosis. Thus, if one cannot palpate the yin, the yang diagnosis will wither away.[24p]
Without the sun, there is no shadow; to maintain learning without depth leads to confusion. One may know the left but not the right, or know the right but not the left; one may know the top but not the bottom, or know the beginning but not the end—therefore, governance does not last long. One knows the ugly and the good, knows sickness and health, knows high and low, knows sitting and standing, knows action and stillness; using these with regulation, one grasps the Way completely, thus avoiding peril for eternity.

One arises with reserves and knows one’s deficiencies. In addressing matters, one understands the upper and lower, the pulse and its conditions; hence, if the body is weak and energy is deficient, death follows. If the body has excess but energy is lacking, life ceases.

Thus, diagnosis has standard principles, sitting and standing have their constants. There are established ways of entering and exiting, focusing the spirit keenly. One must be clear and tranquil, observing above and below, managing the eight right and wrongs, distinguishing the five central categories, feeling the pulse’s movement and stillness, following the fluctuations of cold and heat, considering its size, matching it with possible ailments, discerning whether to go against or follow, and then further identifying the names of maladies—all this makes diagnosis complete and avoids losing a sense of humanity.

Thus, in diagnosis, one observes breath and intention without losing coherence. The Way is clear and discerning, allowing longevity. Not understanding this Way leads to losing the foundation and severing logic. Speaking recklessly and setting baseless expectations is known as losing the Way.[25p]
The Yellow Emperor was in the Ming Hall when Lei Gong asked, "I have received teachings about applying the principles from classic texts, instructing on the theories of yin and yang, acupuncture, moxibustion, and medicinal substances. In practice, there are the wise and the unwise, and one cannot always achieve perfection. If one first speaks of sorrow, joy, anger, and fear, of dryness, dampness, cold, and heat, as well as the distinctions of yin and yang among men and women, I seek to understand the underlying reasons for these matters. Regarding the statuses of lowly or wealthy, the forms of people, and their relationships with the world, one must carefully heed the commands. I have humble and foolish inquiries not covered in the classics, and I wish to know their nature." The Emperor replied, "This is profound!"

The Classic of the Spiritual Axis

On the Pathologies of Evil Qi in the Organs

The Yellow Emperor asked Qi Bo, "I have heard that by observing color, one can know a disease, which is called ‘ming’ (brightness); by feeling the pulse, one knows the disease, which is called ‘shen’ (spirit); by inquiring about the illness, one knows its location, which is termed ‘gong’ (work). I wish to learn how to see it, understand it, feel it, and inquire thoroughly about it. What should I do?" Qi Bo responded, "Color and pulse correspond to each other as do the resonance of a drum and a stick; they cannot be separated. This also pertains to the root and leaves: when the root dies, the leaves wither. The relationship between color, pulse, and flesh cannot be separated. Therefore, understanding one indicates mastery of work (gong), understanding two indicates spirit (shen), and understanding three indicates both spirit and brightness. Thus, those adept in feeling the pulse do not rely on color; those skilled in assessing color do not depend on the pulse. Those who can integrate and apply these can be considered top practitioners, achieving ten out of ten nine times. Those who apply two qualify as intermediate practitioners, achieving ten out of ten seven times. Those who apply one qualify as lower practitioners, achieving ten out of ten six times."[26p]
Root and Junction

The essence of using acupuncture lies in understanding the balance of yin and yang. When yin and yang are harmonized, the vital energy can shine forth. Integrating spirit and energy allows the spirit to be contained within. Therefore, it is said that the skilled practitioner regulates the energy, the intermediate practitioner causes disharmony in the pulse, and the unskilled practitioner brings danger to life. Thus, it is stated that the unskilled practitioner must proceed with caution. One must carefully examine the changes in the five organs’ illnesses, the responses of the five pulses, the realities and insufficiencies of the meridians, and the softness and brittleness of the skin before taking action.

Official Acupuncture

Those who practice acupuncture must understand the effects of age, the fluctuations of qi, and the origins of deficiency and excess, or they cannot be considered skilled practitioners.

Reversal and Order of Thickness and Thinness

Qi Bo said: The way of the sage corresponds above with heaven, below with earth, and in the middle with human affairs. There must be clear principles to formulate measures. The methods of regulation and precision can later be transmitted. Thus, a craftsman cannot abandon the measurements and mistakenly envision lengths or depths, nor ignore the tools and create level waters. A worker cannot ignore the compass to create a circle, nor discard the square to create a rectangle. Understanding and applying these principles is crucial for natural phenomena and the normalcy of reversal and order.[27p]
On Harmonizing Qi across the Four Seasons

The Yellow Emperor said: If there are variations in the times, what could be the reason? Qi Bo replied: It is due to the failure to harmonize with the seasonal qi; when an organ is solely affected by disease, it must be due to the qi of that organ being weak against the seasonal influence, while its stronger seasonal counterparts arise. The Yellow Emperor asked: How should one treat this? Qi Bo replied: By aligning with the timing of nature, one can effectively address the illness. To be in harmony is to be skilled; to act against it is to be crude.

On Deviant Winds

The Yellow Emperor said: What is the reason for those who simply chant a blessing? Qi Bo replied: The ancient shamans understood the victories of various ailments and were aware of the origins of those illnesses, thus they could merely chant blessings.

On the Twelve Difficulties

The scriptures state: When the pulses of the five organs are exhausted internally, needling should be directed externally; when the pulses of the five organs are exhausted externally, needling should turn inward. How does one distinguish between the internal and external failures? Indeed, when the pulses in the five organs are exhausted internally, it also signifies the exhaustion of the kidney and liver pulses; yet, the physician mistakenly reinforces the heart and lungs. Conversely, when the pulses of the five organs are exhausted externally, the heart and lung pulses are similarly depleted; however, the physician wrongly reinforces the kidneys and liver. Complementing yang when yin is depleted, and complementing yin when yang is depleted—this is termed reality and illusion, diminishing the insufficient and augmenting the excessive. In such cases, the physician is essentially responsible for the patient’s demise.[28p]
Thirteen Difficulties

The classic text states that when one sees the complexion but cannot detect the pulse, if one finds a pulse that is victorious over another, it signifies death; if one finds a pulse that nurtures another, the illness will resolve itself. The colors and pulses should correspond; but how should this be done? Indeed. Each of the five organs has five colors, which can all be observed on the face, and they should also correspond with the pulse at the wrist. For example, if the color is blue, the pulse should be taut and quick; if red, the pulse should be floating, large, and scattered; if yellow, the pulse should be moderate and large; if white, the pulse should be floating, thin, and short; if black, the pulse should be deep, moist, and slippery. This illustrates how the five colors correspond with the five pulses. If the pulse is rapid, then the skin at the wrist is also rapid; if the pulse is quick, the skin is also quick; if the pulse is slow, the skin is also slow; if the pulse is thin, the skin is also thin; if the pulse is slippery, the skin is also slippery. Each organ has its own sound, color, smell, and taste, which should correspond with the pulse at the wrist; failure to correspond indicates illness. For instance, if the color is blue, and the pulse is floating, thin, and short, it would indicate a victory if it is large and slow; floating and large, and small and slippery would indicate nurturing. The classic states that knowing one signifies being a lesser practitioner, knowing two represents a mid-level practitioner, and knowing three signifies a superior practitioner. The superior practitioner achieves perfection nine times out of ten, the mid-level practitioner achieves eight out of ten, and the lesser practitioner achieves six out of ten. This is what it refers to.[29p]
The 61st Difficulty

The classics state that to know by observation is called divine, to know by hearing is called sage, to know by inquiry is called skillful, and to know by pulse examination is called artful. What does this mean? Indeed. To know by observation means to see the five colors to understand the illness; to know by hearing means to listen to the five sounds to differentiate the sickness; to know by inquiry means to ask about their desired five flavors to understand the source of the illness; to know by pulse examination means to diagnose by checking the wrist, looking at its fullness and emptiness to understand which organ the illness resides in. Knowledge derived from external means is called sage, while knowledge derived from internal means is called divine. This is what is meant.[30p]

Chapter 77

The classics say, "The superior healer addresses the disease before it manifests; the intermediate healer treats the disease once it appears." What does this mean? Indeed, treating a disease before it manifests refers to recognizing the illness of the liver, which is known to affect the spleen. Thus, one first strengthens the spleen's qi to prevent the invasion of the liver’s pathogenic influence, hence it is said to treat before the disease. The intermediate healer, however, sees the liver illness and focuses solely on the liver without understanding the connections, thus it is said to treat the disease after it manifests.

Chapter 81

The classics say, "There is both reality and illusion; one drains what is insufficient while benefiting what is excessive—this is the pulse at the wrist. Is the nature of the disease related to these realities and illusions? How should one balance the depletion and the excess?" Indeed, it is not the pulse at the wrist that signifies illness; it indicates that the disease itself has realities and illusions. For instance, if the liver is full and the lung is deficient, where the liver represents wood and the lung represents metal, wood and metal should balance each other. One must know to balance metal with wood. Conversely, if the lung is full and the liver is deficient, and there’s little qi, one should not supplement the liver with needles, but rather focus on reducing the excessive lung condition. Thus, it is said there is reality and illusion; depleting what is insufficient while benefiting what is excessive. This is detrimental to the intermediate healer.

Source: Jin Kui Yao Lue by Zhang Ji (Han Dynasty)

On the order of diseases related to viscera, bowels, meridians, and pulse diagnostics.[31p]
Question: What does it mean to treat diseases before they occur?

Master: To treat diseases before they occur means to recognize the illness of the liver and understand how it affects the spleen; therefore, one should first strengthen the spleen. If the spleen, the ruler of the four seasons, does not suffer from evil influences, then it should not be supplemented. An intermediate practitioner, lacking this understanding, will see liver illness but will only treat the liver without resolving the spleen.

Path to Eliminate Illness:

The method to eliminate illness focuses on observing symptoms, inquiring about preferences, examining the causes of disease, and considering the common ailments of the time; thus, one can trace the course of an illness from beginning to end. Once the disease is understood, treat external diseases internally and address upper issues to rescue lower issues. Differentiate between the deficiency and excess of organ diseases, recognize the relationships between mother and child organs, assess their strengths and weaknesses, and tailor the prescriptions accordingly, leading to a superior practitioner’s expertise. The best prescriptions use single flavors, two flavors come second, and multiple flavors rank lowest. Sour flavors enter the bones, sweet flavors resolve toxins, bitter flavors eliminate heat, salty flavors guide downward, and spicy flavors disperse stagnation. One should test the medicine; if it has not been tested, be cautious about hasty use. Once the major condition has passed, residual symptoms should not be treated again with medicine. Those who are well-nourished should receive ample doses, while the frail and weak should have reduced doses.

Using medicine is like waging war, and utilizing a physician is like employing a general. A good military leader may not rely solely on war chariots; a proficient herbalist shows the effectiveness of ginger and cassia. Knowing a person's abilities to assign them to a task represents the way of employing generals; knowing the techniques to prescribe denotes the way of employing physicians. In the world, there are no difficult-to-treat diseases, only poorly skilled physicians; there are no irreplaceable medicines, only inadequately skilled individuals who misprescribe. Among the common people, certain deaths can be recognized.[32p]
Examination of the Subtle

Illness can mistakenly be perceived as coolness and then result in true coldness; there can also be phenomena that appear to be true but are actually false. A small deviation can lead to significant loss of life. The "Floating Chest Sutra" in the chapter on the two energies states: All diarrhea is due to heat, and all coolness relates to regulation. Heat first affects the inner organs, while cold first influences the blood's warmth. In the chapter on abdominal ailments, it states: Dry pain should at times be considered as caused by worms, and residual stabbing pain often leads to swelling. The chapter on cold damage indicates: Wind patterns and epidemics, damp heat and lingering phlegm, cause malaria and rashes, all similar to cold damage. When malaria is common, one should take precautions against it; when rashes are common, one should be wary of catching them. Spring plagues and summer epidemics first reveal internal symptoms. For dampness and heat, it is advisable to test with certain herbs like Lingzhi and Atractylodes. When administering dispersing agents, both vomiting and sweating must occur; such symptoms indicate lingering phlegm and, if not guided properly, may lead to limb dysfunction. Alas! Some illnesses are subtle yet deadly, while conditions that seem severe are easily treated. The precision of subtle differentiation is the hallmark of great practitioners in the world![33p]
Distinguishing Books

Yin Yancheng asked: In today's world, is there a way to engage with the works of ancient masters? The response was: A good teacher and friend, a skilled physician, learn through words and recognize changes; they review old texts, using nets to seek fish. With extensive knowledge of diseases, multiple diagnoses, and repeated use of effective medicines, what shame is there in being compared to ancient people!

Discussion on Medicine

A physician cannot be entrusted to one who is not benevolent, wise, and discerning. They must also be honest and genuinely good. Therefore, in ancient times, those who practiced medicine were carefully chosen from well-known families, possessing virtue and the ability to be compassionate and loving. They had to be intelligent and articulate, capable of understanding the celestial and spiritual realms, discerning the factors of life and death, distinguishing between vacuity and actuality, determining the rules of oppositions and agreements, evaluating the severity of ailments, and adjusting medicine's dosage accordingly, paying attention to the subtle and the profound, without neglecting the minute details. Thus, they are called good doctors.

Furthermore, Daoists value coolness, using plants that grow in cold; meanwhile, doctors advocate warmth, facilitating the flow of blood and channels. It is not enough to grasp the general principles; one must understand the finer details, including the balance of hardness and softness, the nuances of seasonal changes, and the regulations of intake and excretion. Renowned physicians examine the pulse at the three positions of the wrist, which reveals the truth. They measure the seasonal changes and assess warmth and cold, observing the pulse dynamics and aligning weight with lightness, proportioning medicine and minerals accordingly. This can be called a renowned physician. There are those who are well-known yet ineffective, and others who remain obscure yet are proficient. Those in authority who employ physicians must thoroughly evaluate and discern their qualifications.[34p]
The Book of Sui

The Treatise on Classics

Medical prescriptions are techniques used to remove illnesses and preserve life. The heavens have the dualities of yin and yang, the elements of wind and rain, and cycles of light and darkness; humans experience emotions such as joy, anger, sorrow, love, and hatred. By regulating these emotions, one achieves harmony and balance, whereas indulging exclusively leads to deterioration and illness. Thus, the sage understands the essence of blood vessels, using needles and stones, employing medicinal substances, regulating the internal balance, nurturing energy, unblocking stagnation, and alleviating constrictions, thus returning to a state of simplicity. Those skilled in this can understand governance through pulse diagnosis and relate illnesses to the state. The physician's role, as detailed in the Zhou rituals, involves gathering various medicines to treat all who are ill; this is their duty. If the unworthy engage in this, they undermine the fundamental nature. Therefore, it is said that those with illnesses who do not seek treatment will invariably rely on traditional medicine.[35p]
On the Practice of Great Physicians

If one wishes to become a great physician, one must be well-versed in the "Su Wen," "Jia Yi," "Huang Di's Canon of Acupuncture," "Mingtang Liu Zhu," the twelve meridians, the three departments and nine conditions, the five organs and six bowels, the exterior and interior points, the herbal remedies of "Ben Cao," and the classical formulas of Zhang Zhongjing, Wang Shuhe, Yuan Henan, Fan Dongyang, Zhang Miao, Jin Shao, and others. Moreover, one must deeply understand the theories of yin and yang, the principles of fate, the observations of the tortoise's five signs, the "I Ching," and the art of the six ren. Mastery of these is essential to becoming a great physician.

Without such understanding, it would be akin to wandering blind at night, leading to danger and demise. Next, one should thoroughly read these formulas, contemplate their profound meanings, and dedicate oneself to research; only then can one engage in the medical field. Additionally, one must explore various books. Why, you ask? If one does not read the Five Classics, one will not know the principles of benevolence and righteousness; if one does not read the Three Histories, one will not know the affairs of past and present; if one does not read the various schools of thought, one will not be able to silently recognize them; if one does not read the "Inner Canon," one will not know the virtues of compassion, joy, and charity; if one does not study Zhuangzi and Laozi, one cannot adequately navigate the universal principles, thus becoming hindered by fortune and misfortune. As for the Five Elements and the Seven Luminaries in astronomy, one must also engage with these; if one can comprehensively study them, then in the medical field, one will experience no obstacles and attain excellence.[36p]
On the Great Physician's Precision and Sincerity

Zhang Zhan said: The difficulty in mastering the classic formulas has existed for a long time. Today, diseases can have similar internal characteristics but different external manifestations, and vice versa. Therefore, the fullness and emptiness of the five organs and six bowels, as well as the circulation and obstruction of blood and qi, cannot be discerned merely by sight and hearing; they must first be diagnosed and examined. The pulse at the inch and the cun can show various irregularities such as floating, sinking, tightness, and slackness. The acupoints can have differences in elevation and depth, while the skin, muscles, and bones may vary in thickness and softness. Only those who are meticulous and discerning can truly understand these matters.

To seek the most refined and subtle knowledge through coarse and superficial thinking is perilous! If one adds to what is already full, depletes what is empty, aggravates what is open, and obstructs what is blocked, if one cools what is cold and warms what is hot, this only exacerbates the illness while hoping for recovery; I have seen such patients perish. Therefore, the art of medicine and divination is challenging to perfect. If it were not divinely inspired, how could one grasp the profound and subtle? In this world, there are fools who, after studying formulas for three years, claim there are no diseases that can be treated, and after three years of treating diseases, come to realize that there are no formulas that can be universally applied.[37p]
Therefore, scholars must extensively explore the sources of medicine, diligently and tirelessly, avoiding hearsay while claiming to have grasped the medical path. This is truly lamentable! Any great physician treating illness must first calm the mind and settle the will, free from desires and aspirations. They should cultivate profound compassion and a heart of pity, vowing to universally relieve the suffering of all sentient beings. When someone in distress comes seeking help, they must not inquire about wealth or poverty, status or age, beauty or ugliness, familial ties or friendships, whether they are of the nobility or the common folk; they should regard everyone as equals, akin to one's closest kin. Furthermore, they must not look forward or backward, worrying about fortune and misfortune, nor should they be concerned about their own safety. Witnessing others in pain, one should feel as if it were their own suffering, experiencing deep sorrow: One must not avoid hardship, whether day or night, through cold and heat, hunger and thirst, or fatigue, but must wholeheartedly rush to assist, without the intention of seeking recognition or reputation. In this way, one can truly be a great physician for the people; if one deviates from this, they are akin to a great thief of sentience. Throughout history, esteemed figures have treated illnesses, often risking their lives to aid those in dire straits. Although one may claim to value human life over that of animals, in the face of danger, life is ultimately one and the same. To benefit oneself at the expense of others is a shared suffering of all beings; how much more should this apply to humans? Killing for the sake of survival only distances one further from life. The reason why I do not use life as medicine today is precisely because of this understanding.[38p]
Among the creatures like leeches and pests, if there are those that have died beforehand available in the market, they may be used, as this does not violate the principle. For instance, in the case of chicken eggs, since they are still not fully formed, there is undoubtedly a critical need, and one may reluctantly use them; those who can refrain from using them are truly wise, a level beyond reach. When faced with ailments such as sores or dysentery, which are foul and unbearable to look at—things that are detestable to others—the reaction should be shame and compassion, without the slightest thought of personal gain; this is my aspiration.

For a great physician, one must achieve a tranquil mind, able to introspect, and look upon the situation with a serene demeanor, expansive and free, not shining bright nor drearily dim, thoroughly inspecting symptoms and illnesses, with deep intention and careful observation, missing no detail. In administering treatment, one must not be haphazard. While it is said that ailments should be quickly addressed, one must not be confused in the process. It is essential to examine the situation carefully and reflect deeply, without hastily seeking reputation at the expense of life, for that would be lacking in benevolence. Additionally, when visiting a patient’s home, regardless of the lavish decor one should not look around indiscriminately; even if musical instruments play nearby, one must not appear entertained; even if delicacies are offered, the food should taste bland; fine wines served should seem as if they were nonexistent.[39p]
Thus it is, when one person is in distress, the whole place is filled with sorrow. How much more so is the pain of the sick, who cannot be separated from their suffering, while a physician remains carefree and proud, indulging in joy. This is a shared shame among humans and gods, something that the truly wise would never do—this is the essence of the healer's intention.

As for the practice of medicine, one must not speak too much, jest, chatter, argue about right and wrong, discuss people, flaunt one’s fame, disparage other doctors, or boast of one's own virtue. If by chance one cures a disease, one should not hold one’s head high with an air of superiority, believing oneself to be unparalleled. This is the foolishness of physicians.

Laozi said: "Those who act with virtue in the open are rewarded by heaven; those who act with virtue in secret are harmed by spirits." Following these two paths, the responses of yin and yang are surely not misleading. Therefore, a physician must not rely on their own strengths or focus solely on material gain, but instead cultivate a heart for alleviating suffering, and in the depths of their path, they will naturally feel abundant blessings.

Moreover, one should not treat the wealthy with rare and precious medicines, making it difficult for the poor to obtain them. Flaunting one’s skills is certainly not the way of loyalty and compassion. The intention to provide relief should be at the forefront, hence one must be flexible and considerate in discussions. Scholars should not be ashamed to express humble words![40p]

Discussion on Treatment of Illness: Brief Example

Heaven distributes the five elements to sustain all beings; humanity embodies the five constants to form the five organs. The channels and collaterals, yin and yang, interconnect; the original, the obscure, and the subtle are ever-changing and difficult to grasp. The Book of Changes states: "If it is not the utmost mystery of the world, who can engage with it?" Observing today's physicians, they do not contemplate and seek the essence of the classics to expand their understanding. Rather, each follows their traditional skills, clinging to old methods from beginning to end. When diagnosing illnesses or asking about symptoms, they focus on verbal responses, relying only on brief interactions. They apply remedies without thorough examination, taking superficial measurements that do not capture the whole condition; they check the pulse without understanding its depth, dismiss the body's currents, and disregard important signs. Often, they do not reach fifty proper assessments, and are unaware of crucial diagnoses, lacking a comprehensive view, merely peering through a tube. The challenge of distinguishing life from death is indeed difficult. Should not patients be vigilant about recognizing these shortcomings in their physicians to guard against negligence? Throughout history, physicians have harbored jealousy and malice towards one another; the case of Bian Que, who was harmed by Li Shizhen, the chief physician of the Qin dynasty, exemplifies this. When one physician prescribes, they must not allow another physician to collaborate improperly, risking the addition of harmful substances that would exacerbate the illness, leading to suffering. Such instances are not uncommon; hence, caution is essential.[41p]
It is better to not take medicine and accept the natural order than to have foolish doctors compete, endangering lives—this is indeed tragic. Diagnosing patients is not easy; knowing the conditions through inquiry distinguishes the skilled doctors. Zhang Zhongjing said: "Anyone wishing to harmonize decoctions, herbs, and acupuncture techniques must engage in deep thought. One must understand the twelve meridians, identify the three hundred sixty-five acupoints, recognize the flow of vital energy, and discern the location of diseases—the methods of treatment must be understood." In ancient times, the highest physicians observed colors. The colors, pulses, and forms must not be disconnected. When black dominates red, it leads to death; when red dominates blue, it leads to life. Chinese medicine considers sounds, correlating them to the five tones. Fire perceives water’s sound, leading to anxiety and fright; wood perceives metal’s sound, leading to fear of punishment. The spleen represents earth; it nurtures all things, supports all around. In favorable conditions, it is unseen; in death, it reverts to it. Excessive activation leads to immobility of limbs, while deficiency leads to blocked orifices and closed senses, akin to a person inebriated. The four seasons circulate, ending and beginning anew. Lower physicians diagnose pulses and understand disease origins, observing the flows and shifts through the seasons, knowing their interrelations—this is the essence of mastery.[42p]
On Diagnosing Illness

In ancient times, those skilled in medicine were categorized as follows: the highest physicians treat the state, the middle physicians treat the people, and the lowest physicians treat diseases. Furthermore, it is said that the highest physicians listen to sounds, the middle physicians observe colors, and the lowest physicians diagnose pulses. Additionally, the highest physicians treat diseases that have yet to emerge, the middle physicians treat diseases that are desired to emerge, and the lowest physicians treat diseases that are already present. If one does not carefully apply themselves and allow confusion to arise, it becomes difficult to save the patient.

Foolish physicians do not understand the three divisions, nine pulses, and the principles of the four seasons. They may incorrectly use decoctions or medicines and misapply acupuncture, leading to more ailments, resulting in their demise. Alas, the masses suffer, and half die unjustly; it can be said that there are no good physicians in the world. These practitioners fail to explain the classics regarding the interplay of earth, water, fire, and wind, which together create human life. However, foolish physicians do not consider the pulse pathways and incorrectly treat ailments, causing the internal organs and their five elements to conflict with one another, like pouring oil on a raging fire—this cannot be taken lightly.

In all matters, the four Qi must harmonize, and the four spirits must be at peace. If one Qi is out of balance, a hundred diseases arise; if the four spirits are disturbed, four hundred and four diseases emerge simultaneously. It is also said: one hundred diseases may heal on their own without treatment; one hundred diseases require treatment to heal; one hundred diseases may be difficult to cure even with treatment; one hundred diseases will lead to true death if untreated.[43p]
Notes from Dongpo by Su Shi (Song Dynasty)

Regarding seeking medical treatment and pulse diagnosis

The difficulty in reading pulses has been a longstanding issue through the ages. When the pulse is thin yet has substantial indicators, and when it shows strong signs but is frail, even a slight difference can make the distinction between life and death, fortune and misfortune. This has been a problem throughout history. One cannot avoid consulting a physician, yet truly skilled practitioners are very few. Exceptional horses are rare indeed, as the world has never seen one that did not need to be ridden; likewise, outstanding physicians do not appear often, and patients do not die in vain, as it is due to their strengths that they guard against their weaknesses. Many local doctors hold secret knowledge of the ailments and seek consultations to test the abilities of the physicians, thereby making the diagnosis a wandering search through uncertainty, distinguishing the real from the false amidst suspicion. If the physician unfortunately fails, they still do not acknowledge their failure, resorting to clever embellishments to preserve their reputation; yet, if no recovery is achieved, they say it was indeed difficult to treat. Occasionally, some are diligent and wish to improve, but even if they refer to the words of the patient, they still mix in their own observations, balancing both, leading to ineffective remedies; this is a universal problem of our time that remains unrecognized. Throughout my life, when I sought medical help, I generally observed their skill or lack thereof during normal times. When I did fall ill and sought treatment, I would first fully disclose my ailments, then request a diagnosis. By letting the physician clearly understand the source of the problem first, the matters of deficiency and excess, heat and cold, would be determined beforehand, hence the doubts of the pulse could not confuse. Therefore, even with traditional medicine, my ailments always improved. My only goal was to recover from my illness; I did not seek to trouble the physician.[44p]
The physician prescribes medicine based on intention.

Ouyang Wenzhong once remarked that a patient, when asked about the cause of his illness, said: "I was on a boat and encountered strong winds, which startled me and led to my condition." The physician then used a long-standing remedy: he took a tooth from a long-standing tree, cut it where it was moist with sweat from the boatman’s hands, mixed it with cinnabar and substances like Poria, and the patient recovered after drinking it. Now, according to the "Pharmaceutical Notes," citing the "Discussion on Medicinal Properties," it states that to stop sweating, one should take the root of Ephedra and grind it with ancient bamboo leaves to consume. Wenzhong noted that physicians often rely heavily on cooking and using medicines in this manner. Initially, it seemed trivial like child’s play; however, it sometimes proved effective, though it is difficult to challenge that assertion. I then said: "If you nourish scholars with ink and burnt ash, should they not be cured of laziness? Broadening this perspective, one could say that drinking the washing water of Boyi can cure greed, eating the remnants of Bigan's dish can end flattery, licking the shield of Fan Kuai can heal cowardice, and touching the earrings of Xi Shi can alleviate wickedness." He then laughed heartily. On the seventeenth day of the eighth month in the third year of the Yuanyou era, while sailing into the boundary of Yingzhou, I sat and recalled twenty years ago when I saw Wenzhong here, and casually recorded this moment of conversation and laughter.[45p]
Record of Escaping the Heat
(Song Dynasty, Ye Mengde)

The Scholar-Official Should Not Trust Physicians Lightly

In matters of the world, if the scholar-official is smart and self-assured, there is nothing they cannot accomplish, yet they should not be overly reliant on physicians. In our dynasty, among the high-ranking officials, only Gao Wenzhuang can be considered adept in medicine, particularly in treating fevers. The knowledge he possesses is unknown to us. However, individuals like Sun Zhao and Du Ren have gained some recognition, which is sufficient to mark them in history. Wenzhuang is from Yungzhou, and to this day, Yung has many physicians, especially skilled in treating fevers, all stemming from the Gao lineage. During the Chongning and Daguan eras, while I was in the capital, I encountered Dong Ji, Liu Yin, and others who were well-versed in Zhang Zhongjing’s medical arts; their skills were unmatched by the conventional practitioners since the Jianghuai region. Zi Zhan, residing in Huangzhou, noted that the physician Pang Anchang from Qizhou was also adept in treating fevers and had grasped the essence of Zhongjing’s methods.[46p]
In Shu, there was a figure named Chao Gu who developed a medicinal powder called Sheng San Zi Fang, claiming to have learned it from a mysterious person, without prior mention in the medical texts of past eras. He asserted that it could treat any case of typhus, regardless of symptoms, and it was said to cure all ailments. Zizhan was intrigued by this and wrote a preface, comparing it to Sun Simiao’s San Jiansan, though he did not dare to criticize it too harshly. He included it in his work "Shanghan Lun," and it gained widespread acceptance throughout the land.

However, the treatment was extremely precise; even the slightest error could lead to death in cases of typhus. If the medicine was misused, it would result in many deaths. How could one use such a remedy without regard for symptoms? After the Xuán Hé era, this medicine became popular in the capital, with students from the Taixue trusting it deeply, leading to countless fatalities.

Eventually, medical practitioners realized the truth and began to abandon its use. Chao Gu was originally a chivalrous person with a curiosity for the unusual, having come from Shaanxi and associated with Han Cunbao in the military, though he did not achieve success. He became a guest in Huangzhou, where Zizhan befriended him due to his adventurous spirit and sought his recipe. The world trusted Zizhan's words due to his writings.

The situation was fundamentally unrelated, yet those chasing fame forgot their own safety and recklessly tried the medicine; such confusion among people is truly remarkable.[47p]
Collection of Prescriptions

The scholar-officials should not lightly speak of medicine; however, when people are ill without serious ailments, and the poor cannot obtain medicine, if each can follow their symptoms and apply remedies, it is not without minor benefits. Although an illness may not necessarily lead to death, without medicine quick recovery is impossible. Those who moan aimlessly indeed deserve pity; their misfortune may delay recovery, and many have died from the transformation into other ailments. In urgent times, if there are means to assist, it can be said to bring one back from death. Now, in each district, every summer, a budget of two hundred thousand is allocated to supply medicine for the civilians and soldiers, as requested by Lord Han Wei when he was an advisor. Those in charge of the districts often neglect their duties, frequently letting incompetent physicians steal their salaries, or they may have medicine yet fail to reach the impoverished. When I was in Xuchang, there were indeed many illnesses annually, yet the officials neglected to uphold previous practices, with the last five years being completely forgotten, which shows their negligence.[48p]
He then gathered a thousand strings of cash to market herbal medicine in the capital, while I personally supervised the physicians in their treatments, arranging for attendants to provide daily distributions. I did not consider this beyond my duties, nor did I reproach them. Everyone was eager to participate; what hesitation could there be in this?

Since I have been residing in this mountain, I have often wished to use private funds amounting to hundreds or thousands to help a village, but I have faced the problem of lacking someone to oversee the work, and my own efforts have not been sufficient. I have sought monks or pure individuals to collaborate, yet they have not been able to fulfill my intentions.
