Someone asked: Among the writings of Zhang Zhongjing, Dongyuan, Hejian, and Danxi, which is superior? Which should one focus on studying? The answer is: One should primarily concentrate on the "Inner Canon" while broadly observing the teachings of the four masters; this way, there will be no faults. The "Inner Canon" is to medicine what the Six Classics are to Confucianism and Daoism, as it encompasses everything; the teachings of the four masters are like the "Xue," "Yong," "Yue," and "Meng," serving as steps to the Six Classics, and none can be omitted. There is no distinction of superiority or inferiority among the four works; rather, each elucidates one particular principle. Zhang Zhongjing, noting that the "Inner Canon" contains references to cold damage but lacks a comprehensive account of its variations and transformations, therefore authored his writings to elaborate on these changes.[105p]
Later generations have revered these teachings, and as they have been passed down for so long, the true essence has gradually been lost. They were used to address the ailments caused by heat and cold, leading to misguidance. Thus, the Hejian school emerged, beginning to elucidate methods for treating heat illnesses, and the Dongyuan school emerged, starting to clarify methods for treating internal injuries. The theories from Hejian are in line with the principles of the "Neijing" regarding the five movements and six energies; the teachings from Dongyuan align with the "Neijing" concerning diet and fatigue. It is not that Zhongjing was unaware of the relationship between heat illnesses and internal injuries, but rather that he had not covered these topics in his writings. When it comes to treating cold damage, both Hejian and Dongyuan strictly follow Zhongjing’s principles and dare not deviate. Later, Danxi synthesized the contributions of various physicians, elucidating conditions of Yin deficiency leading to fever and the many diseases resulting from damp heat and internal fire, also documenting these according to symptoms, ultimately just expounding upon the essential meanings of the "Neijing" and supplementing what previous sages had overlooked. Therefore, it is said: for external pathogens, one follows Zhongjing; for internal injuries, one follows Dongyuan; for heat disease, one employs Hejian; for miscellaneous ailments, one turns to Danxi. To integrate these approaches constitutes the comprehensive path of medicine.

Some may question: Zhongjing's prescriptions use very few medicinal ingredients, while Dongyuan’s often exceed twenty.[106p]
Dan Xi said: Whenever I treat illness, I follow the methods of Dong Yuan in using medicine and the prescriptions of Zhong Jing, hoping that by limiting the dosage, the efficacy of the medicine will be concentrated. Why then does Dan Xi not follow Dong Yuan and instead emulate Zhong Jing? It is said: In understanding the properties of medicine, no one surpasses Dong Yuan; he is truly the sage among physicians. Therefore, in Dong Yuan's case, it is acceptable to use more. If others emulate this excess, it can lead to confusion. Dong Yuan, like Han Xin in commanding troops, believes that more is better. However, Dan Xi can only manage a hundred thousand, thus does not dare to imitate the excess.

Someone asked: It is said that in the southeast where the climate is hot, one can take cooling medications; in the northwest where the climate is cold, one can take warming medications. Yet, people in the southeast often consume pepper, ginger, and cassia without falling ill, while those in the northwest avoid spicy and warming foods. Why is this? It is said: Although the southeast is hot, the land is low and often damp, and spicy, warming foods and medicines can also help eliminate dampness; while the northwest, although cold, has high and dry terrain, thus spicy, warming foods and medicines can help combat dryness.[107p]
To treat illnesses with medication, one must understand this principle! Master Danxi treats diseases without departing from the concepts of qi, blood, and phlegm, therefore the key to using medicine lies in three formulations: for qi, use the Four Gentleman Decoction; for blood, use the Four Substance Decoction; for phlegm, use the Erchen Decoction. Furthermore, he states that prolonged illness leads to stagnation, hence the necessity to adopt methods to treat stagnation, such as the Yueju Pill. It can be said that the three ailments of qi, blood, and phlegm often coexist with stagnation, whether stagnation arises from prolonged illness, illness causes prolonged stagnation, or misprescriptions lead to stagnation. Consequently, whenever I treat illnesses, I always incorporate methods for stagnation. If an ailment of qi accompanies stagnation, then add an herb to resolve stagnation to the Four Gentleman Decoction; the same applies for ailments of blood and phlegm. Therefore, these four methods are essential for treating illnesses with medicine. Master Danxi also notes that in recent times, many who treat diseases do not distinguish between qi and blood, merely noticing a deficiency in qi and then using ginseng and astragalus. This is indeed appropriate for qi deficiency; however, if it is blood deficiency, does this not bolster qi while depleting yin-blood? This is referred to as treating blood ailments with qi, which aggravates the depletion of blood and can lead to both qi and blood becoming deficient.[108p]
Therefore, when treating illness with medicine, it is essential to clearly distinguish between Qi and blood; they must not be confused. In ancient times, it was said: when I am ill, if I write the character for death in front of my chest from time to time, all my thoughts will cease, and my heart will find peace, which is better than taking medicine. This is truly a supreme method. If one is not cautious in illness, then death is inevitable. Those who understand this principle can cultivate a clear mind and self-discipline, being attentive in all matters, and they can recover from illness. Otherwise, even with good medicine, there will be no remedy. People often encounter illness yet indulge in their emotions and recklessness, harming themselves; this shows they do not truly fear death. There are also those who, knowing they fear death, are afraid of others discovering it. They remain silent about their condition or feign a light attitude even when seriously ill to deceive others; this is knowing the fear of death yet inadvertently courting it, which is particularly absurd.[109p]
Dongyuan and Danxi often created their own prescriptions for treating illnesses, as both scholars had a deep understanding of the properties of medicinal herbs and an in-depth knowledge of the essential methods from the "Inner Canon." Since the Song Dynasty, pre-made prescriptions have been widely adopted, and people have not dared to create their own with rashness. Although the arguments in the pre-made prescriptions often contain many inaccuracies, Danxi has debated these points. Nevertheless, the formulas are all composed by renowned physicians, and their hierarchical structure, dosage, urgency, and proportions are generally accurate. Recently, the works of Dongyuan and Danxi have become popular, and contemporary doctors, noticing that they do not use ancient formulas, tend to imitate their practices and create their own prescriptions. However, lacking clarity on the properties of the medicines and not comprehending the reasoning behind the prescriptions, they end up causing confusion, with varied and troublesome symptoms arising, making it difficult to recognize and treat them. Furthermore, since different medicines have distinct flavors, for instance, Schisandra has a thick flavor, Dongyuan's prescription calls for five or six grains at a minimum and up to a dozen at most, whereas contemporary doctors may use two or three taels. Gypsum has a light flavor, thus Baihu Decoction uses half a tael, while modern doctors do not dare to use more.[110p]
To supplement the treatment, one should focus on light and minor ailments. Currently, regardless of condition severity, large doses are commonly used. Pills, powders, soups, and liquids each have their specific suitability. Now, regardless of urgency, decoctions are generally the preferred method. Such occurrences are numerous. Today’s physicians, if not well-versed in the "Materia Medica" and deeply studied the "Inner Canon," while lightly creating their own prescriptions, rarely avoid causing harm.

Someone asked: It is said that Dongyuan's methods are suitable for the North and Danxi's methods can be applied in the South. How should this be interpreted? The reply: Dongyuan is a Northern physician, and Luo Qianfu transmitted his teachings, gaining recognition in Jiangsu and Zhejiang. Danxi is a Southern physician, and Liu Zonghou carried on his teachings, becoming well-known in Shaanxi. If this were as they say, would it mean that the "Materia Medica" and the "Inner Canon," the teachings of Shen Nong and Huang Di as well as Qi Bo, are only applicable in the North? However, the Five Elements give rise to different diseases, and their treatments require distinct approaches; the "Inner Canon" has already thoroughly discussed these varied methods and the principles of the Five Constants.[111p]
Just as the north is often cold and the south is often hot, the rivers and lakes are very humid, while Lingnan is prone to miasma. This is attributed to the prevalence of certain climatic influences, resulting in the rise of various diseases in these regions—not because northern diseases lack heat or southern diseases lack cold. Regarding the treatment of cold with heat and heat with cold, the principles are the same across the five directions; how could there be a difference between north and south? However, within human organs, fire occupies two places, while in the six atmospheric influences of heaven, heat accounts for three and a half parts. Therefore, illnesses in the world are characterized by more heat and less cold. This can be seen in the chapter on disease mechanisms in the "Inner Canon." Additionally, the combination of dampness and heat often leads to many diseases. Since the writings of the Grand Minister have fallen into obscurity, the "Jufang" has resorted to the common use of damp-heat remedies. Hence, Danxi emerged to elucidate the teachings of the "Inner Canon," clarify the biases of the "Jufang," and discuss the diseases caused by the interplay of dampness and heat to address the inadequacies of previous scholars. Later generations, not understanding this, regarded the frequent use of bitter and cold medicines like qin, lian, zhi, and ye as appropriate for the south, which is a shallow interpretation![112p]
The Three Books of Baizhai

Methods of Treatment - "Suwen"

In treating illnesses, one must understand the natural laws and geographical conditions, as well as the strength and weakness of the person. It is essential to inquire about daily activities, diet, and extreme pleasures or sufferings. For illnesses originating from within, one must first address the internal causes before treating the external symptoms. Conversely, if the illness comes from external factors, one must first treat the exterior before addressing the interior. If internal and external do not interact, then treat the primary ailment. If urgency arises, treat the superficial symptoms; if the situation is more relaxed, treat the root cause first. Address the related causes before the primary ones. To alleviate urgent conditions, supplement the lower ones to control the upper, and for urgent cases below, supplement the upper to control the lower. For severe poisons, treat ten cases with six remedies; for moderate poisons, treat ten cases with seven remedies; for minor poisons, treat ten cases with eight remedies; and for non-toxic conditions, treat ten cases with nine remedies. It is essential to nourish and harmonize, waiting for recovery. These principles are all derived from the "Suwen" text and are essential knowledge for medical practitioners; they cannot be fully recorded here, so I summarize the key points to illustrate their essence.[113p]
Preface

Medicine is one of the matters of the Confucian scholar, and it is unclear at what point it diverged into two paths. When one’s parents or close relatives fall ill, they entrust their care to others, allowing those without such kinship to take charge of life and death. If there is any mistake, the consequences could last a lifetime. Those who advocate for benevolence to others are unable to apply that same benevolence towards their parents; thus, they fall short in compassion. Friends unite through righteousness, willing to face difficulties, irrespective of the risk—be it water, fire, or warfare; hence, they do not hesitate in urgent matters, regardless of material loss, be it gold, jade, grain, or silk. When one is ill, they might say that they do not understand the ailment; then others claim to thoroughly understand and seek treatment that may not align, each offering conflicting opinions, leading to confusion and inability to discern correctly. They may only be able to address external wounds, yet cannot guide friends through life and death situations; therefore, they lack righteousness. They prioritize personal desires: longing for delicious food, insisting on fine clothing and ornaments, and being careful about offspring and lineage. Yet when sickness arises, they are oblivious, leaving matters to women, relatives, and friends, or to crazed shamans and blind fortune-tellers, and even in dire situations they fail to distinguish between correct and erroneous treatments; thus, they lack knowledge. Among the Five Constants, with three missing, it is imperative for a Confucian to also engage in medicine. Hence it is said: Medicine is one of the matters of the Confucian scholar.[114p]
The Challenges of Medicine

The Five Classics and Four Books, as well as military and national rites and clothing, if discussed with inconsistencies, are merely matters of conduct. However, when it comes to medicinal herbs and prescriptions, even a slight mistake can endanger life. A ruler of a thousand chariots or a wealthy leader of a hundred pieces of gold should surely think deeply and exercise caution. In the past, when Prince Xu served medicine without tasting it, he was charged with murdering his ruler. When Ji Kangzi offered medicine, Confucius expressed uncertainty, knowing well that medicine should not be taken lightly. During the Jin dynasty, a talented individual wished to revise the "I Ching" and various prescriptions, first discussing and debating the classics with Zunuo. Even with differences, they were not enough to damage moral teachings. However, when it comes to medicine, a minor error can lead to life or death; thus, future generations suffer greatly. How can we treat this lightly?

The Essence of a Healer

Any practitioner of medicine must have a general understanding of both ancient and contemporary knowledge, adhere to benevolence and righteousness, and eliminate the desire for fame and profit. One should dedicate oneself to relief and assistance. If this is achieved, one’s understanding will naturally become clear, and spiritual insights will come forth. Why then be anxious to seek fame and engage in petty profit? If not, how could one avoid the shame of Jiang who was too eager for reputation or of Hua Tuo who, despite his skills, met his demise?[115p]
The Physician's Restraint from Lust

During the Xuanhe period, there was a scholar who suffered from a chronic illness and could not find a cure despite numerous treatments. A man named He Cheng was skilled in medicine. His wife requested to see him, leading him into a private chamber and said, “I have seen my husband suffer for a long time, and we have nearly sold everything we own to pay for his treatments. I am willing to offer myself in exchange.” He Cheng sternly replied, “Madam, why would you speak in such a manner? Please rest assured, I will treat your husband and achieve results; do not tarnish yourself in this way. If there exists no man who would issue punishment, certainly there will be retribution from gods and spirits.” Before long, the husband recovered. One night, He Cheng dreamt he entered a shrine, where a judge spoke to him, saying, “Your medical skills have succeeded. At a time of great urgency, you did not succumb to lust or greed. The Supreme Deity decrees you a reward of fifty thousand coins and a government official post.” A few months later, the Eastern Palace fell ill, and the imperial physicians could not cure it. An edict summoned grassroots doctors, and He Cheng responded, bringing medicine that ensured recovery. The court granted him the same official position and compensation as he had dreamt.

The Hidden Physician

The way of medicine has long existed. The healing of illnesses originates from the Yellow Emperor; discerning the flavors of countless herbs comes from Shen Nong; the concoction of decoctions is attributed to Yi Yin. These three sages alleviated the suffering of the common people and contributed to the nurturing of heaven and earth, thus their contributions to future generations are immensely significant. Among all the ages, those who delve deeply into this path are also disciples of the sages. Jia Yi stated: "The sages of old did not dwell in the court; they must have hidden themselves among physicians and diviners." Who says there are no heroes among those skilled in techniques?[116p]
The art of healing is vast! From the time of Shennong, Huangdi, Qibo, and Leigong, it has been nothing but sages and philosophers opening its sources and wise individuals guiding its flow, thus able to alleviate the sufferings of the common people and support the creation of the heavens and earth. As the world has degraded, the scholars and gentry regard this as merely a skill and disdain it, handing it over to the common people. Therefore, the most subtle and profound principles have emerged from the most humble and base thoughts, resulting in many who cannot heal the sick and instead hasten their demise. This is why Fan Wenzheng declared that if one cannot be a good minister, then one should be a good doctor. Mr. Yichuan stated that entrusting treatment of illnesses to mediocre physicians is tantamount to being unkind and unfilial.

Since ancient times, renowned medical practitioners like He Huan, Bian Que, Chunyu Yi, Zhang Zhongjing, and Sun Zhenren consistently emphasized preservation of health as the priority, with medicine as a supplement. Regarding the observation of symptoms and diagnosis of pulses, they would meticulously discern even the most subtle doubts. It is lamentable that today’s physicians simplify the complexities, treating illness as if it were an enemy to be vanquished, much like strategizing for battle. They merely glimpse the teachings of Hua Tuo, who discusses methods such as dissecting and reconnecting tendons, which are just specialized techniques and not the fundamental principles of the "Shennong Bencao" or classical formulas of established medicinal properties. Today, only Zhang Zhongjing's works serve as the model for numerous formulas, and scholars should adhere to his teachings.[117p]
The Continuation of Medical Sayings

Doctors must understand the pulse to recognize illnesses. In ancient times, they only used one medicine to treat a condition; the medicine was pure, and the effect was swift. Nowadays, people do not identify the source of the disease or differentiate pulse patterns, leading to a multitude of medicines—often fifteen or sixteen ingredients—mixed between strengthening and reducing treatments, which makes achieving efficacy difficult. Han Tianjue stated: there is no need for numerous ingredients in a prescription; just look at how simple and convenient Zhang Zhongjing’s formulas are. Danxi mentioned that using medicine is like waging war; more is not necessarily better, as this often leads to confusion.[118p]
The Value of Adaptability in Medicine

Wang Wenzhong stated: Many disciples of Li Ming are located in Zhongzhou, but only Liu Shouzheng's teachings are passed on by the Buddhist masters of Jing Mountains. When the master arrived in Jiangnan, he imparted his knowledge to Luo Zhitai of Song, and all the physicians of the South revered it. In the present dynasty, discussions about medicine do not neglect Liu's teachings. Although Liu and Li's methods differ in their approach to treatment, both can be reconciled and applied according to the situation and evidence; is there not validity in this? Recently, in Wuzhong, a good physician is identified as one who adheres to Dongyuan, while those who follow Zhang and Liu are labeled as esteemed practitioners. Alas! Yao and Shun acted with humility, while Tang and Wu resorted to warfare; as long as they aligned with the way to benefit the world, what need is there for sameness? I often lament those in the world focused solely on attack and defense, for the evil qi has not retreated while the true qi wanes. Those fixated on nourishment may also cause blockages in the qi path, resulting in considerable harm, where the true qi remains unrestored while the evil qi grows fiercer. The ancients said: Medicine is valuable when it is appropriate, and methods must be adaptable. If one adheres too strictly to the usual, ginseng may inadvertently kill; while understanding change, even Aconite can save lives. The words of Master Sun that one should adjust according to the time and circumstances are indeed wise! The complete compilation of ancient and modern medical literature, volume five hundred and three.[119p]
General Discussion

Wen Ding Gong's Family Collection

Monks and Quacks Recklessly Treating Illnesses Leading to Rapid Death

Chen Ruzhong once suffered from a disorder of spleen and stomach due to excessive fire, seeking treatment from Sheng Yongmei. After diagnosing his pulse, he said: "If treated this way, you can live; if treated that way, you will die. This method may lead to a slight prolongation of life, but that method will cause swift death." However, after starting treatment, Ruzhong's recovery was slow. Some recommended a monk known for his medical skills, and Ruzhong was swayed by their words, thus seeking treatment from him. He took the monk's medicine and vomited a liter of blood before dying. Alas! When a physician uses medicine to combat illness, if the illness does not go away but instead leads to death, what is the purpose of medicine? That monk is merely a quack, lacking understanding of medical texts and unable to distinguish the properties of herbs, unable to observe the state of the pulse, ignoring the condition and duration of one's ailments, and only focusing on toxic substances to attack illness—his methods have likely resulted in many deaths. Now I blame Ruzhong for poorly choosing his physician, which led to his swift demise, and I write this as a caution for those who lightly take medicine.[120p]
Medical Integration
By Fu Zi (Ming Dynasty)

The Way of Medicine requires both precision and breadth for a clear understanding of its principles. Therefore, the sages of the past established that one must first read Confucian texts to understand the principles of change. Works such as the "Suwen," "Nanjing," "Bencao," and "Mai Jing" should not be overlooked. Why is this so? It is because without the Four Books, one cannot grasp the intricacies of meaning; without the "I Ching," one cannot comprehend the dynamics of yin and yang; without the "Suwen," one cannot recognize illnesses; without the "Bencao," one cannot identify medicines; and without the "Mai Jing," one cannot diagnose the evidences of cold, heat, deficiency, and excess based on pulse examination. Thus, these texts are indispensable.

Moreover, human life is of utmost importance, and the variations of illness are infinite. There are old and young, differences in constitution, treatments for the five directions, distinctions for the four seasons, and nuances of yin and yang, with the need to discern cold from heat. The internal organs, meridians, qi, and blood should all be categorized properly. The interplay of the six climatic energies and the erratic expressions of the seven emotions, as well as the constant changes in qi, require different approaches and varying urgencies in treatment. Additionally, one must observe characteristics in color and pulse, inquire about daily habits, and understand that although symptoms may be similar, treatments can differ significantly. The sages offer only a glimpse into these matters. Therefore, later scholars must familiarize themselves with the strengths of various texts, integrate what they observe, and apply treatment accordingly, allowing them to be capable practitioners.[121p]
Collected Works of Jiuling Mountain House
Author: Dai Liang of the Yuan Dynasty
On the Common Path of Medicine and Confucianism

Dai Shuming of Jinhua said: "The practice of medicine is fundamentally about preserving life, which is closely aligned with our Confucian principles. Since the Tang Dynasty, medicine has been classified as a skill, yet we Confucians disdain to engage in it. Those who practice medicine in the world merely recite established doctrines from one school, adhering to fixed methods to merely chance upon a cure for illness. They no longer delve deeply into exploration, seeking the sages' intent to clarify the conjunction of yin and yang and the processes of creation; nor are they able to comprehensively study a myriad of texts, select diverse opinions, and adapt their discourse to changing circumstances in treatment.

Moreover, some go so far as to discard ancient methods, adopting speculative interpretations that lead to confusion, and their missteps are not few. Is this truly in line with the virtuous intent of sages and their compassion for the common people?"[122p]
In today's world, there are those who practice medicine purely for the sake of diagnosis and treatment, but there are few who seek a mentor to learn from. Our ancestors established the Imperial Medical Academy in the inner court and set up schools in the provinces and counties to gather individuals under the name of medicine. This was intended to cultivate talent, test their mastery, and subsequently appoint them to positions of public health responsibility, thereby promoting them to become national physicians, which greatly benefits the people of the world.

Historically, during the Chengzhou era, those regarded as physicians were highly selective and appointed as important officials. The current dynasty also established the foundations of medicine by carefully selecting and assigning upper-level officials to gather and educate all those who practiced medicine. They would study the texts of Xuanqi and refine the techniques of Zhang and Li, ensuring mastery before assigning them to positions. By honing their skills and specializing in their craft, they would effectively manage affairs and determine salaries, thereby eliminating the risk of premature death or illness among the populace, allowing everyone to thrive in health and longevity.

Therefore, medicine is considered one of the essential aspects of governance.[123p]
The Essentials of Medicine: Eight Key Considerations

There are eight essential points in treating illnesses; if these eight points are not carefully assessed, the illness cannot be cured. If it cannot be cured, there will be no methods available to the physician. Therefore, it is necessary to thoroughly distinguish these eight points to avoid mistakes.

First is Deficiency, which refers to the five forms of deficiency: a thin pulse, cold skin, low energy, diarrhea, and lack of appetite—these constitute the five deficiencies.

Second is Excess, which refers to the five forms of excess: a strong pulse, hot skin, abdominal distension, blockage in the front and back, and a feeling of fullness—these are the five excesses.

Third is Cold, which indicates the accumulation of cold in the internal organs.

Fourth is Heat, indicating the accumulation of heat in the internal organs.

Fifth is Pathogen, which refers to diseases not associated with the internal organs.

Sixth is Correctness, which implies that it is not an external pathogen that has entered the body.

Seventh is Internal, meaning the illness is not external.

Eighth is External, meaning the illness is not internal.

Once these eight essential points are assessed, they should be compared with the six pulses to determine their origin, and then combined with observation, listening, questioning, and pulse-taking. There should be no illness that cannot be treated![124p]
The use of medicine should not be careless.

Using medicine is like administering punishment; a mistake can lead to life or death. Just as a punishment requires thorough investigation and deliberation before a verdict is given, and a crime is recorded, so must we be meticulous with life, for death cannot be reversed. The same principle applies to medicine. When a physician visits a patient, if they possess the skill to diagnose correctly and understand the relationship between pulse and medicine, then their prescriptions can yield immediate results. However, if a lesser practitioner acts recklessly, applying remedies haphazardly, it can quickly lead to peril. How easy it is to cause harm! This arises from patients' inability to choose their doctors wisely, having never taken care to learn about medical practices beforehand. Is this not a matter for caution?[125p]
The Medical Principles of Ancient and Modern Times
Ming Dynasty - Xu Chunfu

Caution in Illness and Medical Care

The sage treats diseases before they arise, not after they have manifested. This does not mean that one neglects to treat existing illnesses, nor that one is incapable of doing so. It implies that prevention is most effective at the very beginning; by carefully addressing the early signs and guarding against the petty manifestations, one achieves greater success with fewer harms. Only by addressing the subtle beginning does one prevent it from developing into a more serious condition, thereby avoiding any sickness that must be treated later.

People today tend to treat existing illnesses rather than prevent emerging ones, often disregarding early warning signs and waiting for the disease to fully develop before seeking medical help. This neglect of early symptoms leads to more severe conditions, which is clearly evident. The sage’s approach to daily life and actions is meticulous, ensuring proper care at all times. If even minor, obscure ailments arise, they are carefully attended to with preventive measures and treatments. Thus, the sage's practice of treating ailments before they manifest rather than after is exemplified.

The Analects say: “What the Master is cautious about, are the concerns of battle and illness.” Cheng Yi explains: “Battle concerns the oversight of the divine; sincerity leads to spiritual elevation; illness pertains to matters of life and death; and battle relates to the survival of the nation.”[126p]
The three cautions are indeed the most significant; however, is it not to be taken with utmost seriousness? When Master Kang was offered medicine, he said he would not dare to taste it until he was fully aware of its effects. This illustrates how the sages carefully consider illness and medicine with great seriousness.

People in the world not only lack knowledge of preventing illness before it arises, but also, when they do fall ill, they are unaware of the need for caution. At the onset of slight ailments, they indulge without restraint, leading to severe problems. Only in desperation do they seek medical help, often recklessly choosing based on rumors or recommendations, entrusting their care to the foolish. As a result, they suffer significant damage due to their previous negligence and lack of reflection on medical treatment.

In urgent situations, one does not have the luxury to carefully select a physician. Little do they realize that medicine is essential for everyone; even the sages are not exempt from illness. However, they have always sought knowledge and have acquainted themselves with well-regarded physicians. When a minor ailment arises, they seek treatment readily, finding cures with ease.

It is akin to remedying a fire hazard before it ignites; how could one suffer from burning if one acts prudently? Danxi has undoubtedly elaborated on this matter. The superficial understanding often leaves much yet to be comprehended. Indeed, the astonishment of the scholar is something that cannot be avoided.

For those keen on cultivating a healthy life, expanding and deepening their understanding may not be without minor benefits.[127p]
The Ancient Medicine's Fourteen Categories

Among the Fourteen Categories of Ancient Medicine, there was originally a category for the spleen and stomach, which has now been lost. The "Daozang Jing" contains much discussion on this matter. Since the Song and Yuan dynasties, only thirteen categories have been used for evaluating medical practices. These are: the category for wind, followed by the category for external illnesses, then the major pulse category, the minor pulse category, the category for women's obstetrics, the acupuncture category, the ophthalmology category, the category for throat, mouth, and teeth, the category for sores and ulcers, the orthopedics category, the category for gold and arrows, the category for health preservation, and the category for incantations. In our current dynasty, only thirteen categories have been retained, and the category for the spleen and stomach has never been revived. Li Gao from the Yuan dynasty wrote "On the Spleen and Stomach," which is notably detailed. However, it does not mention the absence of the fourteen categories—was this due to his own interpretation of ancient texts, or was it purely a new invention that aligned with ancient knowledge? This indeed is a great fortune for the medical path. Upon observing contemporary physicians, many do not specialize in the spleen and stomach; they merely employ methods of opposite treatment, attacking diseases to address superficial symptoms, knowing only to treat heat with cold, cold with heat, obstruction with passage, and passage with obstruction. Using cold because of cold, or heat because of heat, or passage because of passage, or obstruction because of obstruction, must invariably lead to exacerbating the original condition, neglecting its underlying causes, which is the so-called method of following treatment; they seem utterly unaware of this. Moreover, when it leads to damage of the spleen and stomach, they still do not pay attention, and when the vital energy is compromised, many symptoms can arise.[128p]
If the spleen is deficient and the qi is short, leading to an inability to sustain oneself, resulting in what resembles wheezing and panting, physicians still prescribe medicines to descend qi and alleviate wheezing. If the spleen is weak and the defensive qi does not circulate, leading to swelling, physicians still prescribe medicines to deplete qi and promote water metabolism. If the spleen is weak and there is stagnation, resulting in alternating fever and chills, physicians attribute it to external pathogens and use dispersing medicines. In large segments like these, the deficiency merely aggravates the deficiency, leading to the depletion of qi and death; subsequently administering ginseng decoction or aconite decoction after collapse—how can it possibly nurture life?

From what is observed now, those who are insufficient are often the majority, especially since the forces of competition in the pursuit of fame and profit lead to excessive worry, damaging the spleen and causing illness for many. If the body is robust yet is occasionally invaded by wind, cold, summer heat, or dampness, such cases are rarely seen to recover simply through attack. This explains why traditional Chinese medicine can easily lead to success in treatment. However, when encountering the spleen and stomach deficiency causing attacks from wind, cold, summer heat, or dampness, and treating those who have strong bodies, significant variances can arise. If one continues to aggressively treat without cease, they may say that the medicine does not diminish the dizziness, and the disease does not heal. They must take vigorous measures, yet this further injures the spleen and stomach, making the condition worse. When their techniques are exhausted and they feel there is no solution, they say it is difficult to treat. What a great flaw this is; how could one chronicle it all? Ultimately, it is due to a lack of understanding of the root cause.

The classics say: "Those who have grain will thrive; those who lose grain will perish." It is also said: "Those who have stomach qi live; those who lack stomach qi die." Therefore, the stomach qi and grain qi, is it not the foundation of the human body?[129p]
Time Doctors

It is often said: a skilled doctor is not as good as a timely doctor. This means that though a timely doctor may not be well-educated, their ability to seize the moment and adapt can still yield results. They often say: "If you have an illness, come to me while I am in my prime." They also claim: "Even if you are well-read in the works of Wang Shuhe, you cannot compare to my experience with many cases." There is a local saying: "The left heart, small intestine, liver, and gallbladder are wise; when the time is right, you can earn a thousand coins a day." That is why it is said that a skilled doctor is not as good as a timely doctor. The "Treasured Mirror of Health" refers to a fortune doctor, which serves as a serious warning. Nowadays, patients do not understand this principle, entrusting themselves to timely doctors, which is akin to self-destructive behavior, willingly surrendering to decline. What difference is there?[130p]
Witch Doctors

Witch doctors replace physicians, hence the term "witch doctor."
According to the "Analects," a person without constancy cannot be a witch doctor. Confucius laments that one cannot be without constancy, and his reasoning is sound. Zhuzi comments that a witch interacts with spirits, while medicine pertains to matters of life and death. The two are distinct and shouldn't be equated. The way of medicine originated with Shennong and was elaborated by the Yellow Emperor, who specified certain medicines for certain ailments, reflected in texts like the "Neijing" and "Suwen," considered the sacred canons for aiding people’s lives; how can this be compared to the practices of witch doctors? However, practitioners of medicine vary in skill; thus, their names differ. Those who are proficient in medicine are called wise doctors, those skilled are good doctors, those preserving the health of the country are national doctors, while those who lack understanding are termed incompetent doctors. Those who beat drums and perform dances to ward off illness are called witch doctors. Therefore, witch doctors, who do not understand the principles of medicine, are referred to as such by those in the South. In modern times, they may be called "Duan Gong Tai Bao," or "night-walking diviners," and in the North referred to as "Shi Po." Though they are all considered to be of dubious character, one still cannot lack constancy, how much less so in other matters?[131p]
Famous Physicians

Medicine is entrusted with the mandate of life; it should not be approached with superficial embellishments or fabrications. Thus, a physician should not practice medicine for three generations nor take their own medicine; only by experiencing hardships can one become a good physician. This implies that mastery comes from deep learning. The contemporary practitioners often rely on their renown for fame and profit, failing to dedicate themselves to diligent study. The people in the capital are particularly enamored with empty reputation, disregarding practical knowledge. They rush to offer praise for new theories without ensuring their validity and often do not trust what is newly learned, handing over their lives to hollow names, which is quite regrettable!

Zhi Benzhong said: In the past, the Yue people strategized in support of Duke Lu; strong in resolve but weak in action, they were good at planning but poor at execution. The Zhao and Qi had aspirations that were weak but would assert themselves strongly, lacking in contemplation and suffering from their own stubbornness. They drank medicinal wine to alter the hearts of these two men, transforming them both into celebrated scholars. Observing today’s patients seeking medical help, most prioritize those with timely renown, seldom considering whether the physician has truly studied or understands the pulse. They label them as contemporary, fortunate, or famous physicians, easily elected by the powerful, yielding to treatment even at the cost of their lives, doing so without complaint. Consequently, many physicians hustle for favor with the influential, adopting servile attitudes to seek recommendations, scheming for profit and fame, while knowledgeable individuals mock them. Compassion and moral integrity have been cast aside. How can one expect to drink medicinal wine and change their hearts in such conditions?[132p]
The Confucian Healer

Zhao Conggu said: During the Qingli era, there was a successful candidate named Shen Chang, who was known for his integrity and uprightness. He was usually solitary and often attracted the admiration of those who came after him. Yet he never sought recommendations for advancement, and would often sigh, saying: "I am in dire straits, still unable to escape poverty; is this not my fate?" Thus, he entered the capital, seeking a livelihood.

While wandering to the East Huamen, he happened to see several officials mounted on horses, brandishing whips, with their followers being numerous and impressive. He asked a merchant in the market, inquiring about their status. The merchant replied: “They are officials in the Hanlin Academy and medical officers.” Chang sighed again, saying: “I am poor in the teachings of Confucius; how could I not be aware of the likes of licorice and rhubarb?” This marked the beginning of his intention to study medicine.

Next, he saw merchants selling medicinal goods, speaking skillfully yet with difficulty, and he felt ashamed to pursue that path. Unsure and indecisive, he discussed with his peers, saying: “We, who study, may rise or fall based on the written texts; our teachers must seek out distinguished positions, and surely it ought not to be in common paths.” At that time, I was a physician in the imperial medical office; Shen would often send long letters requesting to see me, and I would eagerly receive him, with no one daring to be negligent.[133p]
It is often said: "Those who come from difficult backgrounds, relying on mutual encouragement, tend to learn medicine. Hearing of your honorable name, I have come to seek guidance." I replied: "The art of medicine is certainly secondary to the Confucian teachings. However, since it concerns life and death, it is not to be taken lightly. If a learner does not possess a genuine passion and dedication, it is difficult to grasp its subtleties. You have mentioned your difficulties, indicating that your ambitions have not yet been realized; returning to study medicine raises concerns that your stagnation might hinder your ability to delve deeply into it." He, often with an annoyed expression, said: "Although you are facing hardships, you still follow Confucianism and read the works of Confucius and Mencius, having a basic understanding of the principles of governance throughout history. Now, simply aspiring to learn a technical skill—how can that be a lofty pursuit?" I responded: "I fear that mere superficial engagement will not suffice. You have not yet comprehended the teachings of the ancient Three Sovereigns on medicine, so let us set that aside. Even so, figures such as Zhang Zhongjing of the Han, Ge Hong of the Jin, Chu Cheng of the Qi, and Tao Yinjun of the Liang all practiced Confucianism while possessing talent and virtue. I have heard that the scholar knows rites and righteousness, while the physician understands gain and loss. Without proper cultivation of rituals and righteousness, one becomes ignorant of the teachings of Confucius and Mencius. Failing to distinguish between gain and loss endangers the lives of the people. Can the realms of Confucianism and medicine be treated lightly? Can they be separated?"[134p]
Five Difficulties

Song Lian said: Alas! How difficult it is to speak of medicine! The life of a person flows in circulation with the energies of heaven and earth. If one manages to nourish oneself correctly, then harmony prevails and a hundred ailments disappear. If one loses this nourishment, internal injuries arise from the seven emotions, and external afflictions from the six energies, leading to illness. The physician must investigate the root and branches; in essence, he must diminish what is substantive and augment what is deficient. The balance of yin and yang must be preserved, allowing one to adapt to the center. If a crude practitioner is ignorant of this, mixing the substantiated and the vacuous, he will exhaust what is lacking while enhancing what is excessive. Consequently, the ability of illness to arise becomes rare; this constitutes one difficulty. The movement of qi and blood requires specific conduits. The arms and legs, each embodying yin and yang, must balance; if the three yang are insufficient, what of the yangming? It pertains to the principle of two yangs converging in brightness. If yin is excessively diminished, what of the jueyin? It refers to the union of two yins to their fullest extent. When facing illness, it is imperative to know which remedies to apply. While treating may not be difficult, one must also understand the medicinal properties that guide the principles; by following this method, no sickness will be without remedy.[135p]
Rough practitioners do not distinguish the Twelve Meridians and apply treatments indiscriminately, much like a sparrow caught in the river or fishing in the woods; even if they occasionally succeed, it is sheer luck! This is the second difficulty. The seasonal energies are different each year, and treatments should be accordingly adjusted. When it is time for rising and falling, we should follow and accept their flow; when faced with cold and heat, we should resist and counter them. In doing so, we can preserve the natural harmony and avoid disorder and error. Rough practitioners, however, blindly go against the grain, reversing what should be followed and following what should be resisted, like a blind person lost in the wilderness, unable to discern east from west. This constitutes the third difficulty. Diseases can manifest as hot or cold; heat requires cooling treatments, while cold should be treated with warmth—this is a fundamental principle. Yet, when the forces of cold and heat are intense, attempting to reverse them suddenly will surely clash and create obstacles. Heat must be treated with heat, and cold with cold; their beginnings may be the same, but their ends are different, which may lead to successful outcomes. Rough practitioners, however, fail to observe this and mix treatments indiscriminately. This is the fourth difficulty. Medicinal properties have yin and yang, yet they are not solely categorized as such; there exists yin within yang and yang within yin. A slight deviation can result in a significant error. Rough practitioners neglect to weigh the relative strengths and recklessly apply treatments. This is the fifth difficulty.[136p]
In a memorial to Emperor Shen-zong of the Song dynasty, Lu Hui states: I originally had no chronic illness, but by chance I encountered a doctor whose techniques were errant. He failed to understand the pulse conditions, the balance of yin and yang, the distinction between root and branch in treatment, and the appropriate order for administering medicine. Recklessly prescribing decoctions and following personal whims, he misdiagnosed and caused calamity to the limbs, endangering the heart and abdomen. While using my personal ailment as an analogy for governance, it underscores the grave errors in medical practice. Should not those who practice medicine take this as a warning?[137p]
On Medicine

Yuli Zi said: Governing the world is akin to practicing medicine, is it not? A physician diagnoses by feeling the pulse to understand the symptoms and examines the symptoms to formulate a treatment plan. Symptoms possess yin and yang, vacuity and solidity, while the pulse can be superficial or deep, fine or strong, and the treatment will involve the right balance of tonification and purgation, acupuncture, cautery, and decoctions. The herbs such as ginseng, poria, ginger, cinnamon, salt, and huang, are applied according to the patient's illness; if appropriately administered, they lead to life, but if not, they result in death. Therefore, to know the symptoms and pulse yet not be adept at formulating treatments, one is not a physician. Even possessing knowledge like Bian Que's, if one merely boasts without usefulness, without understanding symptoms and pulse, relying on hearsay for treatments, declaring, "I can heal," is to be a thief of the world. Thus, disorder is equivalent to symptoms; order and structure are like the pulse; morality and laws correspond to treatments and regulations; talent is like medicine. In the governance of the Xia, loyalty was emphasized, while the Yin took advantage of its deficiencies to restore it with substance; the governance of the Yin valued substance, while the Zhou exploited its flaws to restore it with culture. The Qin applied harsh punishments and severe laws to suppress the world, resulting in widespread suffering. Yet, the Han embraced leniency and maintained tranquility, perfectly matching their treatments with symptoms; their medicinal applications seldom failed, ensuring that few ailments remained untreated. Furthermore, one must understand that if the cold at a single finger is not warmed, it will affect the hands and feet, and if the cold at the hands and feet is not warmed, the entire body will suffer, as the energies and pulses are interconnected. A small issue can lead to a significant one; thus, illnesses in people begin at a minor level. Failure to comprehend the fundamental causes, or even understanding yet being confused, ultimately leads to a major issue that cannot be saved, resulting in death. Is this not tragic? Oh! Someone like Yuli Zi can be said to have a profound understanding of the essence of medicine![138p]
Pao Fang Shu

Duke Xuan of Lu wrote the Pao Fang Shu in Zhongzhou to measure the months; it was not merely to avoid disaster but demonstrated that the mind of a noble person applies itself fully in all matters. The esteemed figures of the past were often able to heal, not only in terms of hygiene but also regarding physical ailments, yet people today are ashamed to speak of this. Recently, wealthy families have been collecting and publishing prescriptions, which are widely disseminated, all reflecting the intentions of benevolent individuals. The "Ben Shi Dan Fang" has recently been published in the collections and miscellaneous writings of various gentlemen from the Four Ming dynasty. There are many famous prescriptions that remain unrecorded, and I have often attempted to compile them, lamenting that my collection of books is not extensive. If there are those who can take my words and share them with others, that would also serve as a contribution to helping others.[139p]

The Unity of Principles

Diseases such as cold damage, internal injury, women’s issues, and children’s ailments are all within the practice of medicine, but they have each become categorized into different schools over the generations. In today’s world, some are labeled as specialists in certain fields, others as having multiple specialties, while some are deemed not to belong to certain fields. They are unaware that in ancient times, there was a physician named Bian Que who traveled past Handan and became known as a female physician when he encountered noble women; he passed by Suiyang and became known as a doctor for the elderly when he heard of the Zhou people's affection for older adults; he entered Xiangyang and became a pediatrician upon learning of the Qin people’s love for children. He adapted to societal norms without distinguishing differences in the treatment of illnesses. When it comes to medicine, all is interconnected by the same principles. To say that one is strong in one area and weak in another also indicates a lack of thorough understanding of these principles.

The Goodness of Acupuncture, Moxibustion, and Medicine

Bian Que remarked that diseases are limited to the area affected by the skin and muscle, or those that penetrate the blood vessels and tissues, or involve the stomach and intestines. Therefore, one must integrate acupuncture, moxibustion, and medicine in order to genuinely engage in the healing arts. Engaging in true medical practice requires a comprehensive understanding akin to that of a well-rounded official in the Zhou dynasty. In the past, the fallacy was to restrict the art of saving lives solely to medication, thus neglecting acupuncture and moxibustion. Whenever faced with diseases like cold damage that penetrate into the blood chamber, or injuries from impact, which cannot be cured by medicinal pills, one might find themselves at a loss without the intervention of acupuncture, feeling inadequate in skill. It was realized that treating illness is much like a strategic confrontation, where offense and defense must align with the characteristics of the enemy, revealing the true benefits of acupuncture, moxibustion, and medicine in relation to the illness at hand.[140p]
Report on the Incompetent Doctor

The essence of medicine lies in specialization; without it, the harm done is not trivial. Occasionally, there are ignorant individuals who misappropriate the name of medicine, copying established prescriptions and barely noting the "Nanjing" and "Pulse Scripture," believing that medicine holds no difficulties. Apart from this, they merely groom their appearance, adorn themselves in fine clothes, practice empty rhetoric, flatter the wealthy, and thus gain hollow praise, becoming self-satisfied and ostentatious. When faced with those who truly understand, they become restless and unable to apply their tricks, yet they continue to flatter while slandering others behind their backs. They ridicule their peers who study and seek knowledge, tiring themselves in vain. Whenever they attempt treatments, they act irrationally, haphazardly dispensing medicines, occasionally finding fleeting success, but invariably demanding excessive compensation. If any harm results, they claim to have done their best. It is common for many to adopt this approach and claim that medicine is simple; is that really the case? Alas! Daily, the practice of medicine is descending into error, and it is no wonder that the learned gentlemen are so shallow.[141p]

It is rare to find a true doctor who is genuinely effective. Fortunately, the way of heaven promotes life and detests killing, promptly revealing the consequences of one's actions, while incompetent doctors unjustly perish, witnessed by all. Recently, there was a scholar who was fatally harmed by a mistake in medication; his family sued, and the legal authorities apprehended and interrogated, but the punishment was merely a beating, and he was released to return home. Within a year, he was brutally murdered by robbers. Is this not a manifestation of heaven’s retribution? A poem mocking incompetent doctors suggests: "Who knows the impostors, while the virtuous remain blind to the truth? If incompetent doctors don't perish early, they could ruin countless lives." Is this not a sign of heavenly disdain for them? Therefore, I urge my students to understand that medicine is a profound way of nature, linked to life and death. Professionals must diligently study to reach the highest standards. One should not think it easy to deceive others or rely solely on luck. If one masters their craft, there will undoubtedly be those who recognize the effort. Those who do not seek to profit at the expense of others will find their integrity within themselves. As the "I Ching" says: "Good and evil accumulate, with misfortune and fortune coming in kind." How can one say there is no consequence?[142p]
The practice of medicine, when not profound, leads to premature death.

What people in the world value most is life; the means to sustain life is medicine, and the foundation of medicine lies in its principles. In ancient times, figures like the Yellow Emperor, Qi Bo, Bian Que, and Hua Tuo mastered the art of reviving the dead and restoring consciousness, carrying these skills down through the three dynasties. However, those who study these arts today are often superficial and lack thorough understanding. Consequently, there are those who mislead others. Why is this so? It is because they interpret the yin with yang, and the yang with yin; they push the void with the solid, and measure the solid with the void; they consider the external in relation to the internal, and the internal in relation to the external; they assess urgency with slowness, and slowness with urgency; they advance with a retreating eye, and probe forward with a retreating stance.

They are reckless in diagnosing pulses, shallow in observing symptoms, confused in judging sounds, and hazy in assessing colors. This leads to a reversal of techniques that blind and confuse, misapplying remedies to the deaf and blind. They cut short lives yet to blossom, and extinguish souls that should thrive. Such practitioners send lives into this world burdened and return them to the grave in torment. Indeed, premature death in the world is common. Alas! The purpose of medicine is to save lives; a lack of mastery leads instead to their demise.[143p]
Method of Using Medicines

When it comes to the method of using medicines, one must not rigidly adhere to ancient formulas. Instead, adjustments should be made based on the severity of symptoms, modifying the ingredients according to the varying properties of heat and cold to ensure they align with the principles of treatment. One must not apply recklessly; misguidedly administering pills or powders can, in an instant, harm or endanger life. Is there not a natural order that physicians must heed carefully? Substances like realgar, chaulmoogra, quartz, borax, and cinnabar must not be fire-cooked. Herbs such as Sichuan pepper, aconite, turmeric, southern star, and pinellia should not be used raw; they must be processed diligently according to proper methods for medicines to be effective.

General Discussion

Luo Qianfu stated: A child, during the fifth month, suffered abdominal pain after consuming cold powder. He subsequently purchased Shenqi Pills from a pharmacy, which led to an increase in cold pain around the navel and abdomen, intermittently for seven to eight years without relief. Reflecting on the ancients' saying, "Cold should be treated with heat," one must adhere to established principles; a competent physician cannot abandon their essential guidelines to change methods. The harmful substance was cold, and the medicine used was also cold; heavy cold injures the stomach, which explains the cold pain. The human spleen and stomach prefer warmth and reject cold, particularly for children whose vital energy and blood are still weak and cannot tolerate cold. Thus, the yang energy becomes dormant, and cold toxins linger, remaining unresolved over time. To treat illness, one must first address the root cause, using medicines that harmonize and nourish the vital energy to rectify earlier mistakes; only after one month of such treatment can recovery be expected.[144p]
Alas! When Kang Zi offered medicine, Confucius bowed and accepted it, and due to his lack of understanding, he did not dare to taste it; this is the essence of preserving life. How is it that ordinary people merely sit silently and pulse-check, claiming to know the illness? The pulse is the blood and energy of a person flowing between the meridians; if heat prevails, the pulse is rapid; if cold prevails, the pulse is slow; when it is robust, it indicates strength; when it is weak, it indicates deficiency. As for what is injured, how can one distinguish its form? Physicians must thoroughly examine the source of illness, and the patient must also disclose the source of their ailment. Yet this man's father did not inform the doctor of the illness' source and sought medicine from the marketplace, while the dispenser of medicine also failed to assess the condition, resulting in a sickness lasting seven or eight years, all missing this principle. The real master Sun said: “Inquiring before diagnosis is most accurate.” Dongpo said: “One should just seek to recover from illness and not burden the physician.” The words of these two gentlemen greatly benefit the world.

Entry to the Study of Medicine
【Ming Dynasty – Li Chen】

Standards for Practicing Medicine

The practice of medicine involves human life; it must be genuine and unpretentious, characterized by calmness and constancy, possessing true knowledge of the nuances of Yin and virtue. One cannot recklessly pursue the study of medicine. Once the resolve is established, then one may deliberate on the application of efforts. Every morning, sit quietly facing the diagram of the primordial, studying the "Classic of Filial Piety," "Analects," and "Elementary Learning"; those with substantial resources should also study all the Four Books, the ancient texts of the I Ching, the "Classic of Documents," "Great Plan," "Unfettered," and the "Classic of Yao." Grasp the general meaning without the need for forced memorization.[145p]
Indeed, the practice of medicine originates from Confucianism. Without studying and understanding principles, one remains ignorant and unable to grasp the complexities of change. Every noon, upon entering, one should read aloud from the beginning to the end, methodically, ensuring that no word is missed, as if it flows from the mouth.

If one desires to specialize in minor subjects, it is also essential to study major subjects; if one wishes to focus on surgery, one must not neglect internal medicine. Understanding one requires knowledge of the other; there has never been a case of mastering one while ignoring the other. It is precisely due to the varied levels of depth and familiarity that there are divisions in specialties.

After thorough reading, one should contemplate quietly, investigating the meanings within. If there are doubts or difficulties, one should consult ancient and modern renowned texts to broaden one’s knowledge. Alternatively, seeking advice from virtuous and wise individuals is advisable. Tao Jie'an stated: "Just do not engage in conversation with the ignorant." This is because formulas and remedies do not stray from the “Ben Cao,” “Su,” and “Nan,” as well as works by Zhang, Liu, Li, and Zhu. Even if there are minor treatments and quick methods, they are ultimately not the work of the masters, and one must be cautious to avoid being misled by them.

Once the fundamental texts have been thoroughly comprehended, one can then qualify as a minor physician. With greater dedication to quiet meditation, one should revisit Confucian texts, gaining a basic understanding of the dynamics of yin and yang, learning from both close relations and distant ones, thus preparing to offer assistance effectively in various situations.

Particularly in one’s conduct, it is crucial to establish consistent guidelines. Each dawn, one should purify the heart through quiet meditation and rise early to engage with Confucian texts, in order to refresh the source of one’s heart.

At all times, one should not lose the vitality of early morning, which is wondrous.[146p]
When examining a patient, one must first inquire about the onset of symptoms and the date they began. From head to toe, one should reference the initial signs of typhoid fever, mixed symptoms, and methods of distinguishing internal and external injuries, questioning each detail thoroughly. If the symptoms are severe but clearly categorized, one need not check the pulse and can discuss treatment options. If the symptoms are mild yet not clearly defined, pulse examination is necessary for careful assessment.

For men, the left side is examined first, followed by the right; for women, the right side is examined first, followed by the left, in accordance with the principles of yin and yang. First, assess each channel individually to understand the hidden curves. Then, take a comprehensive look to determine the state of deficiency or excess, life or death. After the diagnosis, any remarks about the patient's condition must be truthful, whether they indicate excess or deficiency, and one should clarify the treatability and difficulty of the case, sharing the details of symptoms to evaluate one’s own state of mind. If there are aspects that were overlooked, they should be openly clarified without embellishment.

It is crucial to remain calm and consider the options carefully, avoiding hasty or intense actions that may lead to fear. When examining women, it is essential to first consult their close relatives about the symptoms, appearance, and dietary habits; then, proceed according to convenience. For severe cases, one may conduct the examination from a bedside screen, while in less severe cases, examine at the door behind a curtain, always ensuring to cover one's hands with a thin gauze.

In cases involving poor families, the physician should provide their own thin gauze.[147p]
Widows and unmarried women should be even more respectful and careful, as this is not a trivial matter. When discussing illness, one must clearly analyze and discern whether it is an internal injury or an external affliction; whether it pertains to miscellaneous diseases, or whether it shows signs of Yin deficiency; whether it is primarily an internal injury with some external influence, or primarily an external affliction with some internal injury. Discussing prescriptions should be based on the pulse, following what has been established and avoiding any secrecy.

According to ancient methods, one should adjust based on the patient's age, the circumstances of their situation, and whether they have taken any medications before. Women experience menstrual cycles and childbirth, while men deal with the labor and leisure of sexual activity.

Although based on ancient wisdom, one should not be bound by it. Understanding the organs and functions leads to certainty and prevents misjudgment. Care must be taken when administering medicine. For any specific condition, designate one herb as the sovereign, another as the minister, and others as assistants.

All treatment should stem from qualified practitioners; certainly, the application and formulation of medicines must align with established practices, especially in urgent situations — how can those suffering from ailments manage this themselves? If someone insists on self-administering remedies, their intentions should be respected, but they must adhere to the classical methods of preparation without risking the efficacy of the medicine through clever tricks. Should the pathological conditions remain somewhat unclear, and if the medicine does not yield strong results, it is advisable to wait quietly and reflect deeply on the underlying causes, then reassess and adjust the prescription; recovery is assured.

Once the illness is treated, that remains the task of the physician. Even while adhering to simplicity, when using this to sustain one’s livelihood, one should not overly demand compensation but should accept what is offered. If a patient is extremely poor and does not provide anything, it particularly demonstrates the physician’s compassion and integrity.[148p]
If a person cannot repay, then heaven will repay them. Thus, with a steadfast heart, is there a technique that is unclear or ineffective? Someone asked about the essence of a single word, and the answer was: merely to not deceive. One who reads medical books but does not go through them from beginning to end, only partially understanding one method or another, is deceiving themselves; one who reads thoroughly but does not comprehend or integrate the knowledge is also deceiving themselves; one who understands but does not rise early to calmly sit and regulate their breath, thinking this suffices for diagnosis, is deceiving themselves; one who diagnoses a pulse but does not accurately convey the truth is deceiving themselves; one who discusses prescriptions and medications carelessly and without detail is deceiving themselves; one who hopes for gain after recovery, yet remains entangled in worldly ways, is deceiving themselves.

It is not the lack of profit that is concerning with a physician, but rather the lack of clarity. Repeatedly using methods that yield results while gaining understanding, yet not compiling this knowledge to repay heaven and earth for the public good, is also deceitful. Deceit clouds one's conscience and causes the medical way to ultimately fail; honesty, on the other hand, allows for the cultivation of one’s moral sense and the flourishing of medicinal practices. The distinction between honesty and deceit is beyond human manipulation.[149p]
Ten Essentials for Medical Practitioners

  1. Maintain a compassionate heart, which is the best advice; generously help the masses, and your kindness will be profound.

  2. Understand Confucian and Daoist principles, as they are treasures in the world of medicine; clarity in reasoning is valuable, and one must study various texts thoroughly.

  3. Differentiate between emotional and physical conditions; when your understanding is clear, even chronic illnesses can be addressed.

  4. Recognize the origins of diseases; only when a doctor can confidently discuss these matters can they truly be considered specialized.

  5. Understand the flow of Qi to comprehend seasonal changes; adapt treatments of warming or cooling according to the time.

  6. Be familiar with the meridians, and correctly identify diseases; like the famous physician Bian Que, have a complete understanding of internal organs.

  7. Know the properties of medicines and tailor prescriptions to diseases; failing to distinguish between warming and cooling might harm patients with specific conditions.

  8. Master the preparation of medicines, paying attention to the correct heat; too much or too little can determine safety and risk.

  9. Do not be jealous or let personal preferences influence you; the principles of nature are evident, so quickly repent of such thoughts.

  10. Do not prioritize profit; instead, uphold righteousness and benevolence; regardless of wealth or poverty, the use of medicine should be equitable.[150p]
    Ten Essentials for the Ill

  11. Choose a capable physician; it benefits the illness. One must be cautious, as life and death are at stake.

  12. Be willing to take medicine; many ailments can be dispelled. There are foolish people who resist treatment themselves.

  13. Seek early treatment; it is easier at the outset. If one is not careful in frosty conditions, hard ice will suddenly arrive.

  14. Avoid empty rooms; naturally, illness will not arise. If one violates this, even the best doctor cannot help.

  15. Avoid anger; self-reflection is necessary. Anger ignites like fire, making rescue difficult.

  16. Cease idle thoughts; one must rest in tranquility. When worries are cleared, the spirit naturally rejuvenates.

  17. Regulate diet and nutrition; there must be principles. Excess damages the spirit; overeating is hard to manage.

  18. Be careful with daily routines and social interactions; even minor labor can deplete vital energy.

  19. Do not trust the misguided; believing them leads to trouble. Heretical notions and deceptions disturb families.

  20. Do not be frugal with expenses; what does saving mean in this context? I ask you, is life or wealth more precious?[151p]
    Doctors and Patients Share Common Ills

  21. In the south, when a person is ill, they often invite a doctor to their home. After the examination, the doctor simply prescribes a remedy and instructs someone to purchase the medicine from the market, without regard for the authenticity or preparation of the medicine, which they then take without question. They do not show reverence, nor do they reflect on their own faults, but only blame the doctor for incompetence. The next day, they simply switch doctors, and this continues for several days, resulting in an increase in symptoms, while the doctors themselves are confused and do not understand the cause. Alas! Is this the fault of the patients? Or the ignorance of the doctors?

  22. In the north, when a person is ill, they invite a doctor to their home, and regardless of the severity of the illness, they hand over a sum of money and request a prescription. They expect immediate results; if not, they quickly seek out another doctor, changing their minds as easily as switching allegiances. They do not realize that each person has their own unique constitution and that illnesses vary in depth and severity.[152p]
    The affliction of cold and its symptoms can be cured with one or two doses; however, as for injuries and ailments from exhaustion, can they be cured with just a dose or two? This is a common misunderstanding, and many are misled by it; only the wise can discern!

Firstly, the path of medicine, which was once called the way of immortals, is fundamentally about preserving life. Many modern physicians do not understand this meaning; they often focus on the wealthy while neglecting the poor. This is a habitual sentiment among doctors and is hardly a compassionate practice. In my view, medicine concerns life and death, and the responsibility is grave. How can one discriminate based on wealth? Those who claim to be my peers should regard the virtue of cherishing life highly and should be careful not to discuss wealth, as all are living beings; this too is a hidden merit.

Secondly, when a patient seeks medical aid, they are entrusting their life and death; thus, they ought to be respected. One should not treat them lightly; wealth should not be a factor in assessing their situation. One ought to sincerely dedicate oneself, for any slight disrespect would imply a disregard for life itself.[153p]
Moreover, there are even more despicable traitors, who, having the power of medicine, upon recovering from illness, seek financial gain with false claims of blessings received yesterday. They exchange medicine for another’s wealth, and do not give credit to anyone. Alas! They cannot reap benefits nor gain reputation; such individuals are truly devoid of compassion.

Firstly, it is often seen in contemporary times that when people seek medical treatment, they are instructed to lie in dark rooms behind curtains, without being informed of their afflictions. They simply allow pulse-taking, and many women are not even seen. How can they perceive their own condition? Furthermore, they cover their hands with silk cloths, making it inconvenient for the physician to inquire. Even if they do ask, the patients do not disclose the truth; this is not a desire for recovery but rather a means of complicating the treatment.[154p]
Little do they know that even the divine physicians of old relied on observing, listening, asking, and palpating; none of these four can be neglected. How can today's doctors, who may not be as remarkable as those of the past, expect to thoroughly comprehend the viscera by merely feeling the pulse? I write this to inform patients of the world: when a doctor is called to your home, explain your ailments in detail. If the physician then examines your pulse and understands your condition without doubt, the prescribed medicine will surely be effective.

In the past, Dongpo said: "I seek to cure my illness; how can I be a burden to the doctor?"

Firstly, among those in my field, there are individuals without virtue who only boast of their own strengths and expose the weaknesses of others. When they visit a patient, they do not inquire about the illness but instead criticize the previous doctor's mistakes to frighten the patient. Even if the previous physician's medicines were entirely appropriate, what more could one seek? Just because there may have been a temporary oversight that failed to produce results, one cannot generalize that the previous medicine was inept. Medicine is fundamentally a compassionate practice, and since sharing and passing down knowledge is part of this continuum, colleagues must protect and support one another despite minor differences. They should avoid slandering each other, as this reflects a lack of loyalty and integrity. Beware, beware![155p]
Five Precepts and Ten Essentials of Medical Practitioners

  1. First Precept: Regardless of the patient's size, wealth, or status, if someone seeks your help, do not delay or dismiss them. If you wish to go but do not, it is not easy; do not consider the cost of medicine lightly. You should do your best with each case, and naturally, your reputation will grow, without harming your conscience.

  2. Second Precept: When examining women, widows, or nuns, always wait for an attendant to be present before entering the room for diagnosis. If there is no companion, do not examine them alone. If there are inconvenient issues at hand, you should earnestly observe, but even to close relatives, do not speak of it, due to the nature of the domestic environment.

  3. Third Precept: Do not accept precious gifts or items from patients as a means of barter. If it is truly necessary, have them prepare it themselves. If the treatment does not work, there will be no suspicion of blame. Moreover, do not praise the quality of their possessions; such behavior is unworthy of a gentleman.

  4. Fourth Precept: As someone who saves lives, do not engage in leisure activities like climbing mountains, carrying wine, or traveling for pleasure. Additionally, do not leave your home at inappropriate times.[156p]
    Whenever someone arrives with an illness, one must personally examine them, carefully prepare the medicine, and write the prescription according to the texts. One must not fabricate prescriptions or be subject to questioning by others.

The five prohibitions: As for courtesans and private practitioners requesting to see patients, one should treat them as one would the children of respectable families, refraining from inappropriate remarks to derive improper benefits, and upon completing the consultation, they should leave. For the impoverished, the cost of medicine may be returned, but one should only dispense medicine and not allow further visits, to avoid temptations of immorality.

First imperative: One must first understand Confucian principles to then grasp medical theories, whether internal or external. It is essential to diligently read the ancient texts of distinguished medical theories, never letting go of them day or night, and to consult each one thoroughly, absorbing the intricate changes, imprinting them in the heart, and discerning them with the eyes so that one will not err when diagnosing patients.
