When faced with ailments such as sores or dysentery, which are foul and unbearable to look at—things that are detestable to others—the reaction should be shame and compassion, without the slightest thought of personal gain; this is my aspiration.

For a great physician, one must achieve a tranquil mind, able to introspect, and look upon the situation with a serene demeanor, expansive and free, not shining bright nor drearily dim, thoroughly inspecting symptoms and illnesses, with deep intention and careful observation, missing no detail. In administering treatment, one must not be haphazard. While it is said that ailments should be quickly addressed, one must not be confused in the process. It is essential to examine the situation carefully and reflect deeply, without hastily seeking reputation at the expense of life, for that would be lacking in benevolence. Additionally, when visiting a patient’s home, regardless of the lavish decor one should not look around indiscriminately; even if musical instruments play nearby, one must not appear entertained; even if delicacies are offered, the food should taste bland; fine wines served should seem as if they were nonexistent.[39p]

所以尔者，夫一人向隅，满堂不乐，而况病人苦楚，不离斯须，而医者安然欢娱，傲然自得，兹乃人神之所共耻，至人之所不为，斯盖医之本意也。夫为医之法，不得多语调笑，谈谑喧哗，道说是非，议论人物，衒耀声名，訾毁诸医，自矜己德。偶然治瘥一病，则昂头戴面，而有自许之貌，谓天下无双，此医人之膏肓也。老君曰：人行阳德[阳德　原作阴德，据《千金》卷一序例改。]，天自报之；人行阴德，鬼神害之。寻此二途，阴阳报施，岂诬也哉？所以医人不得恃己所长，专心经略财物，但作救苦之心，于冥冥道中，自感多福者耳。又不得以彼富贵，处以珍贵之药，令彼难求。自衒功能，谅非忠恕之道。志存救济，故亦曲碎论之。学者不可耻言之鄙俚也！[40p]

Thus it is, when one person is in distress, the whole place is filled with sorrow. How much more so is the pain of the sick, who cannot be separated from their suffering, while a physician remains carefree and proud, indulging in joy. This is a shared shame among humans and gods, something that the truly wise would never do—this is the essence of the healer's intention.

As for the practice of medicine, one must not speak too much, jest, chatter, argue about right and wrong, discuss people, flaunt one’s fame, disparage other doctors, or boast of one's own virtue. If by chance one cures a disease, one should not hold one’s head high with an air of superiority, believing oneself to be unparalleled. This is the foolishness of physicians.

Laozi said: "Those who act with virtue in the open are rewarded by heaven; those who act with virtue in secret are harmed by spirits." Following these two paths, the responses of yin and yang are surely not misleading. Therefore, a physician must not rely on their own strengths or focus solely on material gain, but instead cultivate a heart for alleviating suffering, and in the depths of their path, they will naturally feel abundant blessings.

Moreover, one should not treat the wealthy with rare and precious medicines, making it difficult for the poor to obtain them. Flaunting one’s skills is certainly not the way of loyalty and compassion. The intention to provide relief should be at the forefront, hence one must be flexible and considerate in discussions. Scholars should not be ashamed to express humble words![40p]

　　　　　论治病略例

　　夫天布五行以植万类，人禀五常以为五脏，经络腑腧，阴阳会通，元冥幽微，变化难极。《易》曰：非天下之至赜，其孰能与于此？观今之医，不念思求经旨以演其所知，各承家伎，始终循旧，省病问疾，务在口给，相对斯须，便处汤药，按寸不及尺，握手不及足，人迎趺阳，三部不参，动数发息，不满五十，短期未知决诊，九候曾无仿髴，明堂阙庭，尽不见察，所谓窥管而已。夫欲视死别生，固亦难矣。此皆医之深戒，病者可不谨以察之而自防虑也？古来医人皆相嫉害，扁鹊为秦太医令李谥所害，即其事也。一医处方，不得使别医和合，脱或私加毒药，令人增疾，渐以致困。如此者非一，特须慎之。[41p]

Discussion on Treatment of Illness: Brief Example

Heaven distributes the five elements to sustain all beings; humanity embodies the five constants to form the five organs. The channels and collaterals, yin and yang, interconnect; the original, the obscure, and the subtle are ever-changing and difficult to grasp. The Book of Changes states: "If it is not the utmost mystery of the world, who can engage with it?" Observing today's physicians, they do not contemplate and seek the essence of the classics to expand their understanding. Rather, each follows their traditional skills, clinging to old methods from beginning to end. When diagnosing illnesses or asking about symptoms, they focus on verbal responses, relying only on brief interactions. They apply remedies without thorough examination, taking superficial measurements that do not capture the whole condition; they check the pulse without understanding its depth, dismiss the body's currents, and disregard important signs. Often, they do not reach fifty proper assessments, and are unaware of crucial diagnoses, lacking a comprehensive view, merely peering through a tube. The challenge of distinguishing life from death is indeed difficult. Should not patients be vigilant about recognizing these shortcomings in their physicians to guard against negligence? Throughout history, physicians have harbored jealousy and malice towards one another; the case of Bian Que, who was harmed by Li Shizhen, the chief physician of the Qin dynasty, exemplifies this. When one physician prescribes, they must not allow another physician to collaborate improperly, risking the addition of harmful substances that would exacerbate the illness, leading to suffering. Such instances are not uncommon; hence, caution is essential.[41p]

宁可不服其药以任天真，不得使愚医相嫉，贼人性命，甚可哀伤。　　凡医诊候，固是不易，又问而知之，别病深浅，名曰巧医。仲景曰：凡欲和汤合药针灸之法，宜应精思。必通十二经脉，辨三百六十五孔穴，荣卫气行，知病所在，宜治之法，不可不通。古者上医相色。色脉与形，不得相失。黑乘赤者死，赤乘青者生。中医听声，声合五音。火闻水声，烦闷善惊；木闻金声，恐畏相刑。脾者土也，生育万物，回助四傍，善者不见，死则归之，太过则四肢不举，不及则九窍不通，六识闭塞，犹如醉人。四季运转，终而复始。下医诊脉，知病源由，流转移动，四时逆顺，相害相生，审知脏腑之微，此乃为妙也。[42p]

It is better to not take medicine and accept the natural order than to have foolish doctors compete, endangering lives—this is indeed tragic. Diagnosing patients is not easy; knowing the conditions through inquiry distinguishes the skilled doctors. Zhang Zhongjing said: "Anyone wishing to harmonize decoctions, herbs, and acupuncture techniques must engage in deep thought. One must understand the twelve meridians, identify the three hundred sixty-five acupoints, recognize the flow of vital energy, and discern the location of diseases—the methods of treatment must be understood." In ancient times, the highest physicians observed colors. The colors, pulses, and forms must not be disconnected. When black dominates red, it leads to death; when red dominates blue, it leads to life. Chinese medicine considers sounds, correlating them to the five tones. Fire perceives water’s sound, leading to anxiety and fright; wood perceives metal’s sound, leading to fear of punishment. The spleen represents earth; it nurtures all things, supports all around. In favorable conditions, it is unseen; in death, it reverts to it. Excessive activation leads to immobility of limbs, while deficiency leads to blocked orifices and closed senses, akin to a person inebriated. The four seasons circulate, ending and beginning anew. Lower physicians diagnose pulses and understand disease origins, observing the flows and shifts through the seasons, knowing their interrelations—this is the essence of mastery.[42p]

　　　　　论诊侯

　　古之善为医者，上医医国，中医医人，下医医病。又曰：上医听声，中医察色，下医诊脉。又曰：上医医未病之病，中医医欲病之病，下医医已病之病。若不加心用意，于事混淆，即病者难以救矣。愚医不通三部九候及四时之经，或用汤药倒错，针灸失度，顺方治病，更增他疾，遂致灭亡。哀哉烝民，枉死者半，可谓世无良医，为其解释经说，地水火风，和合成人。然愚医不思脉道，反治其病，使脏中五行，共相克切，如火炽燃，重加其油，不可不慎。凡四气合德，四神安和。一气不调，百病一生；四神动作，四百四病，同时俱发。又云：一百一病，不治自愈；一百一病，须治而愈；一百一病，虽治难愈；一百一病，真死不治。[43p]

On Diagnosing Illness

In ancient times, those skilled in medicine were categorized as follows: the highest physicians treat the state, the middle physicians treat the people, and the lowest physicians treat diseases. Furthermore, it is said that the highest physicians listen to sounds, the middle physicians observe colors, and the lowest physicians diagnose pulses. Additionally, the highest physicians treat diseases that have yet to emerge, the middle physicians treat diseases that are desired to emerge, and the lowest physicians treat diseases that are already present. If one does not carefully apply themselves and allow confusion to arise, it becomes difficult to save the patient.

Foolish physicians do not understand the three divisions, nine pulses, and the principles of the four seasons. They may incorrectly use decoctions or medicines and misapply acupuncture, leading to more ailments, resulting in their demise. Alas, the masses suffer, and half die unjustly; it can be said that there are no good physicians in the world. These practitioners fail to explain the classics regarding the interplay of earth, water, fire, and wind, which together create human life. However, foolish physicians do not consider the pulse pathways and incorrectly treat ailments, causing the internal organs and their five elements to conflict with one another, like pouring oil on a raging fire—this cannot be taken lightly.

In all matters, the four Qi must harmonize, and the four spirits must be at peace. If one Qi is out of balance, a hundred diseases arise; if the four spirits are disturbed, four hundred and four diseases emerge simultaneously. It is also said: one hundred diseases may heal on their own without treatment; one hundred diseases require treatment to heal; one hundred diseases may be difficult to cure even with treatment; one hundred diseases will lead to true death if untreated.[43p]

　　　　东坡杂记 【宋　苏轼】

　　　　　求医诊脉

　　脉之难明，古今所病也。至虚有实候，而大实有羸状，差之毫厘疑似之间，便有死生祸福之异，此古今所病也。病不可不谒医，而医之明脉者，天下盖一二数。骐骥不时有，天下未尝徒行；和扁不世出，病者未尝徒死，亦因其长而护其短耳。土大夫多秘所患而求诊，以验医之能否，使索病于冥漠之中，辨虚实伶热于疑似之间，医不幸而失，终不肯自谓失也，则巧饰遂非，以全其名；至于不救，则曰，是固难治也。间有谨愿者，虽或因主人之言，亦复参以所见，两存而杂治，以故药不效，此世之通患而莫之悟也。吾平生求医，盖于平时默验其工拙，至于有疾而求疗，必先尽告以所患，而后求诊。使医者了然知患之所在也，然后求之诊，虚实冷热，先定于中，则脉之疑似，不能惑也。故虽中医，治吾疾常愈。吾求疾愈而已，岂以困医为事哉？[44p]

Notes from Dongpo by Su Shi (Song Dynasty)

Regarding seeking medical treatment and pulse diagnosis

The difficulty in reading pulses has been a longstanding issue through the ages. When the pulse is thin yet has substantial indicators, and when it shows strong signs but is frail, even a slight difference can make the distinction between life and death, fortune and misfortune. This has been a problem throughout history. One cannot avoid consulting a physician, yet truly skilled practitioners are very few. Exceptional horses are rare indeed, as the world has never seen one that did not need to be ridden; likewise, outstanding physicians do not appear often, and patients do not die in vain, as it is due to their strengths that they guard against their weaknesses. Many local doctors hold secret knowledge of the ailments and seek consultations to test the abilities of the physicians, thereby making the diagnosis a wandering search through uncertainty, distinguishing the real from the false amidst suspicion. If the physician unfortunately fails, they still do not acknowledge their failure, resorting to clever embellishments to preserve their reputation; yet, if no recovery is achieved, they say it was indeed difficult to treat. Occasionally, some are diligent and wish to improve, but even if they refer to the words of the patient, they still mix in their own observations, balancing both, leading to ineffective remedies; this is a universal problem of our time that remains unrecognized. Throughout my life, when I sought medical help, I generally observed their skill or lack thereof during normal times. When I did fall ill and sought treatment, I would first fully disclose my ailments, then request a diagnosis. By letting the physician clearly understand the source of the problem first, the matters of deficiency and excess, heat and cold, would be determined beforehand, hence the doubts of the pulse could not confuse. Therefore, even with traditional medicine, my ailments always improved. My only goal was to recover from my illness; I did not seek to trouble the physician.[44p]

　　　　　医者以意用药

　　欧阳文忠公尝言，有患疾者，医问其得疾之由，曰：乘船遇风，惊而得之。医取多年柂牙为柂工手汗所渍处割末，杂丹砂、茯神之流，饮之而愈。今《本草注》引《药性论》云：止汗用麻黄根节，及古竹扇为末服之。文忠因言医以煮用药多此。比初似儿戏，然或有验，殆未易致诘也。予因谓：公以笔墨烧灰饮学者，当治昏惰耶？推此面广之，则饮伯夷之盥水可以疗贪，食比干之馂余可以已佞，舐樊哙之盾可以治怯，齅西子之珥可以疗恶矣。公遂大笑。元佑三年闰八月十七日，舟行入颍州界，坐念二十年前，见文忠公于此，偶记一时谈笑之语，聊复识之。[45p]

The physician prescribes medicine based on intention.

Ouyang Wenzhong once remarked that a patient, when asked about the cause of his illness, said: "I was on a boat and encountered strong winds, which startled me and led to my condition." The physician then used a long-standing remedy: he took a tooth from a long-standing tree, cut it where it was moist with sweat from the boatman’s hands, mixed it with cinnabar and substances like Poria, and the patient recovered after drinking it. Now, according to the "Pharmaceutical Notes," citing the "Discussion on Medicinal Properties," it states that to stop sweating, one should take the root of Ephedra and grind it with ancient bamboo leaves to consume. Wenzhong noted that physicians often rely heavily on cooking and using medicines in this manner. Initially, it seemed trivial like child’s play; however, it sometimes proved effective, though it is difficult to challenge that assertion. I then said: "If you nourish scholars with ink and burnt ash, should they not be cured of laziness? Broadening this perspective, one could say that drinking the washing water of Boyi can cure greed, eating the remnants of Bigan's dish can end flattery, licking the shield of Fan Kuai can heal cowardice, and touching the earrings of Xi Shi can alleviate wickedness." He then laughed heartily. On the seventeenth day of the eighth month in the third year of the Yuanyou era, while sailing into the boundary of Yingzhou, I sat and recalled twenty years ago when I saw Wenzhong here, and casually recorded this moment of conversation and laughter.[45p]

　　　　避暑录话 【宋　叶梦得】

　　　　　士大夫不可轻信医

　　士大夫于天下事，苟聪明自信，无不可为，惟医不可强。本朝公卿能医者，高文庄一人而已，尤长于伤寒。其所从得者，不可知矣。而孙兆、杜壬之徒，始闻其绪余，尤足名一世。文庄，郓州人。至今郓多医，尤工伤寒，皆本高氏。余崇宁大观间在京师，见董汲、刘寅辈皆精晓张仲景方术，试之数验，非江淮以来俗工可比也。子瞻在黄州，蕲州医庞安常亦善医伤寒，得仲景意。[46p]

Record of Escaping the Heat
(Song Dynasty, Ye Mengde)

The Scholar-Official Should Not Trust Physicians Lightly

In matters of the world, if the scholar-official is smart and self-assured, there is nothing they cannot accomplish, yet they should not be overly reliant on physicians. In our dynasty, among the high-ranking officials, only Gao Wenzhuang can be considered adept in medicine, particularly in treating fevers. The knowledge he possesses is unknown to us. However, individuals like Sun Zhao and Du Ren have gained some recognition, which is sufficient to mark them in history. Wenzhuang is from Yungzhou, and to this day, Yung has many physicians, especially skilled in treating fevers, all stemming from the Gao lineage. During the Chongning and Daguan eras, while I was in the capital, I encountered Dong Ji, Liu Yin, and others who were well-versed in Zhang Zhongjing’s medical arts; their skills were unmatched by the conventional practitioners since the Jianghuai region. Zi Zhan, residing in Huangzhou, noted that the physician Pang Anchang from Qizhou was also adept in treating fevers and had grasped the essence of Zhongjing’s methods.[46p]

蜀人巢谷出圣散子方，初不见于前世医书，自言得之于异人，凡伤寒不问证候如何，一以是治之，无不愈。子瞻奇之，为作序，比之孙思邈三建散，虽安常不敢非也，乃附其所著《伤寒论》中，天下信以为然。疾之毫厘不可差，无甚于伤寒，用药一失共度，则立死者皆是，安有不问证候，而可用者乎？宣和后，此药盛行于京师，太学诸生信之尤笃，杀人无数。今医者悟，始废不用。巢谷本任侠好奇，从陕西将韩存宝出入兵间，不得志，客黄州，子瞻以故与之游，子瞻以谷奇侠而取其方，天下以子瞻文章而信其言。事本不相因，而趋名者又至于忘性命而试其药，人之惑盖有至是也。[47p]

In Shu, there was a figure named Chao Gu who developed a medicinal powder called Sheng San Zi Fang, claiming to have learned it from a mysterious person, without prior mention in the medical texts of past eras. He asserted that it could treat any case of typhus, regardless of symptoms, and it was said to cure all ailments. Zizhan was intrigued by this and wrote a preface, comparing it to Sun Simiao’s San Jiansan, though he did not dare to criticize it too harshly. He included it in his work "Shanghan Lun," and it gained widespread acceptance throughout the land.

However, the treatment was extremely precise; even the slightest error could lead to death in cases of typhus. If the medicine was misused, it would result in many deaths. How could one use such a remedy without regard for symptoms? After the Xuán Hé era, this medicine became popular in the capital, with students from the Taixue trusting it deeply, leading to countless fatalities.

Eventually, medical practitioners realized the truth and began to abandon its use. Chao Gu was originally a chivalrous person with a curiosity for the unusual, having come from Shaanxi and associated with Han Cunbao in the military, though he did not achieve success. He became a guest in Huangzhou, where Zizhan befriended him due to his adventurous spirit and sought his recipe. The world trusted Zizhan's words due to his writings.

The situation was fundamentally unrelated, yet those chasing fame forgot their own safety and recklessly tried the medicine; such confusion among people is truly remarkable.[47p]

　　　　　集方书

　　士大夫固不可轻言医，然人疾苟无大故，贫不可得药，能各随其证而施之，亦不无小补。盖疾虽未必死，无药不能速愈。呻吟无聊者固可悯，其不幸迟延，苟变而生他证，因以致死者多矣。方其急时，有以济之，虽谓之起死可也。今列郡每夏岁支系省钱二百千，合药散军民，韩魏公为谏官时所请也。为郡者，类不经意，多为庸医盗其直，或有药而不及贫下人。余在许昌，岁适多疾，使有司修故事，而前五岁皆忘不及举，可以知其怠也。[48p]

Collection of Prescriptions

The scholar-officials should not lightly speak of medicine; however, when people are ill without serious ailments, and the poor cannot obtain medicine, if each can follow their symptoms and apply remedies, it is not without minor benefits. Although an illness may not necessarily lead to death, without medicine quick recovery is impossible. Those who moan aimlessly indeed deserve pity; their misfortune may delay recovery, and many have died from the transformation into other ailments. In urgent times, if there are means to assist, it can be said to bring one back from death. Now, in each district, every summer, a budget of two hundred thousand is allocated to supply medicine for the civilians and soldiers, as requested by Lord Han Wei when he was an advisor. Those in charge of the districts often neglect their duties, frequently letting incompetent physicians steal their salaries, or they may have medicine yet fail to reach the impoverished. When I was in Xuchang, there were indeed many illnesses annually, yet the officials neglected to uphold previous practices, with the last five years being completely forgotten, which shows their negligence.[48p]

遂并出千缗市药材京师，余亲督众医分治，率幕官轮日给散，盖不以为非职而责之。人人皆喜从事，此何惮而不为乎？自余居此山，常欲岁以私钱百千，行之于一乡，患无人主其事，余力不能自为，每求僧或净人中一二成余志未能也。今年，余家婢多疾，视药囊常试有验者，审其证用之，十人而十愈。终幸推此以及邻里乎？陆宣公在忠州集古方书五十篇。史云：避谤不著书，故事尔。避谤不著书可也，何用集方书哉？或曰：忠州近边裔，多瘴疠，宣公多疾，盖将以自治。尤非也！宣公岂以一己为休戚者乎？是殆援人于疾苦死亡而不得者，犹欲以是见之，在他人不可知，若宣公此志必矣。[49p]

He then gathered a thousand strings of cash to market herbal medicine in the capital, while I personally supervised the physicians in their treatments, arranging for attendants to provide daily distributions. I did not consider this beyond my duties, nor did I reproach them. Everyone was eager to participate; what hesitation could there be in this?

Since I have been residing in this mountain, I have often wished to use private funds amounting to hundreds or thousands to help a village, but I have faced the problem of lacking someone to oversee the work, and my own efforts have not been sufficient. I have sought monks or pure individuals to collaborate, yet they have not been able to fulfill my intentions.

This year, many of my servants fell ill, and I consistently examined the medicinal pouch for effective remedies, rigorously confirming their applications, achieving a success rate of ten out of ten. Ultimately, can I hope to extend this goodwill to the neighbors?

Lord Lu Xuan collected fifty ancient remedy texts in Zhongzhou. Historical accounts suggest that he refrained from writing to avoid slander, which is understandable. However, what need is there to compile remedy texts to avoid slander? Some say that Zhongzhou is near areas prone to miasma, and Lord Xuan has suffered many ailments, intending to manage his own health. This is not the case! Would Lord Xuan prioritize his own wellbeing above others’? He is indeed assisting those in suffering and in danger of death, yet he seeks to do so anonymously, where others may not recognize it; surely, if his intentions are indeed as stated.[49p]

古之名医扁鹊和缓之术，世不得知，自张仲景、华陀、胡洽、深师、徐彦伯有名一世者，其方术皆医之六经，其传有至于今，皆后之好事者纂集之功也。孙真人为《千金方》两部，说者谓凡修道养生者，必以阴功协济而后可得成仙。思邈为千金前方时，已百余岁，固已妙尽古今方书之要，独伤寒未之尽，似未尽通仲景之言，故不敢深论。后三十年作《千金翼》，论伤寒者居半，盖始得之，其用志精审不苟如此。今通天下言医者，皆以二书为司命也。思邈之为神仙，固无可疑。[50p]

In ancient times, the famous physician Bian Que employed gentle techniques that the world did not know. Since Zhang Zhongjing, Hua Tuo, Hu Qia, Shen Shi, and Xu Yanbo, those who became renowned in their time all utilized the six classic medical texts. Their teachings have been passed down to the present day through the efforts of later scholars who compiled them. Sun Zhenren authored the "Thousand Gold Prescriptions" in two volumes, and it is said that anyone who cultivates and nurtures life must first harmonize their efforts with Yin practices to achieve immortality. As for Sun Simiao, when he composed the front part of the "Thousand Gold" at over a hundred years old, he had already mastered the essentials of ancient and modern prescription texts, yet he felt he hadn’t fully explored the topic of cold damage, seeming to lack a comprehensive understanding of Zhongjing’s words, so he did not dare to discuss it deeply. Thirty years later, he wrote the "Thousand Gold Supplements," dedicating half of it to cold damage, indicating that he had begun to grasp it, and his meticulous commitment was evident in this work. Today, those who speak of medicine in the world regard these two books as essential guides. There is no doubt about Sun Simiao's status as an immortal.[50p]

然唐人尤记中间有用蝱虫、水蛭之类，诸生物命，不得升举，天之恶杀物者如是，则欲活人者，岂不知之？况宣公之志乎！　　古方施之富贵人多验，贫下人多不验；俗方施之贫下人多验，富贵人多不验。吾始疑之，乃卒然而悟曰：富贵人平日自护持甚谨，其疾致之必有渐，发于中而见于外，非以古方术求之，不能尽得。贫下人骤得于寒暑燥湿饥饱劳逸之间者，未必皆真疾，不待深求其故，苟一物相对，皆可为也，而古方节度，或与之不相契。今小人无知，所疾苟无大故，但意所习熟，知某疾服某药，得百钱鬻之市，人无不愈者。设与之以非其所知，盖有疑而不肯服者矣。况古方分剂汤液，与今多不同，四方药物所产，及人之禀赋亦异。《素问》有为异法力宜立论者、言一病治各不同而皆愈。即此理推之，以俗方治庸俗人，亦不可尽废也。[51p]

Indeed, the Tang people especially remember that there are those who use insects and leeches; among all living beings, their lives are not to be elevated. If the heavens treat living things in such a cruel manner, how could they be unaware of the desire to preserve human life? What of the aspirations of Duke Xuan!

The ancient methods were often successful for the wealthy, but many of the poor and lowly did not experience such outcomes; conversely, the folk remedies were often effective for the poor, while the wealthy found little success. I initially doubted this but eventually realized: wealthy people typically safeguard themselves carefully; when they fall ill, their ailments must develop gradually, arising from within before manifesting outward, and cannot be entirely resolved by the ancient methods alone.

The poor, however, quickly acquire sickness from fluctuations in temperature, humidity, hunger, fatigue, and rest. These are not necessarily genuine illnesses, and there is no need to deeply investigate the causes; so long as there is something present, anything can be treated, while the ancient methods and strict protocols may not align with them.

Today, the ignorant commoners, encountering ailments without significant cause, simply rely on familiar remedies, knowing that if they have a certain illness, they should take a particular medicine, and there’s hardly anyone who does not recover after spending a hundred coins at the market. If presented with something unfamiliar, they will likely hesitate and refuse to take it. Additionally, the ancient methods of dividing decoctions and liquids are considerably different from the present ones; the herbs from different regions vary, and so do people’s innate qualities.

The "Suwen" states that there should be distinct principles for varied treatments, emphasizing that each illness requires a different approach for a cure. Based on this reasoning, even if we treat ordinary people with folk remedies, we should not completely dismiss them.[51p]

　　　　玉涧杂书 【宋　叶梦得】

　　　　　医不能起死人

　　华陀固神医也，然范晔陈寿记其治疾，皆言若发结于内，针药所不能及者，乃先令以酒服麻沸散，既醉无所觉，因刳破腹背，抽割积聚；若在肠胃，则断裂湔洗，除去疾秽，既而缝合，敷以神膏，四五日创愈，一月之间皆平复。此决无之理。人之所以为人者以形，而形之所以生者以气也。佗之药能使人醉无所觉，可以受其刳割，与能完养使毁者复合，则吾所不能知。[52p]

The Jade Stream Miscellany
Song Dynasty - Ye Mengde

A Physician Cannot Revive the Dead

Hua Tuo was indeed a divine physician, yet both Fan Ye and Chen Shou recorded that when treating illnesses, they spoke of conditions that were internally lodged and beyond the reach of needles and medicines. First, he would administer麻沸散 (a type of anesthetic) in wine, causing the patient to become drunk and unaware, after which he would cut open the abdomen and back to remove accumulations. If the issue was in the intestines or stomach, he would carefully dissect and cleanse, removing impurities, and then stitch up the wounds, applying a divine ointment. In four to five days, the wounds would heal, and within a month, the patient would be restored. This is indeed a matter of decisive action. What makes a person a person is their form, and what gives rise to that form is vital energy. The ability of certain medicines to cause a person to be oblivious to surgery and yet allow for restoration is something I cannot comprehend.[52p]

然腹背肠胃，既已破裂断坏，则气何由舍？安有如是而复生者乎？审佗能此，则凡受支解之刑者，皆可使生，王者之刑，亦无所复施矣。太史公扁鹊传记虢庶子之论，以为治病不以汤液醴酒，镵石挢引，而割皮解肌，抉脉结筋，湔浣肠胃，漱涤五脏者，言古俞跗有是术耳，非谓扁鹊能之也，而世遂以附会于佗。凡人寿夭死生，岂一医工所能增损？不幸疾未必死，而为庸医所杀者，或有之矣。未有不可为之疾而医可活也。方书之设，本以备可治之疾，使无至于伤人而已。扁鹊亦自言越人非能生死人也，彼当生者，越人能起之耳。故人与其因循疾病而受欺于庸医好奇无验之害，不若稍知治身，摄生于安乐无事之时，以自养其天年也。[53p]

If the abdomen and internal organs are already ruptured and damaged, how can the vital energy be restored? Is there any possibility of regeneration in such a case? If one could do this, then all who suffer corporal punishment could be made to live again, and the punishments of kings would have no further application.

The historian Sima Qian, in his account of Bian Que, discusses the notion that treating illness should not rely on medicinal liquids, terrain techniques, incisions, or carving the flesh. He refers to the ancient methods of treatment, not suggesting that Bian Que possessed such arts, but rather that the world has misconstrued this. The lifespan of every person, whether long or short, cannot hinge on the skill of a single physician. It may be unfortunate for one to fall ill and escape death, yet still be harmed by incompetent doctors. There is no malady that a physician cannot address to restore life when there is potential for recovery.

The texts on medicine are meant to address treatable ailments and prevent harm. Bian Que himself stated that a skilled physician can aid those who are naturally destined to live, implying that they can merely help that which is meant to survive. Therefore, instead of relying on foolish doctors who thrive on dubious curiosities and untested methods, one would be better served to gain knowledge of self-care and nurture their health during a peaceful and carefree time to prolong their natural life.[53p]

　　　　梦溪笔谈 【宋　沈括】

　　　　　用药不可不慎

　　医之为术，苟非得之于心，而恃书以为用者，未见能臻其妙。如朮能动锺乳，按乳石论曰：服锺乳，当终身忌朮。五百诸散，用锺乳为主，复用朮，理极相反，不知何谓！予以问老医，皆莫能言其义。按乳石论云：石柱虽温而体本沉重，必待其相蒸薄然后发。如此则服石多者，势自能相蒸；若更以药触之，其发必甚。五石散杂以众药，用石殊少，势不能蒸，须借外物激之令发耳。如火少必因风气所鼓而后发，火盛则鼓之反为害，此自然之理也。故孙思邈云：五石散大猛毒，宁食野葛，不服五石，遇此方即须焚之，勿为含生之害。又曰：人不服石，庶事不佳，石在身中，万事休泰。[54p]

Dream Brook Talks
Song Dynasty - Shen Kuo

Caution in Using Medicine

In the art of medicine, if one does not understand it from the heart and relies solely on books, they are unlikely to attain its wonderful subtleties. For example, the herb Zhu can affect the essence of limestone; according to the theory of limestone, if one consumes limestone, they must avoid Zhu for life. The various formulas that include limestone as the main ingredient but also contain Zhu are fundamentally contradictory, and I do not understand what this means! I have asked experienced doctors, yet none could explain its significance. The theory of limestone states that although the stone pillars are warm, their substance is inherently heavy and can only become effective after being steamed and thinned out. Therefore, if one consumes a large quantity of the stone, it tends to steam itself; but if additional medicine is used to touch it, the effect will certainly be intensified. Just as a small fire must be fanned by the wind to ignite, a strong fire can be harmed by excessive fanning; this is a principle of nature. Hence, Sun Simiao stated: "The five stone powder is highly toxic; it is better to eat wild kudzu than to use the five stones. When encountering this formula, one must burn it to avoid the dangers of consumption." He also said: "People should not take stones; otherwise, all affairs will be unwell; with stones in the body, all things will be futile."[54p]

唯不可服五石散，盖以五石散聚其所恶，激而用之，其发暴故也。古人处方，大体如此，非此书所能尽也。况方书仍多伪杂，如《神农本草》最为旧书，其间差讹尤多，医不可不知也。　　予一族子旧服芎藭，医郑叔熊见之云：芎藭不可久服，多令人暴死。后族子果无疾而卒。又予姻家朝十张子通之妻，因病脑风服芎藭甚久，亦一旦暴亡，皆予目见者。又予尝苦腰重，久坐则弛，距十余步然后能行，有一将佐见予曰：得无用苦参洁齿否？予时以病齿，用苦参数年矣。曰：此病由也。苦参入齿，其气伤肾，能使人腰重。后有太常少卿舒昭亮用苦参揩齿，岁久亦病腰。自后悉不用苦参，腰疾皆愈。此皆方书旧不载者。[55p]

One should not use Wushi San, as it gathers what is detrimental and when provoked can lead to violent effects. The ancient physicians had a general principle like this, which cannot be fully detailed in this book. Furthermore, prescription books often contain many forgeries and mixed content. For instance, the "Shennong Bencao" is one of the oldest texts, yet it contains numerous errors that physicians must be aware of.

I had a relative who used to take Chuanxiong; a doctor named Zheng Shuxiong saw him and said that Chuanxiong should not be taken for a long time, as it can cause sudden death. Eventually, my relative, though he had no illness, passed away. Similarly, my in-law, the wife of Zhang Zitong, took Chuanxiong for a long time due to a disease called "wind in the brain," and also died unexpectedly; I witnessed both cases.

I once suffered from heavy loins; I would feel relaxed after sitting for a long time but could only walk after covering a distance of over ten steps. An assistant I had asked me, "Could it be that you are using bitter bean for cleaning your teeth?" At that time, I had used bitter bean for my dental issues for years. He said, "That is the cause of your ailment." Using bitter bean on the teeth affects the kidneys and can lead to heavy loins. Later, there was a minor official named Shu Zhaoliang who also used bitter bean for his teeth. After many years, he too suffered from lumbar issues. After that, I completely stopped using bitter bean, and my back pain was cured. These are all matters not recorded in ancient prescriptions.[55p]

　　　　省心录 【宋　林逋】

　　　　　论医

　　无恒德者，不可以作医，人命死生之系。庸人假医以自诬，其初则要厚利，虚实补泻，未必适当，幸而不死，则呼需百出，病者甘心以足其欲；不幸而毙，则曰饮食不知禁嗜，欲有所违，非药之过也。厚载而出，死者何辜焉！世无扁鹊望而知死生，无华佗涤肠以愈疾，轻以性命托庸医，何如谨致疾之因，固养生之本，以全天年耶？呜呼悲夫！[56p]

On Medicine

One without constant virtue cannot practice medicine; human life hangs in the balance of life and death. A mediocre person masquerades as a physician to deceive themselves, initially seeking great profit. Their treatments, whether adding or removing remedies, may not be appropriate. Fortunately, if the patient does not die, they will falsely claim satisfaction to fulfill their desires; if alas, the patient dies, they blame it on the indulgence in food and drink, asserting the patient disregarded prohibitions, claiming it is not the fault of the medicine. Burdened by their actions, how can the dead be innocent? In this world, there are no skilled doctors like Bian Que who can foresee life and death, nor Huatuo who can cleanse ailments. How foolish it is to entrust life to a mediocre physician! Is it not better to scrutinize the causes of illness and diligently nurture the foundations of health throughout the year? Alas, how tragic![56p]

　　　　外科精要 【宋　陈自明】

　　　　　论医者更易良方

　　李氏云：尝见世间医者，每有妙方秘而不传，或更改以惑人，诚可恶也。余思西华麦饭石膏，守死不传，其立心私刻，君子责之矣。昔赵公宣教字季修，来宰龙泉，兼程而进，患鼻衄，日出数升，时家兄教以服藕汁、地黄膏。赵叩诸医，一云：此为戒服之剂。乃数易医无效。家兄阴馈前汁，服三日而疾愈。兄曰：此印前所献之方。赵惊叹曰：向非医者谲计以惑我，早信此方，岂久受此困耶？今以屡试屡验，不可易者。如因热毒冲心而呕，用内托散，一名万金散。因脾胃虚弱而呕，嘉朮散。热毒肿痛，麦饭石膏。[57p]

Essentials of Surgery
(Song Dynasty, Chen Ziming)

On Physicians with Better Remedies

Li said: I have often seen physicians in the world who possess excellent remedies but keep them secret or alter them to confuse others, which is truly contemptible. I ponder the case of Xihua Maimen Stone Gypsum, which is guarded and not passed down; such intentions are selfish, and a gentleman would be accountable for this. In the past, Zhao Gongxuan taught Ji Xiu, who came to preside over Longquan. He hurriedly advanced but suffered from a nosebleed, losing several sheng daily. At that time, his elder brother advised him to take lotus root juice and Rehmannia paste. Zhao consulted various physicians, and one said: This is a cautionary remedy. He changed doctors several times, all to no avail. His elder brother secretly provided the juice, and after three days of taking it, he recovered. His brother said: This is the formula presented earlier. Zhao was astonished and said: If it were not for the deceptive tactics of non-medical people misleading me, had I trusted this remedy sooner, would I have endured this suffering for so long? Now, I have tested and verified repeatedly—there are things that should not be exchanged. For instance, if vomiting stems from heat toxins affecting the heart, one should use Neituo San, also known as Wanjin San. If vomiting arises from weak spleen and stomach, one should use Jiazhu San. For heat toxins causing swelling and pain, Maimen Stone Gypsum should be used.[57p]

阴虚作渴，加减八味丸。此四方屡为医人眩惑，使人勿用，深悯患者之受苦，故举赵公之事以例之。　　薛己曰：愚窃以为方者仿也，仿病因以立方，非谓《内经》无方也。若执古方以治今疾，犹拆旧宇以对新宇，其长短大小，岂有舍匠氏之手而能合者乎？设或有合，以为　古不易之方，此又先王普济之神术，奚必秘而私之耶？余尝观太无先生治滇南一僧，远游江浙，思亲成疾，先生惠之以饮食药饵，复赠金一镒，以资其归，此固我医道之当然也。今之医者，或泥古，或吝秘，或嗜利以惑人，其得罪于名教多矣。[58p]

Yin deficiency causes thirst; the modified Eight Flavor Pills should be used. This situation has repeatedly confused physicians in the past, leading them to advise against its use, deeply sympathizing with the suffering of patients. Thus, I cite the example of Duke Zhao.

Xue Ji said: I foolishly believe that formulas are derived from imitation; they are formulated based on the causes of diseases. It is not that there are no formulas in the "Inner Canon." If one insists on using ancient formulas to treat contemporary illnesses, it is akin to dismantling an old house to match a new one. The dimensions and specifications differ; how could one expect to have a good fit without the craftsmanship of a skilled builder? Even if there is a match, considering it an unchanging ancient formula is misguided; this was the universal divine skill of the former kings. Why must it be kept secret and private?

I once observed Mr. Tai Wu treat a monk from Yunnan who, after traveling far to Jiangsu and Zhejiang, fell ill from longing for his family. The master generously provided him with food and herbal medicine, along with a gold coin to support his return. This is indeed the proper way of my medical practice. Yet, today's physicians may cling to the ancients, be secretive, or be obsessed with profit to mislead people; they have greatly violated the principles of knowledge and ethics.[58p]

古今图书集成医部全录卷五百二

总论

　　　　珍珠囊指掌 【元　李杲】

　　　　　用药法

　　夫用药之法，贵乎明变，如风会有古今之异，地气有南北之分，天时有寒暑之更，禀赋有厚薄之别，受病有新旧之差，年寿有老少之殊，居养有贵贱之辨。用药之际，勿好奇，勿执一，勿轻妄，勿迅速，须慎重精详，圆融活变，不妨沉会，以期必妥，药于是乎功成。惜先贤未有发明，后学因而弗讲，其悞世也，不既多乎！　　夫病有宜补，以泻之之道补之。病有宜泻，以补之之道泻之。病有宜寒剂者，以热剂为向导之兵。病有宜热剂者，以寒剂为类从之引。病在上者治下，病在下者治上。病同也而药异，病异也而药同。其义至微，学者最宜深究。　　用药之忌，在乎欲速。欲速则寒热温凉行散补泻，未免过当，功未获奏，害已随之。夫药无次序，如兵无纪律，虽有勇将，通以勇而偾事。又如理丝，缓则可清其绪，急则愈坚其结矣。[59p]

Volume 500 of the Complete Collection of Ancient and Modern Books in the Medical Section

General Discussion

On the Use of Medicine (by Li Gao from the Yuan Dynasty)

The method of using medicine emphasizes clarity and flexibility. Just as the wind may differ from ancient to modern times, the qualities of the land vary between the north and south, the timing of weather transforms with seasons, one’s constitution can be thick or thin, the nature of illnesses can be new or old, age distinctions exist between the young and the old, and social status defines the rich and the poor. When administering medicine, one should not be overly curious, nor should one cling to singular notions, be reckless, or act hastily. Caution and precision are essential; one must be adaptable and flexible, allowing for deep contemplation to ensure effectiveness. It is a lament that earlier sages did not articulate these principles, leaving later scholars ill-informed, thus leading to widespread confusion.

Illnesses may require tonics, which should be addressed with purging methods. Conversely, some illnesses may need purging, which must then be treated with tonics. If a condition calls for cold medicine, one should initiate treatment with heat; if warmth is needed, cold medicines should lead the way. Conditions located above should be treated from below, while those below should be treated from above. An illness may manifest in similar ways yet require different medicines, or it may present uniquely while responding to the same treatment. The subtleties are numerous, and scholars would do well to investigate deeply.

The primary prohibition in using medicine lies in the desire for speed. Aiming for rapid results can lead to improper application of cold, heat, warmth, and coolness in treatment, risking adverse outcomes without achieving any benefits. Medicine must be administered in proper order, just as soldiers must follow discipline. Even the bravest generals achieve success through proper strategies. It is also like untangling threads; a gentle approach can clear the knots, while haste only tightens them further.[59p]

　　　　此事难知 【元　王好古】

　　 　　　医之可法

　　自伏羲神农黄帝而下，名医虽多，所可学者有几人哉？至于华氏之剖腹，王氏之灸针，术非不神也，后人安得而效之？非若岐伯之圣经，雷公之炮炙，伊贽之汤液，箕子之《洪范》，越人之问难，仲景之伤寒，叔和之《脉诀》，士安之《甲乙》，启元子之传注，钱仲阳之论议，皆其活法，所可学者，岂千方万论印定后人眼目者，所能比哉？其间德高行远，奇才异士，与夫居缙绅隐草莽者，虽有一法一节之可观，非百代可行之活法，皆所不取也。岂予好辨哉？欲使学者观此数圣贤，而知所向慕而已。或有人焉，徒能广览泛涉，自以为多学，而用之无益者，岂为知本？[60p]

This matter is hard to comprehend.

Since the time of Fuxi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor, although there have been many famous doctors, how many can truly be learned from? Consider the abdominal surgeries by the Hua family and the acupuncture of the Wang family; their techniques are indeed remarkable, but how can later generations emulate them? They are not like the sacred classics of Qibo, the fire cautery of Lei Gong, the herbal formulas of Yi Zhi, the "Hong Fan" of Jizi, the challenging questions from the Yue people, the "Shang Han" of Zhang Zhongjing, the "Pulse Diagnosis" of Shu He, the "Jia Yi" of Shi An, the annotations of Qiyuanzi, or the discussions of Qian Zhongyang. All these represent live practices that can be learned. Can extensive discussions and numerous formulas that merely validate what later generations see ever compare? Among them, those who are virtuous and accomplished, extraordinary talents and individuals, along with those who exist in high positions yet remain humble in obscurity, while they may have observable methods or principles, none give rise to lasting practices that can endure for centuries. Is it that I wish to quibble? No, I only wish for learners to look up to these sages and know whom to aspire to. Yet, there are those who, though they can extensively peruse and dabble everywhere, mistakenly believe themselves knowledgeable without utility—are they truly grasping the essence?[60p]

　　　　卫生宝鉴 【元　罗天益】

　　 　　　福医治病

　　丙辰秋，楚丘县贾君次子二十七岁，病四肢困倦，躁热自汗，气短，饮食减少，咳嗽痰涎，胸膈不利，大便秘，形容嬴削，一岁间更数医不愈。或曰：明医不如福医。某处某医虽不精方书，不明脉候，看证极多，治无不效，人目之曰福医。谚云：饶你读得王叔和，不如我见病证多。颇有可信，试命治之。医至，诊其脉曰：此病予饱谙矣，治之必效。于肺腧各灸三七壮，以蠲饮枳实丸消痰导滞，不数服，大便溏泄无度，加腹痛，食不进，愈添困笃。其子谓父曰：病久瘦弱，不任其药。病剧遂卒。冬予从军回，其父以告予，予曰：思《内经》云：形气不足，病气不足，此阴阳俱不足，泻之则重不足。此阴阳俱竭，血气皆尽，五脏空虚，饬骨髓枯，老者绝灭，壮者不复矣。故曰不足补之，此其理也。令嗣久病羸瘦，乃形不足；气短促，乃气不足；躁作时嗜卧，四肢困倦，懒言语，乃气血皆不足也。[61p]

Sanitary Treasure: Medical Treatment by Fortune
(Yuan Dynasty, Luo Tianyi)

In the autumn of the year Bingchen, a twenty-seven-year-old son of Mr. Jia from Chuqiu County fell ill with exhaustion in all four limbs, excessive heat and sweating, shortness of breath, decreased appetite, coughing with phlegm, discomfort in the chest, constipation, and a gaunt appearance. Over one year, he consulted several doctors without recovery. Some said: "The skilled doctor is not as good as the lucky doctor." Although a certain doctor may not be proficient in prescriptions or aware of pulse conditions, he has treated numerous conditions with success, earning him the title of "Fortune Doctor."

There is a saying: "Even if you can read Wang Shuhe's texts, it’s not as good as my experience with many cases." This is rather credible, so I decided to try him. The doctor arrived, examined his pulse, and said: "I am very familiar with this illness; treatment will surely be effective." He applied moxibustion on the points of the lungs three times seven, and used a formula of Pinellia and bitter orange to resolve phlegm and eliminate stagnation. After a few doses, the stool became excessively loose, causing abdominal pain and loss of appetite, which further escalated the weakness.

The son told his father that he had been ill for a long time and was unable to tolerate the medicine. The illness worsened, and he eventually died. During the winter, upon returning from military service, his father informed me, and I said: “The Inner Canon states: when the form and energy are insufficient, the disease's energy is also lacking; both yin and yang are deficient, and purging will exacerbate this insufficiency. When both yin and yang are depleted, the blood and energy are all exhausted, the five organs are empty, the bone marrow is dried out; the elderly will perish, and the young will find no recovery. Thus, it is said that insufficiency must be replenished; this is the principle.

Furthermore, a prolonged illness resulting in emaciation indicates a deficiency in form; shortness of breath signifies a lack of energy; feelings of restlessness and an urge to lie down, along with languid limbs and a tendency to avoid speech, indicate that both energy and blood are deficient.[61p]

补之惟恐不及，反以小毒之剂泻之，虚之愈虚，损之又损，不死何待？贾君叹息而去。予感其事，略陈其理。夫高医愈疾，先审岁时太过不及之运，察人之血气衣食勇怯之殊，病有虚实浅深在经在脏之别，药有君臣佐使大小奇偶之制，治有缓急因用引用反正之别。孙真人云：凡为大医，必须谙《甲乙》、《素问》、《黄帝针经》、《明堂流注》、十二经、三部九候、五脏六腑、表里孔穴、本草药性、仲景、叔和诸部经方，又须妙解五行阴阳，精熟《周易》，如此方可为大医。[62p]

To supplement is to fear it may not be enough; instead, a small toxic remedy is used to purge, which makes the deficiency even more profound, weakening further. If not dead, what awaits? Duke Jia sighed and left. I sensed the matter and briefly explained its reasoning. A great physician, in curing illness, must first examine the seasonal excesses or deficiencies affecting the patient's condition, observe differences in their blood, energy, diet, and temperament. Diseases may vary in vacuity or repletion, their profundity, depending on the meridians or organs involved; medicines have hierarchies among sovereign, minister, assistant, and envoy, with variations in size and peculiarities. Treatments vary as to urgency and the principles of application and correction. Master Sun said: In all matters of great medicine, one must be well-versed in "Jia Yi", the "Suwen", the "Huangdi Neijing", "Mingtang Liu Zhu", the twelve meridians, the three divisions and nine indications, the five viscera and six organs, the surface and interior points, the nature of medicinal substances, the doctrines of Zhang Zhongjing and Sun Simiao, and also profoundly understand the five elements and yin-yang theory; mastery of the "Zhouyi" is essential. Only thus can one be considered a great physician.[62p]

不尔则无目夜游，动致颠陨。正五音者，必取师旷之律吕，而后五音得以正；为方圆者，必取公输之规矩，而后方圆得以成。五音方圆特末技耳，尚取精于其事者，况医者人之司命，列于四科，非五音方圆之比。不精于医，不通于脉，不观诸经方本草，乃以命通运达而号为福医，病家遂委命于庸人之手，岂不痛哉？噫！医者之福，福于渠者也。渠之福，安能消病者之患焉？世人不明此理，而委命于福医，至于伤生丧命，终不能悟，此惑之甚者也，悲夫！[63p]

If not, then there are no eyes to guide one through the night, leading to stumbling and falling. To correctly establish the five musical tones, one must consult the laws and principles of Shi Kuang; only then can the five tones be made accurate. To determine shapes and dimensions, one must take the standards of Gongshu; only then can shapes and dimensions be achieved. The five tones and shapes are merely minor skills; hence, one should seek those who are proficient in the craft. How much more is this true for physicians, the custodians of human lives, categorized into four specialties—far beyond the comparison of the five tones and shapes? If one is not skilled in medicine, not familiar with pulses, not observant of the various classics, prescriptions, and herbal texts, yet claims to reach life and death matters by mere luck and is labeled as a fortunate physician, then patients entrust their lives to incompetent hands—how painful that is! Alas! The fortune of the physician is but a fortune of the fortunate. How can this fortune alleviate the sufferings of the sick? People in the world fail to understand this principle and entrust their lives to fortunate physicians, leading to injuries and loss of life, and in the end, they cannot comprehend; such is the greatest confusion—how tragic![63p]

　　　　丹溪心法 【元　朱震亨】

　　　　　不治已病治未病论

　　与其救疗于有疾之后，不若摄养于无疾之先，盖疾成而后药者，徒劳而已。是故已病而后治，所以为医家之法；未病而先治，所以明摄生之理。夫如是则思患而预防之者，何患之有哉？此圣人不治已病，治未病之意也。尝谓备土以防水也，苟不以闭塞其涓涓之流，则滔天之势不能遏。备水以防火也，若不以扑灭其荧荧之光，则燎原之焰不能止。其水火既盛，尚不能止遏，况病之已成，岂能治欤？故宜夜卧早起于发陈之春，早起夜卧于蕃秀之夏，以之缓形无怒而遂其志，以之食凉食寒而养其阳，圣人春夏治未病者如此。[64p]

Danxi Heart Method
(Yuan Dynasty, Zhu Zhenheng)

On Treating the Ill Before They Fall Ill

Rather than trying to cure illness after it has occurred, it is better to nurture health before illness arises. Indeed, to administer medicine only after the onset of disease is futile. Thus, the practice of treating illness after it has developed is the way of doctors, while preventing illness before it arises is the essence of the principles of health preservation. If one anticipates and prevents troubles, what troubles could there be? This is the intent of the wise, who do not treat existing illness but address the unseen. For instance, one prepares earth to prevent water; if one does not block the trickling flow, the flood cannot be contained. One prepares water to combat fire; if one does not extinguish the flickering flame, the raging fire cannot be stopped. Since it is difficult to contain a surge of water or fire, how much more so for a disease already established? Therefore, one should go to bed early and rise early in the spring to prevent illness, and similarly, early rising and late resting in summer promotes health. These routines calms the body and tempers anger, enabling the fulfillment of one's ambitions, while embracing cooling and light foods to nourish vitality. Thus act the wise in the spring and summer, treating illness before it arises.[64p]

与鸡俱兴于平明之秋，必待日光于闭藏之冬，以之敛神匿志而私其意，以之食温食热而养其阴，圣人秋冬治未病者如此。或曰：见肝之病，先实其脾之虚，则木邪不能传；见右颊之赤，先泻其肺经之热，则金邪不能盛，此乃治未病之法。今以顺四时调养神志而为治未病者，是何意耶？盖保身长全者，所以为圣人之道；治病十全者，所以为上工术。不治已病治未病之说，若于四时调神大论，厥有旨哉！昔黄帝与天师难疑答问之书，未尝不以摄养为先，始论乎天真，次论乎调神，既以法于阴阳而继之以调于四时，既曰饮食有节而又继之以起居有常，谆谆然以养生为急务者，意欲治未然之病，无使至于已病难图也。厥后秦缓达乎此见，晋侯病在膏肓，语之曰：不可为也。扁鹊明乎此，视齐侯病至骨髓，断之曰：不可救也，噫！惜齐晋之侯，不知治未病之理。[65p]

In the bright autumn, one must wait for sunlight in the hidden winter to gather the spirit, conceal aspirations, and keep one's intentions private; to nourish the yin with warming and hot food. This is how the sage treats illness before it arises in autumn and winter. Some say: to address a liver ailment, one must first strengthen the spleen; only then can the wood pathogen not spread. To address redness in the right cheek, one must first drain the heat from the lung meridian; only then can the metal pathogen not become dominant. This is indeed the method for preventing illness.

Now, to harmoniously adjust the spirit according to the four seasons as a means of preventing illness, what might this mean? To preserve one’s health and longevity is the path of the sage; to treat illnesses thoroughly is the skill of the superior practitioner. The discourse on treating illness only after it has occurred, in relation to the adjustment of the spirit in different seasons, carries a profound intention! In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor and the Heavenly Master had extensive exchanges where they never neglected nourishment; they first discussed innate nature, then the adjustment of spirit, applying principles of yin and yang followed by seasonal adjustments. They emphasized moderation in diet and regularity in daily activities, earnestly prioritizing health care, intending to prevent potential illnesses rather than react to severe diseases that are hard to remedy.

Later, the Qin state was slow to understand this principle. When the Duke of Jin experienced a serious ailment in the diaphragm, he was told: It cannot be treated. Bian Que understood this well; he diagnosed the Duke of Qi’s illness, which had reached the marrow, and declared: It cannot be saved. Alas! It is regrettable that the lords of Qi and Jin did not grasp the principles of preventing illness.[65p]

　　　　　审察病机无失气宜论

　　邪气各有所属也，常穷其要于前；治法各有所归也，当防其差于后。盖治病之要，以穷其所属为先，苟不知法之所归，未免于无差尔。是故疾病之生，不胜其众，要其所属，不出乎五运六气而已。诚能于此审察而得其机要，然后为之治，又必使之各应于运气之宜，而不致有一毫差悞之失，若然，则治病求属之道，庶乎其无愧矣！至真要大论曰：审察病机，无失气宜，意蕴诸此。尝谓医道有一言而可以尽其要者，运气是也。天为阳，地为阴。[66p]

Examining the Pathology of Disease Without Losing the Correspondence to Qi

Evil Qi has its own specific origins, often tracing back to its essentials; therapeutic methods also have their corresponding conclusions, which should be preventively handled. The essence of treating disease lies in thoroughly understanding its sources first; if one does not comprehend the direction of the therapeutic principles, one is likely to err. Therefore, the emergence of disease, due to its multitude, does not transcend the Five Movements and Six Qi. If one can carefully examine and discern its critical points, then apply treatment accordingly, ensuring each aligns with the appropriateness of the Qi and seasons, one will not have even the slightest oversight. If so, the path to treating illness and finding its origins will be commendable. The Treatise on the True Essentials states: "Examine the pathology of disease without losing the correspondence to Qi," implying deep meaning. It is often said that the essence of medical practice can be summed up in one phrase: it is the movements and Qi. Heaven represents Yang, while Earth represents Yin.[66p]

阴阳二气，各分三品，谓之三阴三阳。然天非纯阳，而亦有三阴；地非纯阴，而亦有三阳。故天地上下，各有风热火湿燥寒之六气，其斡旋运动乎两间者，而又有木火金水之五运。人生其中，脏腑气穴，亦与天地相为流通。是知众疾之作，而所属之机无出乎是也。然而医之为治，当何如哉？惟当察乎此，使无失其宜而后可。若夫诸风掉眩，皆属肝木；诸痛疮痒，皆属心火；诸湿肿满，皆属脾土；诸气膹郁，皆属肺金；诸寒收引，皆属肾水，此病机属于五运者也。[67p]

The two forces of Yin and Yang are each divided into three categories, known as the Three Yin and Three Yang. However, the heavens are not purely Yang; they also have Three Yin, while the earth is not purely Yin; it also has Three Yang. Therefore, both heaven and earth, above and below, have the six forces of wind, heat, fire, dampness, dryness, and cold that circulate and move between them, together with the five elements of wood, fire, metal, and water. Human beings exist within this, and the organs and energy points flow in correspondence with heaven and earth. Thus, one realizes that the origin of various diseases stems from this mechanism. However, how should medicine treat these conditions? One must carefully observe these phenomena to ensure nothing is overlooked before proceeding. For instance, all dizziness caused by wind relates to the liver and wood; all pains, ulcers, and itching pertain to the heart and fire; all swelling and fullness due to dampness relate to the spleen and earth; all stagnation of qi concerns the lungs and metal; and all contraction and withdrawal due to cold pertains to the kidneys and water—these disease mechanisms belong to the five elements.[67p]

诸暴强直，皆属于风；诸呕吐酸，皆属于热；诸躁越狂扰，皆属于火；诸痓强直，皆属于湿；诸濇枯涸，皆属于燥；诸病水液澄澈清冷，皆属于寒，此病机属于六气者也。夫惟病机之察，虽曰既审，而治病之施，亦不可不详。故必别阴阳于疑似之间，辨标本于隐微之际。有无之殊者，求其有无之所以殊；虚实之异者，责其虚实之所以异。为汗吐下，投其所当投；寒热温凉，用其所当用。或逆之以制其微，或从之以导其甚。上焉以远司气之犯，中焉以辨岁运之化，下焉以审南北之宜。使大小适中，先后合度，以是为治。[68p]

All violent and rigid conditions belong to wind; all vomiting and sourness belong to heat; all restlessness and agitation belong to fire; all convulsions and rigidity belong to dampness; all dryness and desiccation belong to dryness; all diseases characterized by clear and cool water fluids belong to cold. This discussion of the pathological mechanisms relates to the six qi. The investigation of these mechanisms, while appearing to be thorough, cannot overlook the details in treating diseases. Therefore, it is essential to differentiate between yin and yang amidst confusion, and to discern the root and branches in subtle situations. For those who differ in existence and non-existence, seek the reasons for their differences; for those who differ in deficiency and excess, identify the reasons for their variations. Use sweat-inducing, vomiting, or purging methods according to appropriateness; apply hot, cold, warm, or cool treatments as required. Some may be countered to control subtle manifestations, while others follow to guide the more severe cases. Address higher matters by addressing the offenders of the qi from afar, tackle middle matters by discerning the transformations of seasonal changes, and handle lower matters by assessing southern and northern appropriateness. Ensure that larger and smaller matters are balanced, and that actions are timely and proportional; this is the basis of treatment.[68p]

又岂有差殊乖乱之失耶？又考之《内经》曰：治病必求其本。《本草》曰：欲疗病者，先察病机。此审病机之意也。六元正纪大论曰：无失天信，无逆气宜。五常大论曰：必先岁气，无伐天和。此皆无失气宜之意也。故《素问》、《灵枢》之经，未尝不以气运为言。既曰：先立其年，以明其气；复有以戒之曰：治病者必明天道地理阴阳更胜。既曰：不知年之所加，气之盛衰，虚实之所起，不可以为工矣。谆谆然若有不能自已者，是岂圣人私忧过计哉？以医道之要，悉在乎此也。观乎《原病式》一书，比类物象，深明乎气运造化之妙，其于病机气宜之理，不可以有加矣。[69p]

Is it not the case that there are significant failures in deviation and disorder? Furthermore, examining the "Inner Canon," it states: to treat illness, one must seek its root. The "Herbal Classic" states: to cure a sickness, one must first understand the mechanism of the illness. This is the essence of understanding the illness mechanism. The "Great Treatise on the Six Primary Elements" posits: do not disregard natural signs, and do not oppose vital energies. The "Great Treatise on the Five Constant Elements" states: one must first consider seasonal energies and not disrupt the harmony of nature. All of this reflects the importance of understanding vital energies. Thus, the texts of the "Plain Questions" and "Spiritual Pivot" consistently emphasize the significance of vital energies. It has been stated: first determine the year to clarify its energies; and there is also a caution that those treating illness must understand heavenly principles, geographical factors, and the dynamics of yin and yang. Another statement asserts: without knowledge of the year’s progression, the rise and fall of energies, and the origin of deficiency and excess, one cannot be effective as a practitioner. These warnings are earnest, as though the sages had an unmanageable concern, illustrating that the essence of medical practice rests completely in these principles. Observing the "Treatise on Origination of Illness," which categorizes and compares phenomena, reveals a profound understanding of the transformation of vital energies. This understanding is crucial for grasping the principles of illness mechanisms and vital energies.[69p]

　　　　　能合色脉可以万全论

　　欲知其内者，当以观乎外；诊于外者，斯以知其内。盖有诸内者形诸外。苟不以相参，而断其病邪之逆顺，不可得也。为工者，深烛厥理，故望共五色以青黄赤白黑，以合五脏之脉，穷其应与不应；切其五脉急大缓啬沉，以合其五脏之色，顺与不顺。诚能察其精微之色，诊其微妙之脉，内外相参而治之，则万举万全之功，可坐而致矣。《素问》曰：能合色脉，可以万全。其意如此。原夫道之一气，判而为阴阳，散而为五行，而人之所禀皆备焉。夫五脉者人之真，行血气，通阴阳，以荣于身；五色者气之华，应五行，合四时，以彰于面。[70p]

The ability to harmonize color and pulse enables complete understanding.

To know what is within, one should observe the outside; diagnosis from the outer reveals the inner. What exists within manifests outwardly. If one does not correlate these aspects, understanding the nature of disease—whether it is progressing or regressing—cannot be achieved. A skilled practitioner illuminates the principles deeply, thus looking at the five colors: blue, yellow, red, white, and black, to relate them to the pulses of the five organs, exploring their responses and lack thereof; feeling the urgency, size, slackness, and depth of the five pulses, to associate them with the colors of the organs, determining alignment or misalignment. If one can truly perceive the subtle colors, diagnose the delicate pulses, and correlate the inner and outer aspects for treatment, then success in all endeavors can be achieved effortlessly. The "Suwen" states: the ability to harmonize color and pulse can lead to complete success. This is its meaning.

The essence of the Dao is a unified qi, divided into yin and yang, dispersed into the five elements, all of which are possessed by humans. The five pulses are true to a person, circulating blood and qi, connecting yin and yang, nourishing the body; the five colors are the expressions of qi, corresponding to the five elements, aligning with the four seasons, illuminating the face.[70p]

惟其察色按脉而不偏废，然后察病之机，断之以寒热，归之以脏腑，随证而疗之，而获全济之效者，本于能合色脉而已。假令肝色如翠羽之青，其脉当微弦而急，所以为生；若浮濇而短，色见如草滋者，岂能生乎？心色如鸡冠之赤，其脉当浮大而散，所以为顺；若沉濡而滑，色见如衃血者，岂能顺乎？脾色如蟹腹之黄，其脉当中缓而大，所以为从；若微弦而急，色见如枳实者，岂能从乎？肺色如豕膏之白，其脉当浮濇而短，所以为吉；若浮大而散，色见如枯骨者，岂能吉乎？以至肾色见如乌羽之黑，其脉沉濡而滑，所以为生；或脉来缓而大，色见如煤者死。[71p]

Only by carefully observing colors and feeling pulses without neglecting either can one discern the mechanisms of illness, determine them with respect to cold and heat, attribute them to the organs, and treat them according to the symptoms, thereby achieving complete healing. For instance, if the liver color is as green as emerald feathers, its pulse should be slightly taut and rapid, which indicates vitality; if it is floating, weak, and short, with a color resembling withered grass, how could it thrive? If the heart color is as red as a chicken's comb, its pulse should be floating, large, and scattered, indicating harmony; if it is heavy, moist, and slippery, with a color like dark blood, how could it be harmonious? If the spleen color resembles the yellow of a crab’s belly, its pulse should be moderate, steady, and large, indicating compliance; if it is slightly taut and rapid, with a color like bitter orange, how could it comply? If the lung color is as white as pig fat, its pulse should be floating, weak, and short, indicating fortune; if it is floating, large, and scattered, with a color like dried bones, how could it be fortunate? Lastly, with the kidney color appearing as dark as crow feathers, its pulse should be heavy, moist, and slippery, indicating vitality; if the pulse is slow and large, with a color like coal, it indicates death.[71p]

死生之理，夫惟诊视相参，既以如此，则药证相对，厥疾弗瘳者，未之有也。抑尝论之：容色所见，左右上下各有其部；脉息所动，寸关尺中皆有其位。左颊者，肝之部以合左手关位，肝胆之分，应于风木，为初之气。额为心之部以合于左手寸口，心与小肠之分，应于君火，为二之气。鼻为脾之部，合于右手关脉，脾胃之分，应于湿土，为四之气。右颊肺之部，合于右手寸口，肺与大肠之分，应于燥金，为五之气。颐为肾之部，以合于左手尺中，肾与膀胱之分，应于寒水，为终之气。至于相火，为三之气，应于右手命门三焦之分也。[72p]

The principle of life and death relies on mutual examination. Given this, when the medicine is applied, it corresponds to the ailment, and there will be no illness left untreated. Moreover, it has been discussed: the observable hues of the face correspond to specific areas; the pulse and breath have their respective positions within the inch, bar, and foot. The left cheek corresponds to the liver, aligned with the left wrist position, representing the wind wood and the initial qi. The forehead corresponds to the heart, aligned with the left wrist’s inch, representing the heart and small intestine, associated with the emperor fire and the second qi. The nose corresponds to the spleen, aligned with the right wrist pulse, representing the spleen and stomach, related to the damp earth and the fourth qi. The right cheek corresponds to the lungs, aligned with the right wrist inch, representing the lungs and large intestine, connected to the dry metal and the fifth qi. The jaw corresponds to the kidneys, aligned with the left wrist’s bar, representing the kidneys and bladder, associated with the cold water and the final qi. As for the fire of the heart, it corresponds to the third qi, which relates to the right wrist's mingmen and triple burner.[72p]

若夫阴阳五行相生相胜之理，当以合之于色脉而推之也。是故脉要精微论曰：色合五行，脉合阴阳。十三难曰：色之与脉，当参相应。然而治病万全之功，苟非合于色脉者，莫之能也。五脏生成篇云：心之合脉也，其荣色也。夫脉之大小滑濇沉浮可以指别，五色微诊可以目察，继之以能合色脉，可以万全。谓夫赤脉之至也喘而坚，白脉之至也喘而浮，青脉之至也长而左右弹，黄脉之至也大而虚，黑脉之至也上坚而大。此先言五色，次言五脉，欲后之学者，望而切之以相合也。厥后扁鹊明乎此，述之曰：望而知之谓之神，切而知之谓之巧，得《内经》之理也。下逮后世，有立方者，目之曰神巧万全，厥有旨哉！[73p]

The principle of the interaction of Yin, Yang, and the Five Elements should be harmonized with color and pulse. Thus, the "Essentials of Pulse Diagnosis" states: Colors correspond to the Five Elements, and pulses correspond to Yin and Yang. The "Thirteen Difficulties" states: Colors and pulses should correspond to each other. However, the complete practice of medicine cannot be achieved without this harmony of color and pulse. The "Treatise on the Generation of the Five Organs" says: The heart corresponds to the pulse, which reflects its color. The characteristics of the pulse—its size, texture, sinking or floating—can be differentiated, while the subtle diagnoses of the Five Colors can be observed. Following this, the ability to harmonize color and pulse can lead to comprehensive healing. Thus, the red pulse is characterized by being urgent and strong, the white pulse by being urgent and floating, the green pulse by being long and oscillating left and right, the yellow pulse by being large and weak, and the black pulse by being firm and large. This initially discusses the Five Colors, then the Five Pulses, intending for future scholars to focus on their harmonization. Later, Bian Que articulated this clearly, stating: To observe and know is called 'spirit,' to feel and know is called 'skill,' which is the essence of the "Inner Canon." In later generations, practitioners have established methods, calling it 'spiritual skill and comprehensive perfection.' How profound that is![73p]

　　　　汤液本草 【元　王好古】

　　　　　察病轻重

　　凡欲疗病，先察其源，先候其机，五脏未虚，六腑未竭，血脉未乱，精神未散，服药必效。若病已成，可得半愈；病势已过，命将难存。自非明医，听声察色，至于诊脉，孰能知未病之病乎？[74p]

Tangye Ben Cao (by Wang Haogu in the Yuan Dynasty)

Observing the Severity of Illness

In any case of wanting to treat illness, one must first investigate its source and gauge its timing. If the five organs are not depleted, the six bowels are not exhausted, the blood vessels are not chaotic, and the spirit is not dispersed, then taking medicine will surely be effective. If the illness has already developed, one may achieve partial recovery; if the condition has progressed too far, survival will be difficult. Unless one is a skilled physician who can discern through sound and color, and even pulse diagnosis, who can truly understand the illness that has not yet manifested?[74p]

　　　　　医之可法

　　钱氏、《活人》、王朝奉、王德孚所论皆宋人，易老、守真皆金人，所用之剂，寒热之不同者，盖本诸此。读此数书而用之，亦当以地方世代所宜责之。然莫若取法于洁古，折衷于仲景汤液，则万世不易之大法也。
　　　　医门群经辨论【明　吕复】

　　　　　古方论

　　《内经》、《素问》世称黄帝岐伯问答之书，及观其旨意，殆非一时之言，其所撰述，亦非一人之手。刘向指为诸韩公子所著，程子谓出于战国之末，而其大略，正如《礼记》之萃于汉儒，而与孔子、子思之言并传也。盖灵兰秘典、五常政、六元正纪等篇，无非阐明阴阳五行生制之理，配象合德，实切于人身，其诸色脉病名，针砭治要，皆推是理以广之。而皇甫谧之《甲乙》、杨上善之《太素》，亦皆本之于此，而微有异同。医家之大纲要法，无越是书矣。[75p]

On Medical Methods

The discussions by the Qian family, as well as "Living People," Wang Chaofeng, and Wang Defu, all originate from the Song dynasty, while Yi Lao and Shouzhen come from the Jin dynasty. The various medicines they used, differing in warmth and coolness, primarily derive from these sources. When reading these several books and applying their principles, one must also consider the appropriateness for the locality and the generations. However, nothing is better than taking principles from the pure ancients and synthesizing them with the formulas of Zhongjing, for this constitutes the unchanging great law for all ages.

On Ancient Formulas

The "Inner Canon" and "Questions of the Simple" are reputedly the dialogues between the Yellow Emperor and Qi Bo. Observing their meanings, they are clearly not the words of just one time, nor solely the work of one person. Liu Xiang attributed them to the sons of the various Han lords, while Chengzi said they originated at the end of the Warring States period. Their general ideas are akin to how "The Book of Rites" was compiled by Han scholars and transmitted along with the words of Confucius and Zisi. The "Secret of the Linglan," "Principles of the Five Constants," and "Chronicle of the Six Origins," among other texts, merely elucidate the principles of yin, yang, and the five elements that govern existence, aligning symbols with virtues. They are directly applicable to the human body. The various colors, pulses, diseases, names, acupuncture points, and treatments all expand upon these principles. Similarly, Huangfu Mi's "Jia Yi" and Yang Shangshan's "Tai Su" are both based on this, albeit with slight differences. The overarching principles that medical practitioners must adhere to are found within these texts.[75p]

然按西汉《艺文志》有《内经》十八卷及扁鹊、白氏二《内经》，凡三家，而《素问》之目乃不列；至隋《经籍志》始有《素问》之名，而不指为《内经》；唐王冰乃以《九灵》九卷牵合汉志之数，而为之注释，复以阴阳大论，托为其师张公所藏，以补其亡逸，而其用心亦勤矣。惜乎朱墨混淆，玉石相乱，训诂失之于迂疏，引援或至于未切。至宋林亿、高若讷等正其误文，而增其缺义，颇于冰为有功。今于各篇之内，注意与经相类者，仍断章摘句，而释以己意，冀与同志商榷，非敢妄议前修也。[76p]

According to the "Bibliography of Arts and Literature" from the Western Han Dynasty, there are eighteen volumes of the "Inner Canon" as well as two "Inner Canons" by Bian Que and the Bai family, totaling three versions; however, the title of "Suwen" is not listed. It wasn't until the Sui Dynasty's "Catalogue of Classics" that the name "Suwen" first appeared, and it was not identified as part of the "Inner Canon." During the Tang Dynasty, Wang Bing made annotations based on the nine volumes of "Jiu Ling," connecting them with the numbers in the Han record and also included discussions on Yin and Yang, claiming them to be the writings of his teacher, Zhang Gong, to compensate for what was lost. His efforts were indeed diligent. Unfortunately, there was a confusion of red and black ink, mixing precious stones and ordinary rocks, and the interpretations suffered from excessive leniency, with some references being rather inaccurate. Later in the Song Dynasty, scholars like Lin Yi and Gao Ruone corrected the erroneous texts and filled in the missing meanings, greatly benefiting Wang Bing's work. Now, in each section, noticing connections with the classics, I still quote passages and interpret them with my own understanding, hoping to engage in discussion with colleagues, not daring to carelessly criticize the earlier scholars.[76p]

《内经》、《灵枢》，汉、惰、唐《艺文志》皆不录，隋有《针经》九卷，唐有《灵宝注》及《黄帝九灵经》十二卷而已。或谓王冰以《九灵》更名为《灵枢》，又谓《九灵》尤详于针，故皇甫谧名之为《针经》，即隋志《针经》九卷。苟一书而二名，不应唐别出《针经》十一二卷也。所谓《灵宝注》者，乃扁鹊太元君所笺，世所罕传。宋季有《灵枢略》一卷，今亦湮没。绍兴初，史崧并是书为十二卷，而复其旧，较之他本颇善，学者当与《素问》并观，盖其旨意，互相发明故也。[77p]

The "Inner Canon" and "Spiritual Pivot" are not recorded in the catalogs of the Han, Wei, and Tang dynasties. In the Sui dynasty, there were nine volumes of the "Needle Classic," and in the Tang dynasty, there were only the "Spirit Treasure Annotations" and the twelve volumes of the "Yellow Emperor's Nine Spirits." Some say Wang Bing renamed the "Nine Spirits" to "Spiritual Pivot," and it is said that the "Nine Spirits" is particularly detailed regarding needles, which is why Huangfu Mi referred to it as the "Needle Classic," corresponding to the nine volumes of the "Needle Classic" recorded in the Sui catalogs. If it is indeed a singular work with two names, there should not be an additional eleven or twelve volumes of the "Needle Classic" emerging in the Tang dynasty. The so-called "Spirit Treasure Annotations" are notes from Bian Que, the Supreme Source Lord, which have rarely been transmitted through the ages. In the late Song dynasty, there was a one-volume "Brief of the Spiritual Pivot," which has also become lost. At the beginning of Shaoxing, Shi Song compiled this work into twelve volumes, restoring it to its former state, and compared to other editions, it is quite good. Scholars should study it alongside the "Simple Questions," as their meanings illuminate each other.[77p]

《本草》三卷，旧称《神农本经》，汉《艺文志》未详，至梁陶隐居始尊信而表章之，谓此书应与《素问》同类，但后人多更修饰之耳。秦皇所焚，医方卜术不与，故犹得全录。及遭汉献之迁徙，晋怀之奔迸，文籍焚燹，千不遗一。今之所存，有此三卷，是其本经，然所出郡县，乃多后汉时制，疑张仲景、华元化所记。旧经之药，止三百六十五种，因而注释，分为七卷。唐李英公世绩与苏恭参考得失，又增一百一十四种，分为二十卷，世谓之《唐本草》。宋刘翰等又附益医家当用者二百二十种。伪蜀孟昶亦命其臣韩保升等，以唐本《图经》参考增广，世谓之《蜀本草》。[78p]

"Ben Cao" consists of three volumes, formerly known as "Shen Nong Ben Jing." The Han Dynasty's "Yi Wen Zhi" does not describe it in detail, but it was during the Liang Dynasty that Tao Yinju began to honor and document it, suggesting that this book should be grouped with the "Su Wen." However, later generations primarily made revisions. The book was burned by the First Emperor of Qin, and it did not include medical formulas and divination, thus it was still able to be completely transcribed. When it suffered during the migration of Emperor Xian of Han and the chaos of Jin Huai, literary works were consumed by fire, and only rare remnants survived. What exists now are these three volumes of the original text, yet the counties and regions they mention are mostly from the Later Han period, casting doubt that they were recorded by Zhang Zhongjing and Hua Yuanhua. The original text listed only 365 types of medicine, which were then annotated and divided into seven volumes. During the Tang Dynasty, Li Yingong's achievements and Su Gong's references led to an addition of 114 types, dividing it into twenty volumes, commonly referred to as "Tang Ben Cao." In the Song Dynasty, Liu Han and others further supplemented it with 220 types commonly used by physicians. The false Shu figure Meng Chang also ordered his minister Han Baosheng and others to refer to the Tang "Tu Jing" to expand it, which became known as "Shu Ben Cao."[78p]

至宋掌禹锡等补注，新旧药合一千八十二种，定以白字为神农所说，黑字为名医所传，草石之品，可谓大备也。若雷公以下，蔡邕、徐大山、秦承袓、王季璞、郑虔诸公所撰名《本草》者，凡三十九部，三百五十卷，虽显晦不齐，无非辅翼旧经焉耳。近代陈衍作《本草折衷》，王好古作《汤液本草》，亦删繁之遗意也。窃意旧记郡县，古今沿革不同，及一物而根苗异名，或同名异质，而主疗互见者，尚须考定。俾归于一可也。[79p]

During the Song dynasty, Yuxi and others compiled supplementary notes, resulting in a total of 1,082 medicinal substances combined from ancient and modern texts. The substances are categorized with white characters representing what Shen Nong said, and black characters indicating the transmissions of famous physicians. The range of herbs and minerals can be considered extensive. Regarding the works written by figures such as Lei Gong, Cai Yong, Xu Dashan, Qin Chengzu, Wang Jip, and Zheng Qian, there are a total of 39 volumes across 350 sections; though their content may vary in clarity, they all serve to support the old classics. In modern times, Chen Yan compiled "A Compendium of Materia Medica," and Wang Haogu produced "A Guide to Decoctions," both of which aimed to simplify previous complexities. I privately believe that the records of prefectures and counties have differed between ancient and modern times, and there are cases where one thing has different names based on its roots and sprouts, or where the same name applies to diverse qualities. It is essential to establish clear distinctions for proper therapeutic use. This would allow for a more unified understanding.[79p]

《难经》十三卷，乃秦越人祖述《黄帝内经》，设为问答之辞，以示学者，所引经言，多非《灵》、《素》本文，盖古有其书而今亡之耳。隋时有吕博望注本，不传；宋王惟一集五家之说，而醇疵或有相乱；惟虞氏粗为可观。纪齐卿，注稍密，乃附辨杨元操、吕广、王宗正三子之非；周仲立颇加订异，而考证未明；李子野亦为句注解，而无所启发；近代张洁古证后附药，殊非经意；王少卿演绎其说，目曰重元，亦未足以发前人之蕴。余尝辑诸家之长，先训诂而后辞意，窃附鄙说其间，以便后学，未敢以为是也。《伤寒论》十卷，乃后汉张机仲景用《素问》热论之说，广伊尹汤液而为之。[80p]

The "Nanjing" consists of thirteen volumes and is a work by the Qin man Zu Shu, who based it on the "Huangdi Neijing". It is set up as a dialogue to demonstrate to scholars, and many of the cited words are not from the texts of "Ling" or "Su", as these ancient works have been lost to time. During the Sui Dynasty, there was a commentary by Lu Bowang, which did not spread; in the Song Dynasty, Wang Wei Yi compiled interpretations from five schools, but there were inconsistencies among them. Only the interpretations of the Yu family can be regarded as somewhat substantial. Ji Qiqing's commentary is rather detailed, but it mainly refutes the views of Yang Yuancao, Lu Guang, and Wang Zongzheng. Zhou Zhongli made several revisions, yet his investigations were not thorough. Li Ziye provided explanations at the sentence level but offered no insights. In modern times, Zhang Jiege added ancient evidence and prescriptions that are not particularly relevant to the original text. Wang Shaoqing elaborated on his views, naming it "Chongyuan", but it was also insufficient to reveal the depths of earlier scholars' thoughts. I have tried to compile the strengths of various schools, first explaining the terms and then interpreting the intentions, and I have humbly added my own comments in between for the convenience of future learners, but I do not dare to claim it as definitive. The "Shanghan Lun" consists of ten volumes and was composed by Zhang Zhongjing of the Later Han, using the principles of the "Su Wen" regarding fevers and basing it on the treatment methods of Yi Yin.[80p]

至晋王叔和始因旧说，重为撰次；而宋成无己复为之注释；其后庞安常、朱肱、许叔微、韩袛和、王实之流，固亦互有开发，而大纲大要，无越乎吐汗下温四法而已。盖一证一药，万选万中，千载之下，如合符节，前修指为群方之袓，信矣。所可憾者，审脉时汩王氏之言，三阴率多断简；况张经王传亦往往反复，后先亥家相杂，自非字字句句熟玩而精思之，未有能造其阃奥者。陈无择尝补三阴证药于《三因论》，其意盖可见矣。近人徐止善作《伤寒补亡》，恐与先哲之意不合。余因窃举大要，以补成氏之未备，知医君子或有所取也。[81p]

The Duke of Jin, Wang Shuhe, initially based his work on earlier theories and then reorganized and compiled them anew. Following him, Song Chengwuj, made annotations on this work. Later, figures such as Pang Anchang, Zhu Gong, Xu Shuwei, Han Dihe, and Wang Shizhi also elaborated on these ideas, but fundamentally, none of them went beyond the four methods of sweating, warming, and so forth. Indeed, each diagnosis corresponds to a specific remedy, and in thousands of years, it seems harmonious, pointing towards the ancestors of various prescriptions, which is certainly true. What can be lamented is the reliance on the familial sayings of Wang regarding the diagnosis of the three yin, which are frequently undifferentiated. Furthermore, the accounts of Zhang and Wang often mix the past and present, creating confusion. Without a thorough and thoughtful engagement with each character and phrase, it’s unlikely anyone can grasp the profound meanings. Chen Wuze once supplemented the medicinal prescriptions for the three yin in the "Three Causes Treatise," and his intention can indeed be perceived. Recently, Xu Zhishan composed "Supplementing the Lost of Cold Damage," which unfortunately may not align with the views of earlier scholars. Therefore, I have taken the liberty to present key points to supplement what Cheng's work lacks, hoping that the worthy physicians may find it useful.[81p]

《脉经》十卷，乃西晋太医令王叔和本诸《内经》、《素问》，《九灵》及扁鹊、仲景元化之说，裒次而成，实医门之龟镜，诊切之指的，自与近代仿托钤决者不同。历岁既深，传授不一，各秘所藏，互有得失。至宋秘阁林亿等始考证谬妄，颇加改易，意其新撰《四时经》之类，皆林氏所增入。陈孔固、何大任、毛升、王宗卿辈，皆尝审订刊传，今不多见。近人谢坚白以其所藏善本，刻于豫章，传者始广。余尝摭其精语，并引《内经》之辞，作《诊切枢要》二卷，非敢剪其冗复，间亦补其缺漏，且附私语各条之下，以与同志研究尔。[82p]

The "Classic of Pulses," in ten volumes, is based on the teachings of Wang Shuhe, the Chief Physician of the Western Jin, compiling the "Inner Canon," "Simple Questions," "Nine Spirits," as well as the theories of Bian Que and Zhang Zhongjing. It serves as a pivotal reference for the medical field, differing from modern interpretations and methods. Over the years, the transmission of this work has varied, with various secrets preserved and losses incurred. During the Song Dynasty, scholars like Lin Yi began to examine and correct the inaccuracies, making significant changes; their newly composed "Classic of the Four Seasons," among others, was largely supplemented by the Lin family. Notable scholars such as Chen Konggu, He Daren, Mao Sheng, and Wang Zongqing have also reviewed and published their interpretations, although they are now rarely seen. More recently, Xie Jianbai utilized his collection of good editions to print a version in Yuzhang, which has helped its dissemination. I have gathered its essence and cited excerpts from the "Inner Canon" to create "Essentials of Diagnosis and Pulse Taking," in two volumes, not intending to cut unnecessary repetitions but to also fill in gaps, and I included personal notes under each section for collaborative research among colleagues.[82p]

《脉诀》一卷，乃六朝高阳生所撰，托以叔和之名，谬以七表八里九道之目，以惑学者。通真子刘元宾为之注，且续歌括附其后，辞既鄙俚，意亦滋晦。今代王光国删其旧辞，而益以新语，既不出其畦径，安能得乎原本？余如青溪徐裔、甄权、李上交辈，皆自撰者，凡十余家，亦每蹈袭前说，在叔和之所不取，读者止记入式歌，以驯至乎《脉经》可也。《病原论》五十卷，乃隋大业太医博士巢元方等，奉敕撰集，原诸病候，而附以养生导引诸法，裒成一家之书，醇疵相混，盖可见矣。[83p]

The "Pulse Treatise," a single scroll, was composed by Gao Yang from the Six Dynasties, falsely attributed to the name of Shu He, misleading scholars with references to the Seven Tables, Eight Lines, and Nine Paths. Liu Yuanbin, the True Scholar, annotated it and added supplementary verses at the end. The language is crude, and the meaning is obscure. In today's era, Wang Guoguang has edited out the old expressions and enhanced it with new language; yet, it does not stray from its original paths, how could it possibly capture the essence? I, like Qingxi Xu Yi, Zhen Quan, Li Shangjiao, and others, have all composed our own works, totaling over ten families, and each has followed earlier interpretations, which Shu He himself did not accept. Readers are advised to only note the verses in order to be familiar with the "Pulse Classic." The "Discussion of Pathogens," in fifty volumes, was compiled by Chao Yuanfang, the chief physician of the Sui Dynasty, upon imperial decree. It elaborates on various diseases and includes methods for health preservation and guidance, forming a comprehensive book, though there are mixed merits and faults, which is evident.[83p]

宋之监署，乃用为课试；元复循袭，列医门之七经。然附会杂揉，非复当时之旧，具眼者当自见之。吴景贤亦作《病源》一书，近代不传。《天元玉册元诰》十卷，不知何人所作，历汉至唐诸《艺文志》俱不载录，其文自与《内经》不类，非战国时书。其间有天皇真人，昔书其文，若道正无为，先天有之，太易无名，先于道生等语，皆老氏遗意，意必老氏之徒所著。大要推原五运六气、上下临御、主客胜复、政化淫正及三元、九宫、太乙、司政之类，殊为详明，深足以羽翼《内经》六微旨、五常政等篇。[84p]

The offices of the Song dynasty were used for examinations; the Yuan dynasty continued this practice, listing the seven classics of medicine. However, they mixed and merged ideas, no longer adhering to the old methods of the time, which knowledgeable individuals would recognize. Wu Jingxian also wrote a book titled "Sources of Illness," which has not been passed down in modern times. The "Heavenly Original Jade Book," consisting of ten volumes, remains unattributed; it is not recorded in the literary catalogs from the Han to the Tang dynasties. Its content does not resemble that of the "Inner Canon" and cannot be from the Warring States period. Among its contents, there are writings by the Celestial Sovereign, stating that if one follows the true way of non-action, there exists an original state before heaven, the nameless Great Change, and phrases similar to "the Dao precedes existence," all reflecting the intentions of Laozi, likely authored by his followers. Broadly, the text explores the Five Movements and Six Energies, the governance of heaven and earth, the interplay of subjects and guests, political morality and correction, as well as the Three Origins, Nine Palaces, Great Unity, and governance, which are exceptionally detailed and significantly enhance the subtle principles of the "Inner Canon" and other chapters on the Five Constants of governance.[84p]

太元君扁鹊为之注，犹郭象之于南华，非心学之所易晓。观其经注一律，似出一人之手。谓扁鹊为秦越人，则传中无太元君之号。旧门仿托，率多类此。《元珠密语》十卷，乃启元子所述，其自序谓得遇元珠子而师事之，与我启蒙，故自号启元子，盖启问于元珠也。目曰《元珠密语》，乃元珠子密而口授之言也。及考王氏《素问》序乃云辞理秘密，难粗论述者，别撰《元珠》以陈其道，二序政自相类。意者元珠之名，取诸蒙庄子，所谓黄帝遗元珠，使象罔得之之语，则师事元珠子而号启元者，皆妄也。[85p]

The Taiyuan Lord's commentary on Bian Que is akin to Guo Xiang's interpretations of the Nan Hua, and is not easy to comprehend through the teachings of the mind school. Observing the consistency in the texts and commentaries suggests they came from the same author. While Bian Que is identified as a person from Qin and Yue, there is no mention of the title Taiyuan Lord in the transmission. Older traditions often imitate this style.

The "Secret Words of the Original Pearl," a ten-volume work, is presented by Qiyuanzi.
