In its preface, it states that Qiyuanzi was fortunate enough to encounter and study under the Original Pearl Master, thus calling himself Qiyuanzi as he sought enlightenment from the Original Pearl. The title "Secret Words of the Original Pearl" refers to the teachings passed down privately by the Original Pearl Master. When examining the preface of Wang's "Plain Questions," it notes that the principles and reasoning are obscure and challenging to discuss, prompting the separate creation of the "Original Pearl" to explain its doctrine; both prefaces are similar in nature.

The implication is that the name Original Pearl is derived from Meng and Zhuangzi, specifically regarding the phrase about the Yellow Emperor leaving the Original Pearl, which is said to be elusive. Therefore, claiming one learns from the Original Pearl Master and titles oneself Qiyuan is unfounded.[85p]

宋高保衡等较正《内经》，乃云：详王氏《元珠》世无传者，今《元珠》乃后人附托之文耳。虽非王氏之书，亦于《素问》九卷二十四卷，颇有发明。余尝合《素问》观之，而密语所述，乃六气之说，与高氏所指诸卷，全不相侔，疑必刊传者有所误也。原其所从，盖攟摭《内经》六微旨及至真要等五篇，洎《天元玉册》要言，而附会杂说，其诸纪运休祥之应，未必可征，实伪书也。苟启元别撰，果见于世，又岂止述气运一端而已？览者取其长而去其短可也。《中藏经》八卷，少室山邓处中云：华先生佗游公宜山古洞，值二老人授以疗病之法，得石床上书一函，用以施试，甚验。[86p]

Song Gao Baoheng and others revised the "Neijing," and said: The detailed account of Wang's "Yuan Zhu" has no transmission in the world; the current "Yuan Zhu" is merely a text attributed by later generations. Although it is not Wang's work, it still has some insights regarding the "Suwen," particularly in the ninth and twenty-fourth volumes. I have compared the "Suwen" and found that the secret writings therein discuss the theory of the six qi and do not match at all with the volumes referenced by the Gao family, leading me to suspect that the transmitted versions contain errors. Tracing back to its origins, it likely compiles the subtle meanings of the "Neijing" and the fundamental essentials of five essays, along with essential words from the "Tian Yuan Yu Ce," mingled with miscellaneous theories. The records of qi movements and auspices may not be reliable, and it is indeed a spurious text. If the original author made a separate compilation, it would surely have been known to the world; it would not merely address the aspect of qi movements. Readers may take the strengths and discard the weaknesses. The "Zhong Zang Jing," in eight volumes, states that Laozi traveled to the ancient cave of Mount Yishan, where he encountered two old men who imparted methods for healing. He obtained a stone bed with writings and used it for trial, which proved very effective.[86p]

余乃先生外孙，因吊先生寝室，梦有所授，获是书于石函中。其托伪荒诞如此，竟不考传狱吏焚书之实，其伪不攻自破。按《唐志》有吴普集华氏药方。别无中藏之名。普其弟子，宜有所集。窃意诸论非普辈不能作，邓氏特附别方而更今名耳。盖其方所用太平钱并山药者，盖太平乃宋熙陵初年号，薯蓣以避后陵偏讳，而始名山药。其余可以类推。然脉要及察声色形证等说，必出元化遗意，览者细为审谛，当自知之。[87p]

I am the grandson of the teacher. While visiting the teacher's chamber, I had a dream in which I received a teaching, and I found this book in a stone box. The pretense is so absurd that it cannot withstand scrutiny, especially regarding the true account of the officials burning books, which serves to expose its falsehood. According to the "Tang Records," there is a collection of prescriptions by Wu Pu from the family of Hua. There is no other name for it in the central archives. Pu is his disciple, and it should be a compilation of his own. I suspect that these writings could not have been produced by individuals of the Pu lineage; rather, the Deng family merely attached a separate formula and renamed it. The reason the formula includes the "Taiping" coins and "mountain yam" is because "Taiping" was the era name in the early years of Song Xiling, and "yams" were initially named "mountain yams" to avoid a later taboo. The rest can be inferred similarly. However, discussions on pulse diagnosis and observations of sounds and colors must originate from the intentions of the original transformations. Readers should carefully assess it for themselves.[87p]

《圣济经》十卷，宋徽宗所作，大要祖述《内》、《素》而引援六经，旁及老氏之言，以阐轩岐遗旨。政和间，颁是经于两学，辟雍生吴提为之讲义。若达道正纪等篇，皆足以裨益政道，启迪众工。余如孕元立本制字命物二三章，释诸字义，失于穿凿，良由不考六书之过，瑕瑜具存，固无害于美玉也。其论诸医有曰：扁鹊医如秦鉴烛物，妍媸不隐，又如奕秋遇敌，着着可法，观者不能察其神机。仓公医如轮扁斫轮，得心应手，自不能以巧思语人。张长沙如汤武之师，无非王道，其攻守奇正，不以敌之大小皆可制胜。华元化医如庖丁解牛，挥刃而肯綮无碍，其造谐自当有神，虽欲师之而不可得。[88p]

The "Sheng Ji Jing," in ten volumes, was composed by Song Huizong. It primarily expounds upon the "Nei" and "Su," while also referencing the Six Classics and touching upon the teachings of Laozi to elucidate the essence of the Xuanqi. During the Zhenghe era, this text was issued to the two academies, and Wu Ti, a student at the Biyong, was appointed to lecture on it.

The chapters on the Path and proper governance can truly benefit political affairs and enlighten many skilled workers. As for the sections on the creation of characters and naming objects, although I dissect their meanings, my interpretations may be forced, stemming from a failure to thoroughly study the Six Writings. Both flaws and merits exist, yet they do not diminish the value of fine jade.

In discussing the various physicians, it is said: Bian Que's medicine was like Qin’s lamp illuminating objects—beauty and ugliness are evident, and akin to Yi Qi’s encounters with opponents, each move could be strategic, yet observers could not discern his divine skills. Cang Gong’s medicine resembles a wheel with a skilled hand, adeptly working without the need for clever words. Zhang Changsha was like the teacher of Tang and Wu, adhering strictly to the royal way; his strategies in offense and defense were uniquely effective, capable of overcoming any enemy regardless of size.

Hua Yuanhua's medicine resembled that of a cook skillfully cutting up an ox, his blade moving effortlessly and precisely; his harmony in craftsmanship surely had a divine quality, and although one might wish to learn from it, it could not be attained.[88p]

孙思邈医如康成注书，详于训诂，其自得之妙，未易以示人，味其膏腴，可以无饥矣。庞安常医能启扁鹊之所秘，法元化之可法，使天假之年，其所就当不在古人下。钱仲阳医如李靖用兵，度越纵舍，卒与法会，其始以《颅顖方》著名于时，盖因扁鹊之因时所重，而为之变尔。陈无择医如老吏断案，深于鞫谳，未免移情就法，自当其任则有余，使之代治则繁剧。许叔微医如顾恺写神，神气有余，特不出形似之外，可模而不可及。[89p]

Sun Simiao's medical approach resembles that of Kang Sheng in his annotations, being detailed in interpretation. The essence of his understanding is not easily conveyed to others; savoring its richness can alleviate hunger. Pang Anchang's medicine could unveil the secrets of Bian Que and follow the transformative principles of nature, ensuring that when granted the years, his results would not fall behind those of the ancients. Qian Zhongyang's medical strategies were akin to Li Jing's military tactics, measured in maneuvering and strategic retreats, ultimately aligning with established principles. He first gained fame with the "Skull and Brain Formula," reflecting the importance placed on adapting methods to the era as Bian Que did. Chen Wuze's practice is similar to an old official adjudicating cases, profoundly engaged in interrogations, yet he might let emotions sway him towards the law; if left to his own devices, his results would be ample, but if substituted, would become overly complicated. Xu Shuwei's medicine resembles Gu Kaizhi's artistry, rich in spirit and energy, distinctly not confined to mere resemblance, exemplary yet unattainable.[89p]

张易水医如濂溪之图太极，分阴分阳，而包括理气，其要以古方新病自为家法；或者失察，欲指图为极，则近乎画蛇添足矣。刘河间医如橐驼种树，所在全活，但假冰雪以为春。利于松柏而不利于蒲柳。张子和医如老将对敌，或陈兵背水，或济河焚丹，置之死地而后生，不善效之，非溃则北矣；其六门三法，盖长沙之绪余也。李东垣医如丝弦新絙，一鼓而竽籁并熄，胶柱和之，七弦由是而不谐矣；无他，希声之妙，非开指所能知也。严子礼医如欧阳询写字，善守法度而不尚飘逸，学者易于摹仿，终乏汉晋风度。张公度医专法仲景，如简斋赋诗，并有少陵气韵。王德肤医如虞人张罗，广络原野，而脱兔殊多，诡遇获禽，无足算者耳。[90p]

Zhang Yishui's medicine is like Lianxi's diagram of the Taiji, dividing yin and yang, encompassing principles and qi. Its essence is to use ancient methods for new ailments, making it a household practice; if one misjudges and aims for extreme measures, it is akin to adding legs to a snake. Liu Hejian's medicine is like a camel planting trees, ensuring survival everywhere, but relying on ice and snow as spring. It benefits pines and cypresses but not willows and poplars. Zhang Zihe's medicine is like an old general facing an enemy, sometimes deploying troops with their backs to water or burning the river with dan, placing them in desperate situations to create life; if one does not master this, victory is elusive. His six gates and three methods are indeed remnants of Changsha's teachings. Li Dongyuan's medicine is like a stringed instrument with new silks; when struck, all harmony ceases. It is like bonding with glue, yet the seven strings do not resonate in tune; the essence of harmonious sounds is not something that can be understood merely by opening fingers. Yan Zili's medicine is like Ouyang Xun writing; he skillfully adheres to rules without promoting flamboyance, making it easier for learners to imitate, yet ultimately lacking the charm of the Han and Jin dynasties. Zhang Gongdu's medicine specializes in the methods of Zhongjing, much like a poet from Jianzhai, imbued with the spirit of Shaoling. Wang Defu's medicine is like a fisherman casting nets in open fields, capturing many hares, encountering strange situations that yield game – it is not something that can be easily calculated.[90p]

　　　　青岩丛录 【明　王袆】

　　　　　论医

　　医家之书，自《内经》以下，藏于有司者，一百七十九家，二百九部，一千二百五十九卷，而后出杂著者不与焉。《内经》读为黄帝之书，虽先秦之士，依仿而托之，其言质奥而义宏深，实医家之宗旨，殆犹吾儒之六经乎？秦越人《八十一难经》继作，盖举黄帝岐伯之要旨而推明之，亚于《内经》者也。汉张仲景本《内经》、《难经》之旨，着《金匮玉函经》及伤寒诸论，其论六气之所伤，最为详备。[91p]

On Medicine

The texts of medicine, from the "Neijing" onward, are preserved by the authorities, amounting to one hundred seventy-nine families, two hundred nine works, and one thousand two hundred fifty-nine volumes, while subsequent miscellaneous writings are not included. The "Neijing" is read as the work of the Yellow Emperor; although scholars before the Qin dynasty imitated and attributed their thoughts to it, its words are profound and its meanings vast and deep, truly representing the core tenets of medical practitioners, much like the Six Classics do for Confucian scholars. The "Eighty-One Difficulties" by a man from Qin and Yue continues this tradition, summarizing and clarifying the essential points of the Yellow Emperor and Qi Bo, but it is inferior to the "Neijing." Han Dynasty's Zhang Zhongjing, drawing from the essence of the "Neijing" and "Nanjing," produced the "Jinkui Yaoluan" and discussions on febrile diseases, with his analysis of the six qi injuries being the most detailed.[91p]

晋王叔和纂岐伯、华陀等书为《脉经》，叙阴阳内外，辨三部九候，分人迎气口，条陈十二经，洎三焦五脏六腑之病，尤为精密。二氏之书，诚千古不刊之典也。厥后巢元方着《病源候论》，王冰撰《天元玉策》，要皆有所袓述。然元方言风寒二湿而不着湿热之说，冰推五运六气之变而患在滞而不通，此其失也。至唐孙思邈出，以绝人之识，笃济物之仁，其列《千金》方、翼，所以发前言启后学，有功于医道深矣。当时王焘有《外台秘要》，所言方证符禁，灼灸甚详；然谓针能杀生人，而不能起死人，则一偏之论也。及宋，钱乙、庞安时、许叔微迭兴。[92p]

King Shuhe of Jin compiled the writings of Qibo, Hua Tuo, and others into the "Classic of Pulses," which discusses yin and yang internally and externally, differentiates the three divisions and nine conditions, identifies the points of human reception of Qi, outlines the twelve meridians, and details the diseases of the three burners, five organs, and six bowels, all with remarkable precision. The works of these two scholars are indeed classics that endure through the ages. Subsequently, Chao Yuanfang wrote "On the Sources of Disease," and Wang Bing composed "The Jade Strategies of Heaven," both of which draw on ancestral teachings. However, Yuanfang spoke of wind and cold, and two types of dampness, while neglecting the concept of damp-heat, and Bing theorized about the changes of the five movements and six Qi, focusing on stagnation rather than flow, which were shortcomings. Later, during the Tang dynasty, Sun Simiao emerged with extraordinary insights and deep compassion for humanity, listing formulas in "Prescriptions Worth a Thousand Gold," which enlightened preceding and succeeding scholars and greatly contributed to the medical field. At that time, Wang Tao had "The Secret Essentials of the Outer Chamber," discussing formulas and methods in detail; yet he stated that needles can end a life but cannot revive the dead, which represents a one-sided view. By the Song dynasty, Qian Yi, Pang Anshi, and Xu Shuwei rose in prominence.[92p]

庞则囿于准绳尺寸之中，许则务在出奇而应变，其术皆本于仲景。惟钱深造仲景之阃奥，建为五脏之方，各随所宜用。谓肝有相火则有泻而无补，肾为真水则有补而无泻，可谓启《内经》之秘。惜其遗书散亡，出于阎孝忠之所集者，非乙之本真也。若大观间，陈师文、裴元宗辈所制二百九十七方，则欲以一定之方，而应无穷之病，识者固知其昧于变通之道矣。金氏之有中原也，张洁古、刘守真、张子和、李明之四人者作，医道于是乎中兴。[93p]

Pang was confined within the confines of established measures, while Xu was devoted to ingenuity and adaptability, both drawing from the teachings of Zhang Zhongjing. Only Qian deeply explored the profound mysteries of Zhang Zhongjing, establishing methods for the five organs, each suited for its specific use. It is said that when the liver has excess fire, it should be purged rather than replenished, while the kidneys, being true water, can be replenished but not purged; this could be considered revealing the secrets of the "Inner Canon." Regrettably, his writings have been lost, and what survives, gathered by Yan Xiaozhong, is not the original truth. In the era of Daguang, the formulas compiled by Chen Shiwen, Pei Yuanzong, and others, totaling two hundred ninety-seven, aimed to use fixed formulas to address countless diseases, though those who understand know that they lack insight into the principle of adaptability. The presence of the Jin family in the Central Plains is attributed to four individuals: Zhang Jiefu, Liu Shouzhen, Zhang Zihe, and Li Ming, who contributed to the revival of the medical path.[93p]

洁古以古方新病不能相值，治疾一切不以方，故其书不传，其学则明之深得之。明之推内外二伤，尤先于治脾土，其为法专于补，其所著《脾胃论》，诚根本之言也。子和以吐汗下三法，风寒暑湿火燥六门，为医之关键，其剂多峻厉，其为法主于攻。守真论风火之病，以《内经》病机气宜十九条者为《原病式》，曲尽精微，其治法则与子和相出入者也。张氏一再传，其后无闻。李氏弟子多在中州，独刘氏传之荆山浮图师，师至江南，传之宋中人罗知悌，而南方之医皆宗之。爰及近时，天下之言医者，非刘、李之学弗道也。刘李之法虽攻补不同，会而通之，随证而用之，不存其人乎？[94p]

The ancient principles, while new to modern ailments, cannot adequately address them; thus, the methods of treatment are not conveyed in their texts, but their teachings are indeed profound. They illuminate the internal and external dual afflictions, particularly emphasizing the treatment of the spleen and stomach, focusing on supplementation. The work "On the Spleen and Stomach" is truly foundational.

Zihe proposed three methods involving sweating, purging, and harmonizing, identifying six pathogenic factors: wind, cold, heat, dampness, fire, and dryness, which are key to medicine. His treatments are often severe and offensive in nature.

In discussing the diseases brought about by wind and fire, the discussion relies on the "Inner Canon," outlining nineteen pathogenic mechanisms termed "Original Disease Models," which explore intricate details; however, their treatment methods differ from Zihe's.

Zhang’s teachings were repeatedly passed down yet became obscure afterward. The disciples of Li primarily resided in Zhongzhou, while only the Liu family transmitted their teachings to the priest in Jing Mountain. The priest spread this knowledge in Jiangnan, subsequently passing it to Luo Zhiti of the Song dynasty, who was revered by Southern physicians.

In more recent times, discussions of medicine predominantly concern the teachings of Liu and Li. Although their methods of attack and supplementation differ, they converge and interlink, adapting according to the clinical manifestations. Does the essence of their teachings no longer exist?[94p]

　　　　比事摘录

　　　　　医孝

　　北齐李元忠母老多病，元忠专心医道，研习积年，遂善此技。族弟亦因母病，医为治疗，不愈，遂自精究针药，母病乃除。隋。许道幼亦因其母疾患，遍览经方，得以究极，世号名医。诫诸子曰：人子当视膳药，不知方术，岂为孝乎？由是世相传授。唐　王勃尝谓：人子不可不知医。时长安曹元有秘术，勃从之游，尽得其要。勃之所见，实同道功。唐有王焘，亦因母病学医，因以所学作书行世。宋高若讷亦因母病，遂兼通医书，虽国医皆屈伏。张仲景伤寒论诀，孙思邈方书及《外台秘要》久不传，悉考校讹谬行之世。始知有是名医，多出卫州，皆本高氏学焉。此以医为孝者也。宋　李虚己母丧明，虚己旦日舐睛不懈，二年母目复明。李行简父患痈极痛，行简吮其败膏，不唾于地，疾寻平。此又以孝为医者也。[95p]

Excerpt on Filial Piety in Medicine

In Northern Qi, Li Yuanzhong’s mother was elderly and frequently ill. Yuanzhong dedicated himself to the art of medicine and studied diligently for many years, thus becoming proficient in this skill. His cousin, seeing his own mother suffer from illness, also sought to treat her and, when she did not recover, devoted himself to the study of acupuncture and herbal remedies, ultimately curing her. During the Sui Dynasty, Xu Daoyou also sought out medical texts due to his mother's ailments, and through extensive reading, gained profound knowledge, earning the title of famous physician. He advised his sons, saying: a son ought to be attentive to both diet and medicine; if he does not know the principles of treatment, can he truly be considered filial? This became a teaching passed down through generations.

Tang poet Wang Bo once stated that a son cannot be ignorant of medicine. At that time, there was a secret technique in Chang’an by Cao Yuan, which Bo learned about during his visits and obtained its essentials. What Bo encountered genuinely enhanced his understanding of the craft. In the Tang Dynasty, Wang Tao also learned medicine due to his mother’s illness and published his works in the world. In the Song Dynasty, Gao Ruone, motivated by his mother’s sickness, became well-versed in medical texts, so much so that even the eminent national physicians respected him. Zhang Zhongjing's Treatise on Cold Damage and Sun Simiao’s prescriptions along with "Essentials of the External Respository" had long been overlooked; he carefully examined and corrected their records for circulation. It was then understood that many renowned physicians originated from Weizhou, all learning from the Gao clan. This thus exemplifies the link between medicine and filial piety.

In the Song Dynasty, Li Xujì’s mother lost her eyesight. Xujì diligently licked her eyes daily without fail. After two years, her sight was restored. Li Xingjian’s father suffered from painful boils; Xingjian sucked the pus from them without spitting on the ground, and the illness soon settled. This further highlights the connection between filial piety and medicine.[95p]

　　　　医先 【明　王文禄】

　　　　　论医

　　医者意也，度时致病者意起之，立方医之，若天时圣教不同也。罗太无见元世风俗奢靡，丰于滋味，湿热痰火，致病常多，故授朱丹溪以清金降火之法，乃辟《和剂局方》温补之非，矫之过也。夫《局方》热药固不可，丹溪专用凉药亦不可。况今元气日耗也，用丹溪法治者，多坏脾胃。盖痰生脾湿，热生脾虚，必用东垣补脾法为上。是以医贵审气运，察人情，及致病之原。[96p]

On Medicine
By Wang Wenlu (Ming Dynasty)

Medicine is about intention. It involves understanding the timing of diseases and determining their causes, as well as establishing treatments, just as heavenly principles and sacred teachings may differ. In the Yuan dynasty, customs were extravagant, leading to rich tastes and a humid, hot environment, which often resulted in many illnesses. Therefore, Zhu Danxi was taught methods to clear heat and lower fire, distinguishing them from the warm tonifying methods of the “Formula for Harmonious Medicaments,” correcting excesses. However, while hot medicines of the "Formula" are certainly not permissible, relying solely on Danxi’s cold medicines is also not appropriate. Especially now, as the vital energy is increasingly depleted, treating with Danxi’s methods often harms the spleen and stomach. This is because phlegm arises from spleen dampness, heat from spleen deficiency, and it is imperative to employ the method of supplementing the spleen from Dongyuan as a principal approach. Thus, it is crucial for physicians to carefully discern the flow of vital energy, observe human conditions, and identify the origins of diseases.[96p]

　　　　医学正传 【明　虞抟】

　　　　　或问

　　或问：医学源流自轩岐以来，以医术鸣世，与夫著书立言，俾后人之可法者，几何人哉？请明以告我！曰：予尝阅故学士宋公景濂之文而得其说矣。请陈如左。夫《黄帝内经》虽疑先秦之士依仿而作之，其言深而要，其旨邃以宏，其考辩信而有征，是当为医家之宗。下此则秦越人、和缓。和缓无书可传，越人所著《八十一难经》，则皆举《内经》之要而推明者也。又下此则淳于意、华佗。佗之熊经鸱顾固亦导引家之一术，至于刳腹背，湔肠胃而去疾，则涉于神怪矣。[97p]

The Correct Transmission of Medicine
(Ming - Yu Tuan)

On Medicine's Origins

Someone asked: "The origins of medicine trace back to Xuandi and Qiyi, where medical practices gained recognition in the world, and several individuals authored texts for future generations to follow. How many have done this?" I shall clarify this for you! I have read the writings of the scholar Song Gong Jinglian and have derived my understanding from them. Let me present it as follows: Although the "Huangdi Neijing" is suspected to have been written by scholars of the pre-Qin period imitating earlier works, its arguments are profound and concise, its principles intricate and broad, and its examination and debate are credible and substantiated; it ought to serve as the foundational text for medical practitioners. Following this are the Qin and Yue people, represented by He Huan. He Huan left no texts to pass down, while the Yue people wrote the "Eight-One Difficulties," which articulates and clarifies the essentials of the "Neijing." Below them are Chunyu Yi and Hua Tuo. Tuo's manipulation of the bones certainly belongs to the realm of guiding techniques. As for his method of opening the abdomen and cleansing the stomach and intestines to remove sickness, this drifts into the supernatural.[97p]

意之医状，司马迁备志之，其所谓迵风沓风者，今人绝不知为何病也，况复求其治疗之深旨乎？又下此，则张机之《金匮玉函经》及伤寒诸论，诚千古不刊之妙典，第详于六气所伤，而于嗜欲食饮罢劳之所致者，略而不议，兼之文字错简，亦未易以序次求之也。又下此则王叔和。叔和纂岐伯、华佗等书为《脉经》，叙阴阳内外，辨三部九候，分人迎气口，条陈十二经络，洎夫三焦五脏六腑之病，最为着明，惜乎为妄男子括以肤陋之脉歌，遂使其本书不盛行于世也。又下此则巢元方。其《病源》后编，似不为无所见者，但言风寒二气，而不着湿热之文，乃其失也。又下此则王冰。冰推五运六气之变，撰为《天元玉策》，周详切密，亦人之所难，苟泥之则局滞而不通矣。又下此，则王焘、孙思邈。[98p]

Regarding the medical conditions described by Sima Qian, the "Wei Feng" and "Ta Feng" are conditions that modern people do not understand at all, let alone seek their deeper treatments. Furthermore, there are texts such as Zhang Ji's "Jin Kui Yu Han Jing" and various treatises on typhoid fever, which are indeed timeless classics that elaborate on injuries caused by the six qi, yet they neglect the ailments arising from improper desires related to food, drink, and labor, and their texts are also confusing and difficult to systematically analyze. Below this, we have Wang Shuhe, who compiled the writings of Qi Bo and Hua Tuo into the "Mai Jing," discussing yin and yang, the internal and external, identifying the three divisions and nine pulses, distinguishing the person’s welcoming, qi, and mouth, and detailing the twelve meridians, as well as the diseases of the three burners, five organs, and six bowels, making it quite clear. Unfortunately, due to the superficial interpretations by ignorant men, the essence of this work has not flourished in the world. Following this, there is Chao Yuanfang. Although his later compilation "Disease Sources" may not be unobservant, it primarily discusses the two qi of wind and cold, without addressing the principles of dampness and heat, which is its shortcoming. Then we have Wang Bing, who proposed the changes in the five movements and six qi and wrote "Tian Yuan Yu Ce," which is detailed and intricate, yet difficult for people to grasp; if one becomes muddled in it, they will be stagnant and unable to make progress. Lastly, we have Wang Tao and Sun Simiao.[98p]

思邈以绝人之识，操慈仁恻隐之心，其叙《千金》方、翼，及粗工害人之祸，至为愤切，后人稍闯其藩垣，亦足以术鸣，但不制伤寒之书，或不能无遗憾也。焘虽阐明《外台秘要》，所言方证符禁灼灸之详，颇有所袓迷，然谓针能杀生人而不能起死人者，则一偏之见也。又下此则钱乙、庞安时、许叔微。叔微在准绳尺寸之中，而无所发明；安时虽能出奇应变，而终未离于范围，二人皆得张机之粗者也。惟乙深造机之阃奥，而撷其精华，建为五脏之方，各随所宜，谓肝有相火则有泻而无补，肾为真水则有补而无泻，皆启《内经》之秘，尤知者之所取法也。[99p]

Si Miao, with unparalleled discernment, wielded a heart of compassion and empathy. His discussions on the "Thousand Gold" remedies and the dangers of unskilled practice elicited strong emotions. Subsequent generations, although they breached his boundaries, could still echo his teachings; however, those who do not establish texts on the treatment of cold ailments may indeed face regrets.

Tao, though he elucidated the "Essentials of the Outer Treasury," contained some confusion in his explanations pertaining to the precise restrictions of cautery and moxibustion. He held a rather narrow view in stating that needles can harm the living but cannot revive the dead. Below him were Qian Yi, Pang Anshi, and Xu Shuwei. Xu Shuwei adhered to established standards but did not make any innovations. Anshi, despite his skill in unconventional responses, ultimately remained within conventional limits; both were merely adept in the rudiments of acupuncture.

Only Yi delved deeply into the profound principles of this art, extracting its essence and establishing remedies tailored for the five organs, according to their specific needs. He asserted that the liver, having excess fire, should be purged rather than supplemented, while the kidneys, representing true water, should be supported without purging. These insights revealed the mysteries of the "Inner Canon," particularly the methods known to the knowledgeable.[99p]

世概以婴孺医目之，何其知乙之浅哉？其遗书散亡，出于阎孝忠所集者，多孝忠之意，初非乙之本真也。又下此则上谷张元素、河间刘完素、睢水张从政。元素之与完素，虽设为奇梦异人以神其授受，实闻乙之风而兴起焉者。若从政则又宗乎完素者也。元素以古方今病决不能相值，治病一切不以方，故其书亦不传，其有存于今者，皆后来之所附会，其学则东垣李杲深得之。杲推明内外二伤，而多注意于补脾土之说。盖以土为一身之主，土平则诸脏平矣。从政以吐汗下三法，风寒暑湿燥火六门，为医之关键，其治多攻利，不善学者杀人。完素论风火之病，以《内经》病机气宜一十九条着为《原病式》，阃奥粹微，有非大观官局诸医所可髴仿。[100p]

The world classifies medicine through the lens of infants, how shallow is its understanding! The texts that have been lost, collected by Yan Xiaozhong, largely reflect his own intentions and are not the true essence of this knowledge. Below him are Zhang Yuan Su from Shanggu, Liu Wansuo from Hejian, and Zhang Congzheng from Suishui. Although Yuan Su and Wansuo created strange dreams and unusual figures to mystify their teachings, they actually rose in response to the trends of this knowledge. As for Congzheng, he is again aligned with Wansuo.

Yuan Su, using ancient methods to address modern ailments, found that they simply could not correlate; he treated diseases without relying on established formulas, which is why his writings have not been transmitted. Those that exist today are all later interpretations. His academic lineage is traced through Li Gao of Dongyuan, who absorbed these teachings deeply.

Gao clarified the inner and outer injuries and focused much on the theory of nourishing the spleen as the foundation. He believed that soil is the master of the body; when the soil is balanced, all organs are at peace. Congzheng advocated for treatments including inducing sweating, utilizing three methods, and addressed the six factors of wind, cold, heat, dampness, dryness, and fire as crucial to medicine. His approach often relied on aggressive treatment strategies, which, if misapplied by the unwise, could lead to death.

Wansuo discussed diseases of wind and fire, with the "Inner Canon" delineating the pathogen mechanisms in a way that is encapsulated in nineteen criteria forming the "Original Disease Methods." The profound intricacies therein are not something the common physicians in great institutions can easily imitate.[100p]

究其设施，则亦不越攻补二者之间也。近代名医，若吴中罗天天　原脱，据文义补。益，沧洲吕复，皆承东垣之余绪。武林罗知悌、丹溪朱彦修，各挹完素之流风。又若台之朱佐、越之滑寿，咸有著述，未易枚举。嗟乎！自有《内经》以来，医书之藏有司者，凡一百七十九家，二百有九部，一千二百五十九卷，亦不为不多矣。若夫历代名医出处，举其最者言之耳，岂能悉具于斯乎？　　或问：医学授受之源，既得闻命矣，未审吾子之学，何所适从？传曰：医不三世，不服其药。或谓，袓父相承谓之三世；或谓：善读三世之书，则为三世之医。[101p]

In examining its foundations, it mainly revolves around the dual aspects of attack and supplementation. Among modern famous physicians, there are those like Luo Tiantian from Wuzhong, who interpret and supplement based on the textual meanings, and Lü Fu from Cangzhou, who carries on the legacy of Dongyuan. Luo Zhiti from Wulin and Zhu Yanxiu from Danxi both draw from the refined essence of their predecessors. Additionally, there are writers like Zhu Zuo from Taiwan and Hua Shou from Yue, whose contributions are numerous and not easily listed. Alas! Since the time of the "Inner Canon," there have been 179 notable medical writers, 209 works, and 1,259 volumes in total, which is indeed quite a lot. However, when speaking of the most prominent physicians throughout the ages, how can one possibly enumerate them all?

Someone asked: "Since the origin of medical teachings has been clearly articulated, what path does your learning follow?" The tradition states: "A physician cannot adhere to the medicine of three generations." Some interpret this to mean that the grandfather's teachings passed down define the three generations; others argue that mastering the texts of the three generations qualifies one as a physician of those three generations.[101p]

子读三世之书欤？为袓父相承之家学欤？请明言其故可乎？曰：草莽之学，其可云乎？然医不止于三世，而其书又奚止于三代哉？当取其可法者言之耳。予同邑丹溪朱彦修先生，上承刘、张、李三家之学，而得罗太无为之依归，以医道大鸣于当世，遐迩咸取法焉。予故增叔袓诚斋府君，幸与丹溪生同世，居同乡，于是获沾亲炙之化，亦以英术鸣世。故予袓父相承，家传之学，有所自来。予惟愧夫才疏质钝，而不能奉扬箕裘之业为憾耳。奚足道哉！　　或问：人之寿夭，各有天命存焉。凡人有生必有花，自古皆然。医何益乎？曰：夫所谓天命者，天地父母之元气也。父为天，母为地。父精母血，盛衰不同，故人之寿夭亦异。[102p]

Did you read the books of the three generations? Is this knowledge passed down from your grandfather's family? Please clarify the reason for this. The response is: Can we speak of the trivial knowledge from the wilds? Yet medicine does not stop at the three generations; what, then, limits its texts to the three dynasties? We should only speak of what is legitimate and applicable. I have a fellow townsman, Mr. Zhu Yanxiu of Danxi, who inherited the teachings of the Liu, Zhang, and Li families, and found his foundation in the works of Luo Taiwuwei, thus his medical skills have resonated widely in the present era, with people far and near taking him as a model. As for me, I am fortunate to be a contemporary of Mr. Danxi, sharing the same hometown with my ancestor, Lord Chengzhai, and therefore I also benefit from his guidance, gaining a reputation for my skills. Thus, the family tradition that I inherit has a lineage. I can only feel regret that my talent is dull and insufficient, and I cannot elevate the legacy of my forebears; this is my sorrow. What more is there to say? Someone asked: The lifespan of a person, whether long or short, is predetermined by fate. Every life must have its flowering; this has been true since ancient times. How does medicine help? The response is: What is called fate refers to the original qi of heaven and earth, the parents. The father represents heaven, the mother represents earth. The essence of the father and the blood of the mother vary in their abundance and decline, therefore, the lifespan and early death of people also differ.[102p]

其有生之初，受气之两盛者，当得上中之寿；受气之偏盛者，当得中下之寿；受气之两衰者，能保养仅得下寿，不然多夭折。虽然，又不可以常理拘泥论也。或风寒暑湿之感于外，饥饱劳役之伤乎内，岂能一一尽乎所禀之元气耶？故上古神农氏尝百草，制医药，乃欲扶植乎生民，各得尽乎天年也。今野人有不信医而信巫枉死者，皆不得尽乎正命，而与岩墙桎梏死者何异焉！或曰：今之推命者，皆以所生日时之天上星辰推算，其生死安危，无不节节应验。子以父母之元气为天命，恐非至当之语。曰：天人之理，盛衰无不吻合，如河出图，洛出书，圣人取以画八卦而成《易》书。[103p]

At the beginning of life, those who are influenced by balanced energies will achieve a long life; those influenced by imbalanced energies will have a shorter life; and those whose energies are waning can only preserve a brief existence, or else they face early death. Nevertheless, one must not merely adhere to common logic. External influences such as wind, cold, heat, and dampness, as well as internal injuries from hunger, overeating, or overwork, cannot be entirely governed by one's inherent vital energy. Thus, in ancient times, the divine farmer experimented with various herbs and created medicine to support the populace, allowing each to reach their natural lifespan. Nowadays, there are those who, despite rejecting physicians in favor of shamans, end up dying prematurely, showing no difference from those shackled by sheer rock walls! Some say that present-day fortune-tellers calculate destinies based on celestial bodies at the time of birth, with occurrences of life and death reflecting these stars in an orderly manner. However, using the parents' vital energies as a measure of heavenly mandate may not be the most appropriate perspective. It is said that the principles of heaven and humanity always correspond, just as the river diagrams and Luo charters reveal — the sages used them to draw the hexagrams and compile the Book of Changes.[103p]

凡人之一动一静，与夫吉凶消长之理，进退存亡之道，用之以卜筮，毫发无差。虽然，圣贤谆谆教诲，必使尽人事以副天意，则凶者化吉，亡者得存，未尝令人委之于天命也。传曰：修身以俟命而已矣。是故医者可以通神明而权造化，能使夭者寿而寿者仙。医道其可废乎？　　或问：古者医家有禁咒一科，今何不用？曰：禁咒科者，即《素问》祝由科也。立教于龙树居士，为移精变气之术耳，可治小病。或男女入神庙惊惑成病，或山林溪谷冲着恶气，其证如醉如痴，此为邪鬼所附，一切心神惶惑之证，可以借咒语，以解惑安和而已。古有龙树咒法之书行于世，今流而为师巫、为降童、为师婆，而为扇惑人民哄吓取财之术。噫！邪术为邪人用之，知理者勿用也。[104p]

In every movement and stillness of a person, there lies the principle of good and bad fortune, the paths of progress and retreat, existence and extinction, which can be used for divination with precision. However, the wise and virtuous instruct us to exhaust human effort in accordance with heavenly will, so that misfortunes can be transformed into fortunes, and those who are lost may gain life. They never advocate leaving everything to fate. It is said: "Cultivate oneself and wait for destiny." Therefore, a physician can connect with the divine and influence transformation, capable of extending the life of the prematurely dying and allowing the long-lived to attain immortality. Can the art of medicine be disregarded?

Some ask: In ancient times, physicians had the discipline of spells and incantations; why is it not utilized today? I reply: The discipline of spells refers to the "Su Wen" healing methods. Established by the scholar Longshu, it pertains to techniques for transforming essence and spirit, capable of treating minor ailments. Sometimes, men and women entering temples become bewildered and ill, or they may encounter malevolent energies in mountains and valleys, presenting symptoms akin to drunkenness or confusion, which are caused by evil spirits attaching themselves. For all symptoms of mental distress, spells can be used to resolve confusion and restore peace. In ancient times, the spell methods of Longshu circulated widely, but today they have morphed into practices of sorcery, trickery, and manipulation, cheating the people. Alas! Malicious arts are employed by malicious individuals; those who understand the principles should not use them.[104p]

　　　　明医杂着 【明　王纶】

　　　　　医论

　　或问：仲景、东垣、河间、丹溪诸书孰优？学之宜何主？曰：宜专主《内经》而博观乎四子，斯无弊矣。盖医之有《内经》，犹儒道之六经，无所不备；四子之说，则犹《学》、《庸》、《语》、《孟》，为六经之阶梯，不可缺一者也。四子之书，初无优劣，但各发明一义耳。仲景见《内经》载伤寒，而其变迁反复之未备也，故着论立方以尽其变。[105p]

Medical Discourse

Someone asked: Among the writings of Zhang Zhongjing, Dongyuan, Hejian, and Danxi, which is superior? Which should one focus on studying? The answer is: One should primarily concentrate on the "Inner Canon" while broadly observing the teachings of the four masters; this way, there will be no faults. The "Inner Canon" is to medicine what the Six Classics are to Confucianism and Daoism, as it encompasses everything; the teachings of the four masters are like the "Xue," "Yong," "Yue," and "Meng," serving as steps to the Six Classics, and none can be omitted. There is no distinction of superiority or inferiority among the four works; rather, each elucidates one particular principle. Zhang Zhongjing, noting that the "Inner Canon" contains references to cold damage but lacks a comprehensive account of its variations and transformations, therefore authored his writings to elaborate on these changes.[105p]

后人宗之，传用既久，渐失其真，用以通治温暑内伤诸证，遂致误人，故河间出而始发明治温暑之法，东垣出而始发明治内伤之法。河间之论，即《内经》五运六气之旨；东垣之说，即《内经》饮食劳倦之义。仲景非不知温暑与内伤也，特其著书未之及。河间、东垣之于伤寒，则遵用仲景而莫敢违矣。至于丹溪出而又集诸医之大成，发明阴虚发热类乎外感内伤，及湿热相火为病甚多，随证着论，亦不过阐《内经》之要旨，补前贤之未备耳。故曰：外感法仲景，内伤法东垣，热病用河间，杂病用丹溪。一以贯之，斯医道之大全矣。　　或问：仲景处方药品甚少，及东垣用药多至二十余味。[106p]

Later generations have revered these teachings, and as they have been passed down for so long, the true essence has gradually been lost. They were used to address the ailments caused by heat and cold, leading to misguidance. Thus, the Hejian school emerged, beginning to elucidate methods for treating heat illnesses, and the Dongyuan school emerged, starting to clarify methods for treating internal injuries. The theories from Hejian are in line with the principles of the "Neijing" regarding the five movements and six energies; the teachings from Dongyuan align with the "Neijing" concerning diet and fatigue. It is not that Zhongjing was unaware of the relationship between heat illnesses and internal injuries, but rather that he had not covered these topics in his writings. When it comes to treating cold damage, both Hejian and Dongyuan strictly follow Zhongjing’s principles and dare not deviate. Later, Danxi synthesized the contributions of various physicians, elucidating conditions of Yin deficiency leading to fever and the many diseases resulting from damp heat and internal fire, also documenting these according to symptoms, ultimately just expounding upon the essential meanings of the "Neijing" and supplementing what previous sages had overlooked. Therefore, it is said: for external pathogens, one follows Zhongjing; for internal injuries, one follows Dongyuan; for heat disease, one employs Hejian; for miscellaneous ailments, one turns to Danxi. To integrate these approaches constitutes the comprehensive path of medicine.

Some may question: Zhongjing's prescriptions use very few medicinal ingredients, while Dongyuan’s often exceed twenty.[106p]

丹溪云：余每治病，效东垣用药，效仲景处方，庶品味数少则药力专。丹溪何以不法东垣而效仲景耶？曰：明察药性，莫如东垣，盖所谓圣于医者也。故在东垣则可多，他人而效其多，斯杂乱矣。东垣如韩信将兵，多多益善。丹溪不过能将十万，故不敢效其多。　　或问：人言东南气热，可服寒药；西北气寒，可服温药。然今东南之人，常服胡椒姜桂，不见生病，而西北之人，畏食椒姜辛热之物，何也？曰：东南虽热，然地卑多湿，辛热食药，亦能劫湿；西北虽寒，然地高多燥，辛热食药，却能助燥故耳。[107p]

Dan Xi said: Whenever I treat illness, I follow the methods of Dong Yuan in using medicine and the prescriptions of Zhong Jing, hoping that by limiting the dosage, the efficacy of the medicine will be concentrated. Why then does Dan Xi not follow Dong Yuan and instead emulate Zhong Jing? It is said: In understanding the properties of medicine, no one surpasses Dong Yuan; he is truly the sage among physicians. Therefore, in Dong Yuan's case, it is acceptable to use more. If others emulate this excess, it can lead to confusion. Dong Yuan, like Han Xin in commanding troops, believes that more is better. However, Dan Xi can only manage a hundred thousand, thus does not dare to imitate the excess.

Someone asked: It is said that in the southeast where the climate is hot, one can take cooling medications; in the northwest where the climate is cold, one can take warming medications. Yet, people in the southeast often consume pepper, ginger, and cassia without falling ill, while those in the northwest avoid spicy and warming foods. Why is this? It is said: Although the southeast is hot, the land is low and often damp, and spicy, warming foods and medicines can also help eliminate dampness; while the northwest, although cold, has high and dry terrain, thus spicy, warming foods and medicines can help combat dryness.[107p]

治病用药者，须识此意！　　丹溪先生治病不出乎气血痰，故用药之要有三：气用四君子汤，血用四物汤，痰用二陈汤。又云：久病属郁，立治郁之方，曰越鞠丸。盖气血痰三病多有兼郁者，或郁久而生病，或病久而生郁，或误药杂乱而成郁，故余每用此方治病时，以郁法参之。气病兼郁，则用四君子加开郁药，血病痰病皆然。故四法者，治病用药之大要也。丹溪又云：近世治病，多不知分气血，但见虚病便用参芪，属气虚者固宜矣，若是血虚，岂不助气而反耗阴血耶？是谓血病治气，则血愈虚耗，甚而至于气血俱虚。[108p]

To treat illnesses with medication, one must understand this principle! Master Danxi treats diseases without departing from the concepts of qi, blood, and phlegm, therefore the key to using medicine lies in three formulations: for qi, use the Four Gentleman Decoction; for blood, use the Four Substance Decoction; for phlegm, use the Erchen Decoction. Furthermore, he states that prolonged illness leads to stagnation, hence the necessity to adopt methods to treat stagnation, such as the Yueju Pill. It can be said that the three ailments of qi, blood, and phlegm often coexist with stagnation, whether stagnation arises from prolonged illness, illness causes prolonged stagnation, or misprescriptions lead to stagnation. Consequently, whenever I treat illnesses, I always incorporate methods for stagnation. If an ailment of qi accompanies stagnation, then add an herb to resolve stagnation to the Four Gentleman Decoction; the same applies for ailments of blood and phlegm. Therefore, these four methods are essential for treating illnesses with medicine. Master Danxi also notes that in recent times, many who treat diseases do not distinguish between qi and blood, merely noticing a deficiency in qi and then using ginseng and astragalus. This is indeed appropriate for qi deficiency; however, if it is blood deficiency, does this not bolster qi while depleting yin-blood? This is referred to as treating blood ailments with qi, which aggravates the depletion of blood and can lead to both qi and blood becoming deficient.[108p]

故治病用药，须要分别气血明白，不可混淆。　　昔人有云：我但卧病，即于胸前不时手写死字，则百般思虑俱息，此心便得安静，胜于服药。此真无上妙方也。盖病而不慎，则死必至。达此理者，必能清心克己，凡百谨慎而病可获痊，否则虽有良药无救也。世人遇病而犹恣情任性以自戕贼者，是固不知畏死者矣。又有一等明知畏死而怕人知觉，讳而不言，或病已重而犹强作轻浅态度以欺人者，斯又知畏死而反以取死，尤可笑也。[109p]

Therefore, when treating illness with medicine, it is essential to clearly distinguish between Qi and blood; they must not be confused. In ancient times, it was said: when I am ill, if I write the character for death in front of my chest from time to time, all my thoughts will cease, and my heart will find peace, which is better than taking medicine. This is truly a supreme method. If one is not cautious in illness, then death is inevitable. Those who understand this principle can cultivate a clear mind and self-discipline, being attentive in all matters, and they can recover from illness. Otherwise, even with good medicine, there will be no remedy. People often encounter illness yet indulge in their emotions and recklessness, harming themselves; this shows they do not truly fear death. There are also those who, knowing they fear death, are afraid of others discovering it. They remain silent about their condition or feign a light attitude even when seriously ill to deceive others; this is knowing the fear of death yet inadvertently courting it, which is particularly absurd.[109p]

　　东垣、丹溪治病多自制方，盖二公深明本草药性，洞究《内经》处方要法，故能自制。自宋以来，《局方》盛行，人皆遵用，不敢轻率自为。《局方》论证治病，虽多差谬，丹溪曾辨论之。然方皆名医所制，其君臣佐使，轻重缓急，大小多寡之法，则不差也。近见东垣、丹溪之书大行，世医见其不用古方也，率皆效颦治病，辄自制方。然药性不明，处方之法莫究，卤莽乱杂，反致生变，甚有变证多端，遂难识治耳。且夫药之气味不同，如五味子之味厚，故东垣方少者五六粒，多者十数粒，今世医或用二三钱。石膏味淡薄，故白虎汤用半两，今世医不敢多用。[110p]

Dongyuan and Danxi often created their own prescriptions for treating illnesses, as both scholars had a deep understanding of the properties of medicinal herbs and an in-depth knowledge of the essential methods from the "Inner Canon." Since the Song Dynasty, pre-made prescriptions have been widely adopted, and people have not dared to create their own with rashness. Although the arguments in the pre-made prescriptions often contain many inaccuracies, Danxi has debated these points. Nevertheless, the formulas are all composed by renowned physicians, and their hierarchical structure, dosage, urgency, and proportions are generally accurate. Recently, the works of Dongyuan and Danxi have become popular, and contemporary doctors, noticing that they do not use ancient formulas, tend to imitate their practices and create their own prescriptions. However, lacking clarity on the properties of the medicines and not comprehending the reasoning behind the prescriptions, they end up causing confusion, with varied and troublesome symptoms arising, making it difficult to recognize and treat them. Furthermore, since different medicines have distinct flavors, for instance, Schisandra has a thick flavor, Dongyuan's prescription calls for five or six grains at a minimum and up to a dozen at most, whereas contemporary doctors may use two or three taels. Gypsum has a light flavor, thus Baihu Decoction uses half a tael, while modern doctors do not dare to use more.[110p]

补上治上，济宜轻小。今不论上下，率用大剂。丸散汤液，各有攸宜。今不论缓急，率用汤煎。如此类者多矣。今之医者，若不熟读《本草》，深究《内经》，而轻自制方，鲜不误人也。　　或问：今人有言东垣之法宜用于北，丹溪之法可行于南，如何？曰：东垣，北医也，罗谦甫传其法，以闻于江浙。丹溪，南医也，刘宗厚世其学，以鸣于陕西。果如人言，则《本草》、《内经》，皆神农黄帝岐伯之说，亦止宜施于北方耶？夫五行所生异病，及治之异宜，《内经》异法方宜论、五常政大论，已详言之矣。[111p]

To supplement the treatment, one should focus on light and minor ailments. Currently, regardless of condition severity, large doses are commonly used. Pills, powders, soups, and liquids each have their specific suitability. Now, regardless of urgency, decoctions are generally the preferred method. Such occurrences are numerous. Today’s physicians, if not well-versed in the "Materia Medica" and deeply studied the "Inner Canon," while lightly creating their own prescriptions, rarely avoid causing harm.

Someone asked: It is said that Dongyuan's methods are suitable for the North and Danxi's methods can be applied in the South. How should this be interpreted? The reply: Dongyuan is a Northern physician, and Luo Qianfu transmitted his teachings, gaining recognition in Jiangsu and Zhejiang. Danxi is a Southern physician, and Liu Zonghou carried on his teachings, becoming well-known in Shaanxi. If this were as they say, would it mean that the "Materia Medica" and the "Inner Canon," the teachings of Shen Nong and Huang Di as well as Qi Bo, are only applicable in the North? However, the Five Elements give rise to different diseases, and their treatments require distinct approaches; the "Inner Canon" has already thoroughly discussed these varied methods and the principles of the Five Constants.[111p]

又如北方多寒，南方多热，江湖多湿，岭南多瘴，谓其得此气多，故亦多生此病，非谓北病无热，南病无寒也。至于治寒以热，治热以寒，则五方皆同，岂有南北之异耶？但人之脏腑，火各居二，天之六气，热居三分又半，故天下之病，热多而寒少。观《内经》至真大论病机一篇可见。又湿热相火，致病甚多，自太仆注文湮没，以致《局方》偏用湿热之药，故丹溪出而阐《内经》之旨，辨《局方》之偏，论湿热相火之病，以补前人之未备耳。后人不识，见其多用芩、连、栀、蘗等苦寒之药，遂以为宜于南，浅矣哉！[112p]

Just as the north is often cold and the south is often hot, the rivers and lakes are very humid, while Lingnan is prone to miasma. This is attributed to the prevalence of certain climatic influences, resulting in the rise of various diseases in these regions—not because northern diseases lack heat or southern diseases lack cold. Regarding the treatment of cold with heat and heat with cold, the principles are the same across the five directions; how could there be a difference between north and south? However, within human organs, fire occupies two places, while in the six atmospheric influences of heaven, heat accounts for three and a half parts. Therefore, illnesses in the world are characterized by more heat and less cold. This can be seen in the chapter on disease mechanisms in the "Inner Canon." Additionally, the combination of dampness and heat often leads to many diseases. Since the writings of the Grand Minister have fallen into obscurity, the "Jufang" has resorted to the common use of damp-heat remedies. Hence, Danxi emerged to elucidate the teachings of the "Inner Canon," clarify the biases of the "Jufang," and discuss the diseases caused by the interplay of dampness and heat to address the inadequacies of previous scholars. Later generations, not understanding this, regarded the frequent use of bitter and cold medicines like qin, lian, zhi, and ye as appropriate for the south, which is a shallow interpretation![112p]

　　　　柏斋三书

　　　　　治疗须法《素问》

　　凡治病者，必明天道地理，人之强弱；必问起居饮食，暴乐暴苦。病从内而之外者，先治其内。后治其外：病从外而之内者，先治其外，后治其内。内外不相及，则治主病。急则治其标，缓则治其本，先其所因而后其所主。补上治下制以缓，补下治上制以急。大毒治病十去其六，中毒治病十去其七，小毒治病十去其八，无毒治病十去其九。必养必和，俟其来复。此皆《素问》之文，而医家之所当知也，不能尽录，姑撮其要，以示其概云。
　　　　原机启微 【元　倪维德[元　倪维德　原作无名氏，据本书医术名流列传卷五百十倪维德改。按倪氏生于元大德七年（公元年），卒于明洪武十年（公元年），本书列为明代医家，或因取材于《明外史》之故。]】[113p]

The Three Books of Baizhai

Methods of Treatment - "Suwen"

In treating illnesses, one must understand the natural laws and geographical conditions, as well as the strength and weakness of the person. It is essential to inquire about daily activities, diet, and extreme pleasures or sufferings. For illnesses originating from within, one must first address the internal causes before treating the external symptoms. Conversely, if the illness comes from external factors, one must first treat the exterior before addressing the interior. If internal and external do not interact, then treat the primary ailment. If urgency arises, treat the superficial symptoms; if the situation is more relaxed, treat the root cause first. Address the related causes before the primary ones. To alleviate urgent conditions, supplement the lower ones to control the upper, and for urgent cases below, supplement the upper to control the lower. For severe poisons, treat ten cases with six remedies; for moderate poisons, treat ten cases with seven remedies; for minor poisons, treat ten cases with eight remedies; and for non-toxic conditions, treat ten cases with nine remedies. It is essential to nourish and harmonize, waiting for recovery. These principles are all derived from the "Suwen" text and are essential knowledge for medical practitioners; they cannot be fully recorded here, so I summarize the key points to illustrate their essence.[113p]

　　　　　序

　　医为儒者之一事，不知何代而两途之。父母至亲者，有疾而委之他人，俾他人之无亲者，反操父母之死生，一有误谬，则终身不复。平日以仁推于人者，独不能以仁推于父母乎？故于仁缺。朋友以义合，故赴其难，难虽水火兵革弗顾；故周其急，急虽金玉粟帛弗吝。或疾则曰素不审，他者曰甲审遂求甲者，渠曰乙审又更乙者，纷纷错扰，竟不能辨，此徒能周赴于疮痍，而不能携友于死生也，故于义缺。己身以爱为主。饮食滋味。必欲美也；衣服玩好，必欲佳也；嗣上续下，不敢轻也。疾至而不识，任之妇人女子也，任之宗戚朋友也，任之狂巫瞽卜也，至危犹不能辨药误病焉也，故于知缺。夫五常之中，三缺而不备，故为儒者不可不兼夫医也。故曰：医为儒者之一事。[114p]

Preface

Medicine is one of the matters of the Confucian scholar, and it is unclear at what point it diverged into two paths. When one’s parents or close relatives fall ill, they entrust their care to others, allowing those without such kinship to take charge of life and death. If there is any mistake, the consequences could last a lifetime. Those who advocate for benevolence to others are unable to apply that same benevolence towards their parents; thus, they fall short in compassion. Friends unite through righteousness, willing to face difficulties, irrespective of the risk—be it water, fire, or warfare; hence, they do not hesitate in urgent matters, regardless of material loss, be it gold, jade, grain, or silk. When one is ill, they might say that they do not understand the ailment; then others claim to thoroughly understand and seek treatment that may not align, each offering conflicting opinions, leading to confusion and inability to discern correctly. They may only be able to address external wounds, yet cannot guide friends through life and death situations; therefore, they lack righteousness. They prioritize personal desires: longing for delicious food, insisting on fine clothing and ornaments, and being careful about offspring and lineage. Yet when sickness arises, they are oblivious, leaving matters to women, relatives, and friends, or to crazed shamans and blind fortune-tellers, and even in dire situations they fail to distinguish between correct and erroneous treatments; thus, they lack knowledge. Among the Five Constants, with three missing, it is imperative for a Confucian to also engage in medicine. Hence it is said: Medicine is one of the matters of the Confucian scholar.[114p]

　　　　医说 【宋　张杲[宋　张杲　原缺，据本书医术名流列传卷五百八张季明补。]】
　　　　　医药之难

　　五经四部，军国礼服，若讲用乖越者，止于事迹非宜耳。至于汤药一物，少有乖谬，便性命及之。千乘之君，百金之长，可不深思戒慎耶？昔许太子侍药不尝，加以弒君之罪；季康子馈药，仲尼有未达之辞，知其医药之不可轻也。晋时才人欲刊正《周易》及诸药方，先与袓讷共论辩释经典，纵有异同，不足以伤风教，至于汤药，小小不达，便致寿夭所由，则后人受弊不少，何可轻以裁断？袓之此言，可谓仁识，足为龟鉴矣。
　　　　　医通神明

　　凡为医者，须略通古今，粗守仁义，绝驰骛利名之心，专博施救援之志，如此则心识自明，神物来相，又何戚戚沽名龊龊求利也？如不然，则曷止姜抚沽誉之惭逮，华佗之矜能受戮乎。[115p]

The Challenges of Medicine

The Five Classics and Four Books, as well as military and national rites and clothing, if discussed with inconsistencies, are merely matters of conduct. However, when it comes to medicinal herbs and prescriptions, even a slight mistake can endanger life. A ruler of a thousand chariots or a wealthy leader of a hundred pieces of gold should surely think deeply and exercise caution. In the past, when Prince Xu served medicine without tasting it, he was charged with murdering his ruler. When Ji Kangzi offered medicine, Confucius expressed uncertainty, knowing well that medicine should not be taken lightly. During the Jin dynasty, a talented individual wished to revise the "I Ching" and various prescriptions, first discussing and debating the classics with Zunuo. Even with differences, they were not enough to damage moral teachings. However, when it comes to medicine, a minor error can lead to life or death; thus, future generations suffer greatly. How can we treat this lightly?

The Essence of a Healer

Any practitioner of medicine must have a general understanding of both ancient and contemporary knowledge, adhere to benevolence and righteousness, and eliminate the desire for fame and profit. One should dedicate oneself to relief and assistance. If this is achieved, one’s understanding will naturally become clear, and spiritual insights will come forth. Why then be anxious to seek fame and engage in petty profit? If not, how could one avoid the shame of Jiang who was too eager for reputation or of Hua Tuo who, despite his skills, met his demise?[115p]

　　　　　医不贪色

　　宣和间，有一士人抱病缠年，百治不瘥。有何澄者善医，其妻请到，引入密室，告之曰：妾以良人抱疾日久，典卖殆尽，无以供医药，愿以身酬。澄正色曰：娘子何为出此言！但放心，当为调治取效，切毋以此相污。不有人诛，必有鬼神谴责。未几，良人疾愈。何澄一夜梦入神祠，判官语之曰：汝医药有功，不于艰急之际，以色欲为贪，上帝令赐钱五万贯，官一员。未几月，东宫疾，国医不能治，有诏召草泽医，澄应诏进剂而愈，朝廷赐官赐钱一如梦。
　　　　　隐医

　　医之为道，由来尚矣。原百病之愈，本乎黄帝；辨百药之味，本乎神农；汤液则本乎伊尹。此三圣人者，拯黎元之疾苦，赞天地之化育，其有功于万世大矣。万世之下，深于此道者，是亦圣人之徒也。贾谊曰：古之圣人，不居朝廷，必隐于医卜。孰谓方技之中而无豪杰也哉？[116p]

The Physician's Restraint from Lust

During the Xuanhe period, there was a scholar who suffered from a chronic illness and could not find a cure despite numerous treatments. A man named He Cheng was skilled in medicine. His wife requested to see him, leading him into a private chamber and said, “I have seen my husband suffer for a long time, and we have nearly sold everything we own to pay for his treatments. I am willing to offer myself in exchange.” He Cheng sternly replied, “Madam, why would you speak in such a manner? Please rest assured, I will treat your husband and achieve results; do not tarnish yourself in this way. If there exists no man who would issue punishment, certainly there will be retribution from gods and spirits.” Before long, the husband recovered. One night, He Cheng dreamt he entered a shrine, where a judge spoke to him, saying, “Your medical skills have succeeded. At a time of great urgency, you did not succumb to lust or greed. The Supreme Deity decrees you a reward of fifty thousand coins and a government official post.” A few months later, the Eastern Palace fell ill, and the imperial physicians could not cure it. An edict summoned grassroots doctors, and He Cheng responded, bringing medicine that ensured recovery. The court granted him the same official position and compensation as he had dreamt.

The Hidden Physician

The way of medicine has long existed. The healing of illnesses originates from the Yellow Emperor; discerning the flavors of countless herbs comes from Shen Nong; the concoction of decoctions is attributed to Yi Yin. These three sages alleviated the suffering of the common people and contributed to the nurturing of heaven and earth, thus their contributions to future generations are immensely significant. Among all the ages, those who delve deeply into this path are also disciples of the sages. Jia Yi stated: "The sages of old did not dwell in the court; they must have hidden themselves among physicians and diviners." Who says there are no heroes among those skilled in techniques?[116p]

　　　　　治病委之庸医比之不慈不孝

　　夫医之道大矣！自神农、黄帝、岐伯、雷公而下，无非圣哲开其源，贤知导其流，故能拯黎元之疾苦，赞天地之生育。世道既降，士大夫以此为技艺，不屑为之，而畀之凡流。是以至精至微之理，而出于至卑至贱之思，其不能起人之疾，反以夭其命者多矣。此范文正公所以自谓：不为良相，则为良医。伊川先生有云治病而委之庸医，比之不慈不孝。自昔卓然名家者，如和缓、扁鹊、淳于意、张仲景、孙真人等，其论医也，莫不以保养为先，药石为辅。至于察形诊脉，必致辩于毫芒疑似之末。而深痛夫世之医者，苟简虚憍，顾乃以医之伐病，如将之伐敌，当用背水阵以决胜。是徒见夫华佗之说，时出其间，致于剖臆续筋之法，乃别术所得，非《神农本草》经方条理药性常道尔。今惟仲景一书，为众方之袓，学者当取法云。[117p]

The art of healing is vast! From the time of Shennong, Huangdi, Qibo, and Leigong, it has been nothing but sages and philosophers opening its sources and wise individuals guiding its flow, thus able to alleviate the sufferings of the common people and support the creation of the heavens and earth. As the world has degraded, the scholars and gentry regard this as merely a skill and disdain it, handing it over to the common people. Therefore, the most subtle and profound principles have emerged from the most humble and base thoughts, resulting in many who cannot heal the sick and instead hasten their demise. This is why Fan Wenzheng declared that if one cannot be a good minister, then one should be a good doctor. Mr. Yichuan stated that entrusting treatment of illnesses to mediocre physicians is tantamount to being unkind and unfilial.

Since ancient times, renowned medical practitioners like He Huan, Bian Que, Chunyu Yi, Zhang Zhongjing, and Sun Zhenren consistently emphasized preservation of health as the priority, with medicine as a supplement. Regarding the observation of symptoms and diagnosis of pulses, they would meticulously discern even the most subtle doubts. It is lamentable that today’s physicians simplify the complexities, treating illness as if it were an enemy to be vanquished, much like strategizing for battle. They merely glimpse the teachings of Hua Tuo, who discusses methods such as dissecting and reconnecting tendons, which are just specialized techniques and not the fundamental principles of the "Shennong Bencao" or classical formulas of established medicinal properties. Today, only Zhang Zhongjing's works serve as the model for numerous formulas, and scholars should adhere to his teachings.[117p]

　　　　续医说 【明　俞弁[明　俞弁　原缺，据文补]】
　　　　　处方贵简

　　医者，识脉方能识病。病与药对，古人惟用一药治之，气纯而功愈速。今之人不识病源，不辨脉理，药品数多，每至十五六味，攻补杂施，弗能专力，故治病难为功也。韩天爵云：处方正不必多品，但看仲景方何等简便。丹溪云：东垣用药如用兵，多多益善者，盖讳之也。[118p]

The Continuation of Medical Sayings

Doctors must understand the pulse to recognize illnesses. In ancient times, they only used one medicine to treat a condition; the medicine was pure, and the effect was swift. Nowadays, people do not identify the source of the disease or differentiate pulse patterns, leading to a multitude of medicines—often fifteen or sixteen ingredients—mixed between strengthening and reducing treatments, which makes achieving efficacy difficult. Han Tianjue stated: there is no need for numerous ingredients in a prescription; just look at how simple and convenient Zhang Zhongjing’s formulas are. Danxi mentioned that using medicine is like waging war; more is not necessarily better, as this often leads to confusion.[118p]

　　　　　医贵权变

　　王文忠公云：李明之弟子多在中州，独刘守真之学传之荆山浮屠师。师至江南，传之宋中人罗之悌，而南方之医皆宗之矣。及国朝天下之言医者，非刘氏之学弗道也。刘李之法，虽攻补不同，会而通之，随证而用之，不存其人乎？近时吴中称良医师，则以能持东垣者谓之王道，持张刘者谓之伯道。噫！尧舜以揖让，汤武以干戈，苟合道济世，何必曰同。余常病世之专于攻伐者，邪气未退而真气先苶然矣。专于补养者，或致气道壅塞，为祸不少，正气未复而邪气愈炽矣。古人有云：药贵合宜，法当应变。泥其常者，人参反以杀人；通其变者，乌头可以活命。孙真人所谓随时增损，物无定方。真知言哉！古今图书集成医部全录卷五百三[119p]

The Value of Adaptability in Medicine

Wang Wenzhong stated: Many disciples of Li Ming are located in Zhongzhou, but only Liu Shouzheng's teachings are passed on by the Buddhist masters of Jing Mountains. When the master arrived in Jiangnan, he imparted his knowledge to Luo Zhitai of Song, and all the physicians of the South revered it. In the present dynasty, discussions about medicine do not neglect Liu's teachings. Although Liu and Li's methods differ in their approach to treatment, both can be reconciled and applied according to the situation and evidence; is there not validity in this? Recently, in Wuzhong, a good physician is identified as one who adheres to Dongyuan, while those who follow Zhang and Liu are labeled as esteemed practitioners. Alas! Yao and Shun acted with humility, while Tang and Wu resorted to warfare; as long as they aligned with the way to benefit the world, what need is there for sameness? I often lament those in the world focused solely on attack and defense, for the evil qi has not retreated while the true qi wanes. Those fixated on nourishment may also cause blockages in the qi path, resulting in considerable harm, where the true qi remains unrestored while the evil qi grows fiercer. The ancients said: Medicine is valuable when it is appropriate, and methods must be adaptable. If one adheres too strictly to the usual, ginseng may inadvertently kill; while understanding change, even Aconite can save lives. The words of Master Sun that one should adjust according to the time and circumstances are indeed wise! The complete compilation of ancient and modern medical literature, volume five hundred and three.[119p]

总论

　　　　吴文定公家藏集

　　　　　医僧庸妄治病速死

　　陈汝中尝病脾胃郁火之证，求治于盛用美，诊其脉曰：如此冶可生，如彼治即死；如此治可以少生，如彼治则致速死。既而治之，汝中迟其效。或以浮屠善医荐者，汝中惑其说，遂求冶，饮其药，呕血一升而死。噫！医以用药，药以攻病，病不能去而反以致死，则何以医药为哉？彼浮屠者，乃庸妄人也，目不识医经，口不辨药性，指不察脉候，人之虚实，病之新久，一切置之不问，而惟毒药攻击，其杀人盖亦多矣。今予咎汝中之不善择医而致速死，特书此以为世人轻服药者之戒。[120p]

General Discussion

Wen Ding Gong's Family Collection

Monks and Quacks Recklessly Treating Illnesses Leading to Rapid Death

Chen Ruzhong once suffered from a disorder of spleen and stomach due to excessive fire, seeking treatment from Sheng Yongmei. After diagnosing his pulse, he said: "If treated this way, you can live; if treated that way, you will die. This method may lead to a slight prolongation of life, but that method will cause swift death." However, after starting treatment, Ruzhong's recovery was slow. Some recommended a monk known for his medical skills, and Ruzhong was swayed by their words, thus seeking treatment from him. He took the monk's medicine and vomited a liter of blood before dying. Alas! When a physician uses medicine to combat illness, if the illness does not go away but instead leads to death, what is the purpose of medicine? That monk is merely a quack, lacking understanding of medical texts and unable to distinguish the properties of herbs, unable to observe the state of the pulse, ignoring the condition and duration of one's ailments, and only focusing on toxic substances to attack illness—his methods have likely resulted in many deaths. Now I blame Ruzhong for poorly choosing his physician, which led to his swift demise, and I write this as a caution for those who lightly take medicine.[120p]

　　　　医学集成 【明　傅滋[明　傅滋　原缺，据文补。]】
　　　　　医学须会群书之长

　　医之为道，非精不能明其理，非博不能至其约。是故前人立教，必使之先读儒书，明易理，《素》、《难》、《本草》、《脉经》而不少略者，何也？盖非四书无以通义理之精微，非《易》无以知阴阳之消长，非《素问》无以识病，非《本草》无以识药，非《脉经》无以从诊候而知寒热虚实之证。故前此数者，缺一不可。且人之生命至重，病之变化无穷，年有老幼，禀有厚薄，治分五方，令别四时，表里阴阳，寒热须辨，脏腑经络，气血宜分。六气之交伤，七情之妄发，运气变迁不常，制方缓急尤异。更复合其色脉，问其起居，证有相似，治实不同。圣贤示人，略举其端而已。后学必须会群书之长，参所见而施治之，然后为可。[121p]

Medical Integration
By Fu Zi (Ming Dynasty)

The Way of Medicine requires both precision and breadth for a clear understanding of its principles. Therefore, the sages of the past established that one must first read Confucian texts to understand the principles of change. Works such as the "Suwen," "Nanjing," "Bencao," and "Mai Jing" should not be overlooked. Why is this so? It is because without the Four Books, one cannot grasp the intricacies of meaning; without the "I Ching," one cannot comprehend the dynamics of yin and yang; without the "Suwen," one cannot recognize illnesses; without the "Bencao," one cannot identify medicines; and without the "Mai Jing," one cannot diagnose the evidences of cold, heat, deficiency, and excess based on pulse examination. Thus, these texts are indispensable.

Moreover, human life is of utmost importance, and the variations of illness are infinite. There are old and young, differences in constitution, treatments for the five directions, distinctions for the four seasons, and nuances of yin and yang, with the need to discern cold from heat. The internal organs, meridians, qi, and blood should all be categorized properly. The interplay of the six climatic energies and the erratic expressions of the seven emotions, as well as the constant changes in qi, require different approaches and varying urgencies in treatment. Additionally, one must observe characteristics in color and pulse, inquire about daily habits, and understand that although symptoms may be similar, treatments can differ significantly. The sages offer only a glimpse into these matters. Therefore, later scholars must familiarize themselves with the strengths of various texts, integrate what they observe, and apply treatment accordingly, allowing them to be capable practitioners.[121p]

　　　　九灵山房集 【元　戴良[元　戴良　原脱，据文补。]】
　　　　　医儒同道

　　金华戴叔明曰：医以活人为务，与吾儒道最切近。自唐书列之技艺，而吾儒不屑为之。世之习医者，不过诵一家之成说，守一定之方，以幸病之偶中，不复深为探索，上求圣贤之意，以明夫阴阳造化之会归；又不能博极群书，采择众议，以资论治之权变。甚者至于屏弃古方，附会臆见，展转以相迷，而其为患不少矣，是岂圣贤慈惠生民之盛意哉？[122p]

Collected Works of Jiuling Mountain House
Author: Dai Liang of the Yuan Dynasty
On the Common Path of Medicine and Confucianism

Dai Shuming of Jinhua said: "The practice of medicine is fundamentally about preserving life, which is closely aligned with our Confucian principles. Since the Tang Dynasty, medicine has been classified as a skill, yet we Confucians disdain to engage in it. Those who practice medicine in the world merely recite established doctrines from one school, adhering to fixed methods to merely chance upon a cure for illness. They no longer delve deeply into exploration, seeking the sages' intent to clarify the conjunction of yin and yang and the processes of creation; nor are they able to comprehensively study a myriad of texts, select diverse opinions, and adapt their discourse to changing circumstances in treatment.

Moreover, some go so far as to discard ancient methods, adopting speculative interpretations that lead to confusion, and their missteps are not few. Is this truly in line with the virtuous intent of sages and their compassion for the common people?"[122p]

　　　　大学衍义补 【明　丘浚[明　丘浚　原脱，据文补。]】
　　　　　国设医学

　　今世之业医者，挟技以诊疗则有之矣，求其从师以讲习者，何鲜也？我太袓内设太医院，外设府州县学，而以医学为名，盖欲聚其人，以斆学既成而试之，然后授以一方卫生之任，由是进之为国医，其嘉惠天下生民也至矣。间尝考成周所以谓之医师，国朝所以立为医学之故，精择使判以上官，聚天下习医者，俾其教之养之，读轩岐之书，研张李之技，试之通而后授之职，因其长而专其业，稽其事以制其禄，则天下之人，皆无夭阏之患，而跻仁寿之域矣。是以医为王政之一端也。[123p]

In today's world, there are those who practice medicine purely for the sake of diagnosis and treatment, but there are few who seek a mentor to learn from. Our ancestors established the Imperial Medical Academy in the inner court and set up schools in the provinces and counties to gather individuals under the name of medicine. This was intended to cultivate talent, test their mastery, and subsequently appoint them to positions of public health responsibility, thereby promoting them to become national physicians, which greatly benefits the people of the world.

Historically, during the Chengzhou era, those regarded as physicians were highly selective and appointed as important officials. The current dynasty also established the foundations of medicine by carefully selecting and assigning upper-level officials to gather and educate all those who practiced medicine. They would study the texts of Xuanqi and refine the techniques of Zhang and Li, ensuring mastery before assigning them to positions. By honing their skills and specializing in their craft, they would effectively manage affairs and determine salaries, thereby eliminating the risk of premature death or illness among the populace, allowing everyone to thrive in health and longevity.

Therefore, medicine is considered one of the essential aspects of governance.[123p]

　　　　本草衍义 【宋　寇宗奭宋　寇宗奭　原脱，据文补。】
　　　　　医有八要

　　治病有八要，八要不审，病不能去。非病不去，医无可去之术也。故须辨审八要，庶不有误。其一曰虚，五虚是也。脉细，皮寒，气少，前后泄泻，饮食不进，此为五虚。二曰实，五实是也。脉盛，皮热，腹胀，前后不通，闷瞀，此五实也。三曰冷，脏腑受其积冷是也。四曰热，脏腑受其积热是也。五曰邪，非脏腑正病也。六曰正，非外邪所中也。七曰内，病不在外也。八曰外，病不在内也。既先审此八要，参之六脉，审度所起之源，缀以望闻问切，何有不可治之疾也！[124p]

The Essentials of Medicine: Eight Key Considerations

There are eight essential points in treating illnesses; if these eight points are not carefully assessed, the illness cannot be cured. If it cannot be cured, there will be no methods available to the physician. Therefore, it is necessary to thoroughly distinguish these eight points to avoid mistakes.

First is Deficiency, which refers to the five forms of deficiency: a thin pulse, cold skin, low energy, diarrhea, and lack of appetite—these constitute the five deficiencies.

Second is Excess, which refers to the five forms of excess: a strong pulse, hot skin, abdominal distension, blockage in the front and back, and a feeling of fullness—these are the five excesses.

Third is Cold, which indicates the accumulation of cold in the internal organs.

Fourth is Heat, indicating the accumulation of heat in the internal organs.

Fifth is Pathogen, which refers to diseases not associated with the internal organs.

Sixth is Correctness, which implies that it is not an external pathogen that has entered the body.

Seventh is Internal, meaning the illness is not external.

Eighth is External, meaning the illness is not internal.

Once these eight essential points are assessed, they should be compared with the six pulses to determine their origin, and then combined with observation, listening, questioning, and pulse-taking. There should be no illness that cannot be treated![124p]

　　　　本草类方 【清　年希尧[清　年希尧　原脱，据文补。]】
　　　　　医不可用孟浪

　　夫用药如用刑，误即便隔死生。然刑有司鞫成然后议定，议定然后书罪。盖人命一死不可复生，故须如此详谨。用药亦然。今医者至病家，便以所见用药，若高医识病，知脉药相当，如此即应手奏效，或庸下之流，孟浪乱施汤剂，逡巡便至危殆，如此杀人，何太容易！良由病家不善择医，平日未尝留心于医术也，可不慎哉！[125p]

The use of medicine should not be careless.

Using medicine is like administering punishment; a mistake can lead to life or death. Just as a punishment requires thorough investigation and deliberation before a verdict is given, and a crime is recorded, so must we be meticulous with life, for death cannot be reversed. The same principle applies to medicine. When a physician visits a patient, if they possess the skill to diagnose correctly and understand the relationship between pulse and medicine, then their prescriptions can yield immediate results. However, if a lesser practitioner acts recklessly, applying remedies haphazardly, it can quickly lead to peril. How easy it is to cause harm! This arises from patients' inability to choose their doctors wisely, having never taken care to learn about medical practices beforehand. Is this not a matter for caution?[125p]

　　　　古今医统 【明　徐春甫】

　　　　　慎疾慎医

　　圣人治未病，不治已病，非谓已病而不治，亦非谓已病而不能治也。盖谓治未病，在谨厥始，防厥微，以治之，则成功多而受害少也。惟治于始微之际，则不至于已着而后治之，亦自无已病而后治也。今人治已病，不治未病，盖谓病形未着，不加慎防，直待病势已着，而后求医以治之，则其微之不谨，以至于着，斯可见矣。圣人起居动履，罔不摄养有方，间有几微隐晦之疾，必加意以防之，用药以治之，圣人之治未病，不治已病有如此。《论语》曰：子之所慎，齐战疾。程云：齐，所以交神明，诚至而神格；疾为身之生死所关；战为国家存亡所系。[126p]

The Medical Principles of Ancient and Modern Times
Ming Dynasty - Xu Chunfu

Caution in Illness and Medical Care

The sage treats diseases before they arise, not after they have manifested. This does not mean that one neglects to treat existing illnesses, nor that one is incapable of doing so. It implies that prevention is most effective at the very beginning; by carefully addressing the early signs and guarding against the petty manifestations, one achieves greater success with fewer harms. Only by addressing the subtle beginning does one prevent it from developing into a more serious condition, thereby avoiding any sickness that must be treated later.

People today tend to treat existing illnesses rather than prevent emerging ones, often disregarding early warning signs and waiting for the disease to fully develop before seeking medical help. This neglect of early symptoms leads to more severe conditions, which is clearly evident. The sage’s approach to daily life and actions is meticulous, ensuring proper care at all times. If even minor, obscure ailments arise, they are carefully attended to with preventive measures and treatments. Thus, the sage's practice of treating ailments before they manifest rather than after is exemplified.

The Analects say: “What the Master is cautious about, are the concerns of battle and illness.” Cheng Yi explains: “Battle concerns the oversight of the divine; sincerity leads to spiritual elevation; illness pertains to matters of life and death; and battle relates to the survival of the nation.”[126p]

然此三慎，诚为最大，而疾与乎其中，得非以身为至重耶？康子馈药，则曰未达不敢尝，可见圣人慎疾慎医之心，至且尽矣。世之人非惟不知治未病，及至已病，尚不知谨。始初微略，恣意无忌，酿成大患，方急而求医，曾不加择，惟以其风闻，或凭其吹荐，委之狂愚，卒以巨坏，皆其平日慢不究心于医，至于仓卒不暇择请，殊不知医药人人所必用，虽圣人有所不免，顾在平昔讲求，稔知某为明医，偶有微疾，则速求之以药，治如反掌。譬能曲突徙薪，岂有焦头烂额之诮？丹溪论之，固亦详矣。甫之肤见，尤有未悉之意焉。续貂之讶，诚所不免。有志养生者，扩而充之，亦未必无小补云。[127p]

The three cautions are indeed the most significant; however, is it not to be taken with utmost seriousness? When Master Kang was offered medicine, he said he would not dare to taste it until he was fully aware of its effects. This illustrates how the sages carefully consider illness and medicine with great seriousness.

People in the world not only lack knowledge of preventing illness before it arises, but also, when they do fall ill, they are unaware of the need for caution. At the onset of slight ailments, they indulge without restraint, leading to severe problems. Only in desperation do they seek medical help, often recklessly choosing based on rumors or recommendations, entrusting their care to the foolish. As a result, they suffer significant damage due to their previous negligence and lack of reflection on medical treatment.

In urgent situations, one does not have the luxury to carefully select a physician. Little do they realize that medicine is essential for everyone; even the sages are not exempt from illness. However, they have always sought knowledge and have acquainted themselves with well-regarded physicians. When a minor ailment arises, they seek treatment readily, finding cures with ease.

It is akin to remedying a fire hazard before it ignites; how could one suffer from burning if one acts prudently? Danxi has undoubtedly elaborated on this matter. The superficial understanding often leaves much yet to be comprehended. Indeed, the astonishment of the scholar is something that cannot be avoided.

For those keen on cultivating a healthy life, expanding and deepening their understanding may not be without minor benefits.[127p]

　　　　　古医十四科

　　古医十四科中有脾胃科，而今亡之矣。《道藏经》中颇有是说。自宋元以来，止用十三科考医政。其一为风科，次伤寒科，次大方脉科，次小方脉科，次妇人胎产科，次针灸科，次眼科，次咽喉口齿科，次疮疡科，次正骨科，次金镞科，次养生科，次祝由科。国朝亦惟取十三科而已，其脾胃一科，终莫之续。元李杲着《脾胃论》，极其精详。但不言十四科之阙，此不知其得旧本而加以己意欤？抑尽为创着而得上古之同然欤？是诚医道之大幸也。甫观今世医者，多不工于脾胃，只用反治之法，攻击疾病以治其标，惟知以寒治热，以热治寒，以通治塞，以塞治通而已。用寒因寒用，热因热用，通因通用，塞因塞用，必伏其所主，而先其所因，所谓从治之法，则漠然无所知也。及致脾胃损伤，犹不加察，元气一坏，变证多端。[128p]

The Ancient Medicine's Fourteen Categories

Among the Fourteen Categories of Ancient Medicine, there was originally a category for the spleen and stomach, which has now been lost. The "Daozang Jing" contains much discussion on this matter. Since the Song and Yuan dynasties, only thirteen categories have been used for evaluating medical practices. These are: the category for wind, followed by the category for external illnesses, then the major pulse category, the minor pulse category, the category for women's obstetrics, the acupuncture category, the ophthalmology category, the category for throat, mouth, and teeth, the category for sores and ulcers, the orthopedics category, the category for gold and arrows, the category for health preservation, and the category for incantations. In our current dynasty, only thirteen categories have been retained, and the category for the spleen and stomach has never been revived. Li Gao from the Yuan dynasty wrote "On the Spleen and Stomach," which is notably detailed. However, it does not mention the absence of the fourteen categories—was this due to his own interpretation of ancient texts, or was it purely a new invention that aligned with ancient knowledge? This indeed is a great fortune for the medical path. Upon observing contemporary physicians, many do not specialize in the spleen and stomach; they merely employ methods of opposite treatment, attacking diseases to address superficial symptoms, knowing only to treat heat with cold, cold with heat, obstruction with passage, and passage with obstruction. Using cold because of cold, or heat because of heat, or passage because of passage, or obstruction because of obstruction, must invariably lead to exacerbating the original condition, neglecting its underlying causes, which is the so-called method of following treatment; they seem utterly unaware of this. Moreover, when it leads to damage of the spleen and stomach, they still do not pay attention, and when the vital energy is compromised, many symptoms can arise.[128p]

如脾虚而气短不能以续，变而似喘促，医尚用降气定喘之药；如脾虚卫气不行，变而为浮肿，医尚用耗气利水之药；如脾虚郁滞，变而作寒热，医尚谓外感，用发散之药。大段类此，虚而益虚，直以气尽身亡，始用人参汤、附子汤灌之于殒绝之后，岂有能生之理乎？自今观之，不足者十常八九，况其时势竞驰驱于名利之涂，劳思伤脾而致病者，居其大半。若体实而偶为风寒暑湿之邪袭，则惟攻之而即愈者，亦不多见矣。此则中医治之易成功也。及遇脾胃虚而致风寒暑湿之邪袭，一同体实者而施治之，则大有间然者矣。攻之不已，则曰药不瞑眩，厥疾弗瘳。必大攻之，脾胃益伤而疾益笃，技穷无措则曰难医。时弊如斯，曷可胜纪？要皆不知本之故也。经曰：得谷者昌，失谷者亡。又曰：有胃气者生，无胃气者死。然则胃气谷气，得非人身之本欤？[129p]

If the spleen is deficient and the qi is short, leading to an inability to sustain oneself, resulting in what resembles wheezing and panting, physicians still prescribe medicines to descend qi and alleviate wheezing. If the spleen is weak and the defensive qi does not circulate, leading to swelling, physicians still prescribe medicines to deplete qi and promote water metabolism. If the spleen is weak and there is stagnation, resulting in alternating fever and chills, physicians attribute it to external pathogens and use dispersing medicines. In large segments like these, the deficiency merely aggravates the deficiency, leading to the depletion of qi and death; subsequently administering ginseng decoction or aconite decoction after collapse—how can it possibly nurture life?

From what is observed now, those who are insufficient are often the majority, especially since the forces of competition in the pursuit of fame and profit lead to excessive worry, damaging the spleen and causing illness for many. If the body is robust yet is occasionally invaded by wind, cold, summer heat, or dampness, such cases are rarely seen to recover simply through attack. This explains why traditional Chinese medicine can easily lead to success in treatment. However, when encountering the spleen and stomach deficiency causing attacks from wind, cold, summer heat, or dampness, and treating those who have strong bodies, significant variances can arise. If one continues to aggressively treat without cease, they may say that the medicine does not diminish the dizziness, and the disease does not heal. They must take vigorous measures, yet this further injures the spleen and stomach, making the condition worse. When their techniques are exhausted and they feel there is no solution, they say it is difficult to treat. What a great flaw this is; how could one chronicle it all? Ultimately, it is due to a lack of understanding of the root cause.

The classics say: "Those who have grain will thrive; those who lose grain will perish." It is also said: "Those who have stomach qi live; those who lack stomach qi die." Therefore, the stomach qi and grain qi, is it not the foundation of the human body?[129p]

　　　　　时医

　　俗云：明医不如时医。盖谓时医虽不读书明理，以其有时运造化，亦能侥效。常自云：趁我十年时，有病早来医。又云：饶你熟读王叔和，不如我见病证多。里谚有云：左心小肠肝胆贤，时来每日有千钱。所谓明医不如时医，良以此也。《卫生宝鉴》所谓福医，深足为戒。今之患者，不达此理，委命于时医，亦犹自暴自弃，甘于沟壑者，何异哉？[130p]

Time Doctors

It is often said: a skilled doctor is not as good as a timely doctor. This means that though a timely doctor may not be well-educated, their ability to seize the moment and adapt can still yield results. They often say: "If you have an illness, come to me while I am in my prime." They also claim: "Even if you are well-read in the works of Wang Shuhe, you cannot compare to my experience with many cases." There is a local saying: "The left heart, small intestine, liver, and gallbladder are wise; when the time is right, you can earn a thousand coins a day." That is why it is said that a skilled doctor is not as good as a timely doctor. The "Treasured Mirror of Health" refers to a fortune doctor, which serves as a serious warning. Nowadays, patients do not understand this principle, entrusting themselves to timely doctors, which is akin to self-destructive behavior, willingly surrendering to decline. What difference is there?[130p]

　　　　　巫医

　　以巫而替医，故曰巫医也。
　　《论语》曰：人而无恒，不可以作巫医。孔子叹人不可以无恒，而善其言之有理。朱子注云：巫所以交鬼神，医所以寄死生。岐而二之，似未当也。夫医之为道，始于神农，阐于黄帝，按某病用某药，着有《内经》、《素问》，所谓圣人坟典之书，以援民命，安可与巫觋之流同日而语耶？但学医者，有精粗不同，故名因之有异。精于医者曰明医，善于医者曰良医，寿君保相曰国医，粗工昧理曰庸医，击鼓舞趋祈禳疾病曰巫医。是则巫觋之徒，不知医药之理者也。故南人谓之巫医者，此也。今世谓之端公太保，又称为夜行卜士，北方名之师婆，虽是一切虚诞之辈，则亦不可以无恒也，矧他乎？[131p]

Witch Doctors

Witch doctors replace physicians, hence the term "witch doctor."
According to the "Analects," a person without constancy cannot be a witch doctor. Confucius laments that one cannot be without constancy, and his reasoning is sound. Zhuzi comments that a witch interacts with spirits, while medicine pertains to matters of life and death. The two are distinct and shouldn't be equated. The way of medicine originated with Shennong and was elaborated by the Yellow Emperor, who specified certain medicines for certain ailments, reflected in texts like the "Neijing" and "Suwen," considered the sacred canons for aiding people’s lives; how can this be compared to the practices of witch doctors? However, practitioners of medicine vary in skill; thus, their names differ. Those who are proficient in medicine are called wise doctors, those skilled are good doctors, those preserving the health of the country are national doctors, while those who lack understanding are termed incompetent doctors. Those who beat drums and perform dances to ward off illness are called witch doctors. Therefore, witch doctors, who do not understand the principles of medicine, are referred to as such by those in the South.
