In modern times, they may be called "Duan Gong Tai Bao," or "night-walking diviners," and in the North referred to as "Shi Po." Though they are all considered to be of dubious character, one still cannot lack constancy, how much less so in other matters?[131p]

　　　　　名医

　　医为司命之寄，不可权饰妄造。所以医不三世，不服其药；九折臂者，乃成良医。盖谓学功精深故也。今之承借者，多恃衒名腾价，不能精心研习，京邑诸人皆尚虚誉，不取实学，闻风竞奖，其新学该博而名称未振，以为始习，多不信用，委命虚名，良可惜也！　　支本中曰：昔越人因鲁公扈，志强气弱，足于谋而寡于断；赵齐婴志弱气强，少于虑而伤于专。乃饮以药酒，易置二人之心，使俱为名士。予观今之求医者，率以有时名者为重，初不计其书之读不读，脉之明不明，谓之时医、福医、名医，一承权贵所举，辄凭治疗，虽杀其身，委命无怨。故为医者，往往奔走权门，謟容卑态以求荐，网利沽名，知者笑议，仁心仁闻，毫蔑有也。安得饮以药酒而俱易其心乎？[132p]

Famous Physicians

Medicine is entrusted with the mandate of life; it should not be approached with superficial embellishments or fabrications. Thus, a physician should not practice medicine for three generations nor take their own medicine; only by experiencing hardships can one become a good physician. This implies that mastery comes from deep learning. The contemporary practitioners often rely on their renown for fame and profit, failing to dedicate themselves to diligent study. The people in the capital are particularly enamored with empty reputation, disregarding practical knowledge. They rush to offer praise for new theories without ensuring their validity and often do not trust what is newly learned, handing over their lives to hollow names, which is quite regrettable!

Zhi Benzhong said: In the past, the Yue people strategized in support of Duke Lu; strong in resolve but weak in action, they were good at planning but poor at execution. The Zhao and Qi had aspirations that were weak but would assert themselves strongly, lacking in contemplation and suffering from their own stubbornness. They drank medicinal wine to alter the hearts of these two men, transforming them both into celebrated scholars. Observing today’s patients seeking medical help, most prioritize those with timely renown, seldom considering whether the physician has truly studied or understands the pulse. They label them as contemporary, fortunate, or famous physicians, easily elected by the powerful, yielding to treatment even at the cost of their lives, doing so without complaint. Consequently, many physicians hustle for favor with the influential, adopting servile attitudes to seek recommendations, scheming for profit and fame, while knowledgeable individuals mock them. Compassion and moral integrity have been cast aside. How can one expect to drink medicinal wine and change their hearts in such conditions?[132p]

　　　　　儒医

　　赵从古曰：庆历中有进士沈常，为人廉洁方宜，性寡合，后进多有推服，未尝省荐，每自叹曰：吾潦倒场屋，尚未免穷困，岂非天命也耶？乃入京师，别谋生计。因游至东华门，偶见数朝士，跃马挥鞭，从者雄盛，询之市人，何官位也？人曰：翰林医官也。常又叹曰：吾穷孔圣之道，焉得不及知甘草大黄辈也？始有意学医。次见市廛货药者，巧言艰苦，复又耻为，疑贰不决，与同人共议曰：吾辈学则穷达方书，师必趋事名公，自非常流比也。是时余为太医医师，常辄以长书请见，急迎候之，无敢轻怠。[133p]

The Confucian Healer

Zhao Conggu said: During the Qingli era, there was a successful candidate named Shen Chang, who was known for his integrity and uprightness. He was usually solitary and often attracted the admiration of those who came after him. Yet he never sought recommendations for advancement, and would often sigh, saying: "I am in dire straits, still unable to escape poverty; is this not my fate?" Thus, he entered the capital, seeking a livelihood.

While wandering to the East Huamen, he happened to see several officials mounted on horses, brandishing whips, with their followers being numerous and impressive. He asked a merchant in the market, inquiring about their status. The merchant replied: “They are officials in the Hanlin Academy and medical officers.” Chang sighed again, saying: “I am poor in the teachings of Confucius; how could I not be aware of the likes of licorice and rhubarb?” This marked the beginning of his intention to study medicine.

Next, he saw merchants selling medicinal goods, speaking skillfully yet with difficulty, and he felt ashamed to pursue that path. Unsure and indecisive, he discussed with his peers, saying: “We, who study, may rise or fall based on the written texts; our teachers must seek out distinguished positions, and surely it ought not to be in common paths.” At that time, I was a physician in the imperial medical office; Shen would often send long letters requesting to see me, and I would eagerly receive him, with no one daring to be negligent.[133p]

常曰：此来穷蹇之人，因同人相勉，令某学医，闻君名公也，故来师问。余曰：医术比之儒术，固其次也。然动关性命，非谓等闲，学者若非性好专志，难臻其妙。足下既言穷蹇，是志未得遂，复却学医，深恐郁滞之性，未能精研。常愠色曰：君虽穷蹇，乃自服儒，读孔孟之书，粗识历代君臣治国之道，今徒志学技术，岂为高艺？余曰：恐非浅尝能也。君未谕上古三皇医教，姑且勿论，即如汉之张仲景、晋之葛洪、齐之褚澄、梁之陶隐君，非不服儒有才有行。吾闻儒识礼义，医知损益。礼义之不修，昧孔孟之教，损益之不分，害生民之命，儒与医岂可轻哉？儒与医岂可分哉？[134p]

It is often said: "Those who come from difficult backgrounds, relying on mutual encouragement, tend to learn medicine. Hearing of your honorable name, I have come to seek guidance." I replied: "The art of medicine is certainly secondary to the Confucian teachings. However, since it concerns life and death, it is not to be taken lightly. If a learner does not possess a genuine passion and dedication, it is difficult to grasp its subtleties. You have mentioned your difficulties, indicating that your ambitions have not yet been realized; returning to study medicine raises concerns that your stagnation might hinder your ability to delve deeply into it." He, often with an annoyed expression, said: "Although you are facing hardships, you still follow Confucianism and read the works of Confucius and Mencius, having a basic understanding of the principles of governance throughout history. Now, simply aspiring to learn a technical skill—how can that be a lofty pursuit?" I responded: "I fear that mere superficial engagement will not suffice. You have not yet comprehended the teachings of the ancient Three Sovereigns on medicine, so let us set that aside. Even so, figures such as Zhang Zhongjing of the Han, Ge Hong of the Jin, Chu Cheng of the Qi, and Tao Yinjun of the Liang all practiced Confucianism while possessing talent and virtue. I have heard that the scholar knows rites and righteousness, while the physician understands gain and loss. Without proper cultivation of rituals and righteousness, one becomes ignorant of the teachings of Confucius and Mencius. Failing to distinguish between gain and loss endangers the lives of the people. Can the realms of Confucianism and medicine be treated lightly? Can they be separated?"[134p]

　　　　　五难

　　宋濂曰：呜呼！医其难言矣乎！人之生也，与天地之气相为流通，养之得其道，则百顺集，百邪去，苟失其养，内伤于七情，外感于六气而疾生焉。医者从而治之，必察其根本枝末，其实也从而损之，其虚也从而益之，阴平阳秘，自适厥中。粗工或昧乎此，实实虚虚，损不足而益有余，病之能起者鲜矣，此其难一也。气血之运，必有以疏载之，左右手足，各备阴阳者，三阳既有太少矣，而又有阳明者何？取两阳合明之义也。阴既有太少矣，而又有厥阴者何？取两阴交尽之义也。何经受病，宜用何剂治之，治之固不难，又当知有引经之药，能循此法，则无疾弗瘳矣。[135p]

Five Difficulties

Song Lian said: Alas! How difficult it is to speak of medicine! The life of a person flows in circulation with the energies of heaven and earth. If one manages to nourish oneself correctly, then harmony prevails and a hundred ailments disappear. If one loses this nourishment, internal injuries arise from the seven emotions, and external afflictions from the six energies, leading to illness. The physician must investigate the root and branches; in essence, he must diminish what is substantive and augment what is deficient. The balance of yin and yang must be preserved, allowing one to adapt to the center. If a crude practitioner is ignorant of this, mixing the substantiated and the vacuous, he will exhaust what is lacking while enhancing what is excessive. Consequently, the ability of illness to arise becomes rare; this constitutes one difficulty. The movement of qi and blood requires specific conduits. The arms and legs, each embodying yin and yang, must balance; if the three yang are insufficient, what of the yangming? It pertains to the principle of two yangs converging in brightness. If yin is excessively diminished, what of the jueyin? It refers to the union of two yins to their fullest extent. When facing illness, it is imperative to know which remedies to apply. While treating may not be difficult, one must also understand the medicinal properties that guide the principles; by following this method, no sickness will be without remedy.[135p]

粗工不辨十二经，而一概施之，譬犹罗雀于江，罾鱼于林，万一或幸而得之，岂理也哉！此其难二也。岁气各有不同，攻治亦异其宜。曰升降，曰浮沉，吾则顺而承之；曰寒热，曰温凉，吾则逆而反之。庶几能全其天和，不致颠倒错谬。粗工则伥伥然，当顺则反逆，当逆则反顺，如盲人适野，不辨乎东西。此其难三也。病有寒热，热者当投凉，寒者宜剂之以温，此恒理也。然寒热之势方剧，而遽欲反之，必扞格而难入。热因热用，寒因寒用，其始则同，其终则异，庶几能成其功。粗工则不察而混治之。此其难四也。药性有阴阳而不专于阴阳，有所谓阳中之阴，阴中之阳，差之毫厘，谬以千里。粗工则不核重轻而妄投之，此其难五也。[136p]

Rough practitioners do not distinguish the Twelve Meridians and apply treatments indiscriminately, much like a sparrow caught in the river or fishing in the woods; even if they occasionally succeed, it is sheer luck! This is the second difficulty. The seasonal energies are different each year, and treatments should be accordingly adjusted. When it is time for rising and falling, we should follow and accept their flow; when faced with cold and heat, we should resist and counter them. In doing so, we can preserve the natural harmony and avoid disorder and error. Rough practitioners, however, blindly go against the grain, reversing what should be followed and following what should be resisted, like a blind person lost in the wilderness, unable to discern east from west. This constitutes the third difficulty. Diseases can manifest as hot or cold; heat requires cooling treatments, while cold should be treated with warmth—this is a fundamental principle. Yet, when the forces of cold and heat are intense, attempting to reverse them suddenly will surely clash and create obstacles. Heat must be treated with heat, and cold with cold; their beginnings may be the same, but their ends are different, which may lead to successful outcomes. Rough practitioners, however, fail to observe this and mix treatments indiscriminately. This is the fourth difficulty. Medicinal properties have yin and yang, yet they are not solely categorized as such; there exists yin within yang and yang within yin. A slight deviation can result in a significant error. Rough practitioners neglect to weigh the relative strengths and recklessly apply treatments. This is the fifth difficulty.[136p]

　　　　　医喻政

　　宋吕诲上神宗疏云：臣本无宿疾，偶值医者用术乖方，殊不知脉候有虚实，阴阳有逆顺，治疗有标本，用药有先后，妄投汤剂，率意任情，差之指下，祸延四肢，寝危心腹，虽以身疾喻朝政，深切医之弊也，业医者可不为之戒哉！[137p]

In a memorial to Emperor Shen-zong of the Song dynasty, Lu Hui states: I originally had no chronic illness, but by chance I encountered a doctor whose techniques were errant. He failed to understand the pulse conditions, the balance of yin and yang, the distinction between root and branch in treatment, and the appropriate order for administering medicine. Recklessly prescribing decoctions and following personal whims, he misdiagnosed and caused calamity to the limbs, endangering the heart and abdomen. While using my personal ailment as an analogy for governance, it underscores the grave errors in medical practice. Should not those who practice medicine take this as a warning?[137p]

　　　　　论医

　　郁离子曰：治天下其犹医乎？医切脉以知证，审证以为方。证有阴阳虚实，脉有浮沉细大，而方有补泻针灼汤剂之宜。参、苓、姜、桂、硝、黄之药，随其人之病而施焉，当则生，不当则死矣。是故知证知脉而不善为方，非医也。虽有扁鹊之识，徒哓哓而无用，不知证，不知脉，道听途说以为方，语人曰：我能医，是贼天下者也。故治乱，证也；纪纲，脉也；道德刑政，方与法也；人才，药也。夏之政尚忠，殷乘其弊而救之以质；殷之政尚质，周乘其弊而救之以文。秦用酷刑苛法，以箝天下，天下苦之。而汉乘之以宽大，守之以宁壹，其方与证对，其用药也无舛，天下之病，有不瘳者鲜矣。又曰：一指之寒弗燠则及于手足，一手足之寒弗燠则困于四体，气脉之相贯也。忽于微而至大，故疾病之中人也，始于一腠。理之不知，或知而惑之也，遂至于大而不可救以死，不亦悲夫！噫！若郁离子者，可谓深得医之情矣。[138p]

On Medicine

Yuli Zi said: Governing the world is akin to practicing medicine, is it not? A physician diagnoses by feeling the pulse to understand the symptoms and examines the symptoms to formulate a treatment plan. Symptoms possess yin and yang, vacuity and solidity, while the pulse can be superficial or deep, fine or strong, and the treatment will involve the right balance of tonification and purgation, acupuncture, cautery, and decoctions. The herbs such as ginseng, poria, ginger, cinnamon, salt, and huang, are applied according to the patient's illness; if appropriately administered, they lead to life, but if not, they result in death. Therefore, to know the symptoms and pulse yet not be adept at formulating treatments, one is not a physician. Even possessing knowledge like Bian Que's, if one merely boasts without usefulness, without understanding symptoms and pulse, relying on hearsay for treatments, declaring, "I can heal," is to be a thief of the world. Thus, disorder is equivalent to symptoms; order and structure are like the pulse; morality and laws correspond to treatments and regulations; talent is like medicine. In the governance of the Xia, loyalty was emphasized, while the Yin took advantage of its deficiencies to restore it with substance; the governance of the Yin valued substance, while the Zhou exploited its flaws to restore it with culture. The Qin applied harsh punishments and severe laws to suppress the world, resulting in widespread suffering. Yet, the Han embraced leniency and maintained tranquility, perfectly matching their treatments with symptoms; their medicinal applications seldom failed, ensuring that few ailments remained untreated. Furthermore, one must understand that if the cold at a single finger is not warmed, it will affect the hands and feet, and if the cold at the hands and feet is not warmed, the entire body will suffer, as the energies and pulses are interconnected. A small issue can lead to a significant one; thus, illnesses in people begin at a minor level. Failure to comprehend the fundamental causes, or even understanding yet being confused, ultimately leads to a major issue that cannot be saved, resulting in death. Is this not tragic? Oh! Someone like Yuli Zi can be said to have a profound understanding of the essence of medicine![138p]

　　　　　裒方书

　　陆宣公在忠州裒方书以度月，非特假此以避祸，盖君子之存心，无所不用其至也。前辈名士，往往能医，非止卫生，亦可及物，而今人反耻言之。近时土大夫家藏方，或集验方，流布甚广，皆仁人之用心。《本事单方》，近已刻于四明本朝诸公文集杂说中。名方尚多，未有见类而传之者，予屡为之，恨藏书不广。倘有能因予言集以传于人，亦济物之一端也。[139p]

Pao Fang Shu

Duke Xuan of Lu wrote the Pao Fang Shu in Zhongzhou to measure the months; it was not merely to avoid disaster but demonstrated that the mind of a noble person applies itself fully in all matters. The esteemed figures of the past were often able to heal, not only in terms of hygiene but also regarding physical ailments, yet people today are ashamed to speak of this. Recently, wealthy families have been collecting and publishing prescriptions, which are widely disseminated, all reflecting the intentions of benevolent individuals. The "Ben Shi Dan Fang" has recently been published in the collections and miscellaneous writings of various gentlemen from the Four Ming dynasty. There are many famous prescriptions that remain unrecorded, and I have often attempted to compile them, lamenting that my collection of books is not extensive. If there are those who can take my words and share them with others, that would also serve as a contribution to helping others.[139p]

　　　　　一理贯通

　　伤寒、内伤、妇女、小儿，皆医者通习也，不知何代而各科之。今世指某曰专某科，复指某曰兼某科，又指某曰非某科，殊不知古有扁鹊者，过邯郸，贵妇女，即为女医；过睢阳，闻周人爱老人，即为耳目痹医；入咸阳，闻秦人爱小儿，即为小儿医。随俗为变，曾不分异而为治也。既曰医药，则皆一理贯通。又云此长彼短，亦不善于穷理者也。
　　　　　针灸药三者备为医之良

　　扁鹊有言，疾在腠理熨焫之所及，疾在血脉针石之所及，其在肠胃酒醪之所及，是针灸药三者得兼，而后可与言医。可与言医者，斯周官之十全者也。曩甫谬以活人之术止于药，故弃针与灸而莫之讲，每遇伤寒热入血室，闪挫诸疾，非药饵所能愈，而必俟夫刺者，则束手无策，自愧技穷。因悟治病犹对垒，攻守奇正，量敌而应者将之良，针灸药因病而施治者医之良也。[140p]

The Unity of Principles

Diseases such as cold damage, internal injury, women’s issues, and children’s ailments are all within the practice of medicine, but they have each become categorized into different schools over the generations. In today’s world, some are labeled as specialists in certain fields, others as having multiple specialties, while some are deemed not to belong to certain fields. They are unaware that in ancient times, there was a physician named Bian Que who traveled past Handan and became known as a female physician when he encountered noble women; he passed by Suiyang and became known as a doctor for the elderly when he heard of the Zhou people's affection for older adults; he entered Xiangyang and became a pediatrician upon learning of the Qin people’s love for children. He adapted to societal norms without distinguishing differences in the treatment of illnesses. When it comes to medicine, all is interconnected by the same principles. To say that one is strong in one area and weak in another also indicates a lack of thorough understanding of these principles.

The Goodness of Acupuncture, Moxibustion, and Medicine

Bian Que remarked that diseases are limited to the area affected by the skin and muscle, or those that penetrate the blood vessels and tissues, or involve the stomach and intestines. Therefore, one must integrate acupuncture, moxibustion, and medicine in order to genuinely engage in the healing arts. Engaging in true medical practice requires a comprehensive understanding akin to that of a well-rounded official in the Zhou dynasty. In the past, the fallacy was to restrict the art of saving lives solely to medication, thus neglecting acupuncture and moxibustion. Whenever faced with diseases like cold damage that penetrate into the blood chamber, or injuries from impact, which cannot be cured by medicinal pills, one might find themselves at a loss without the intervention of acupuncture, feeling inadequate in skill. It was realized that treating illness is much like a strategic confrontation, where offense and defense must align with the characteristics of the enemy, revealing the true benefits of acupuncture, moxibustion, and medicine in relation to the illness at hand.[140p]

　　　　　庸医速报

　　医学贵精，不精则害人匪细。间有无知辈，窃世医之名，抄检成方，略记《难经》、《脉诀》不过三者尽之，自信医学无难矣。此外惟修边幅，饰以衣骑，习以口给，谄媚豪门，乃彰虚誉，摇摇自满，适以骇俗。一遇识者洞见肺肝，掣肘莫能施其巧，犹面谀而背诽之。又讥同列看书访学，徒自劳苦。凡有治疗，率尔狂诞，妄投药剂，偶尔侥效，需索百端。凡有误伤，则曰尽命。俗多习此为套，而曰医学无难，岂其然乎？于戏！医而日相流于弊矣，无怪乎缙绅先生之鄙浅也。[141p]

         Report on the Incompetent Doctor

The essence of medicine lies in specialization; without it, the harm done is not trivial. Occasionally, there are ignorant individuals who misappropriate the name of medicine, copying established prescriptions and barely noting the "Nanjing" and "Pulse Scripture," believing that medicine holds no difficulties. Apart from this, they merely groom their appearance, adorn themselves in fine clothes, practice empty rhetoric, flatter the wealthy, and thus gain hollow praise, becoming self-satisfied and ostentatious. When faced with those who truly understand, they become restless and unable to apply their tricks, yet they continue to flatter while slandering others behind their backs. They ridicule their peers who study and seek knowledge, tiring themselves in vain. Whenever they attempt treatments, they act irrationally, haphazardly dispensing medicines, occasionally finding fleeting success, but invariably demanding excessive compensation. If any harm results, they claim to have done their best. It is common for many to adopt this approach and claim that medicine is simple; is that really the case? Alas! Daily, the practice of medicine is descending into error, and it is no wonder that the learned gentlemen are so shallow.[141p]

欲其有得真医亦寡矣。幸天道好生而恶杀，速昭其报施，庸医横亡，人皆目击。迩有士人被误药而立毙，家人讼之，法司拘审，律不过笞罪，随释而驰归，未逾年，被贼肢解而死，岂非天道之报耶？小说嘲庸医早亡诗云：不肖谁知假，贤良莫识真。庸医不早死，误尽世间人。岂非天道恶之耶？故甫尝戒诸子弟，医惟大道之奥，性命存焉。凡业者必要精心研究，以抵于极。毋谓易以欺人，惟图侥幸。道艺自精，必有知者，总不谋利于人，自有正谊在己。《易》曰：积善积恶，殃庆各以其类至。安得谓不利乎？[142p]

It is rare to find a true doctor who is genuinely effective. Fortunately, the way of heaven promotes life and detests killing, promptly revealing the consequences of one's actions, while incompetent doctors unjustly perish, witnessed by all. Recently, there was a scholar who was fatally harmed by a mistake in medication; his family sued, and the legal authorities apprehended and interrogated, but the punishment was merely a beating, and he was released to return home. Within a year, he was brutally murdered by robbers. Is this not a manifestation of heaven’s retribution? A poem mocking incompetent doctors suggests: "Who knows the impostors, while the virtuous remain blind to the truth? If incompetent doctors don't perish early, they could ruin countless lives." Is this not a sign of heavenly disdain for them? Therefore, I urge my students to understand that medicine is a profound way of nature, linked to life and death. Professionals must diligently study to reach the highest standards. One should not think it easy to deceive others or rely solely on luck. If one masters their craft, there will undoubtedly be those who recognize the effort. Those who do not seek to profit at the expense of others will find their integrity within themselves. As the "I Ching" says: "Good and evil accumulate, with misfortune and fortune coming in kind." How can one say there is no consequence?[142p]

　　　　　医业不精反为夭折

　　相彼天下之人所重者生也，生之所击者医也，医之所原者理也。上古有黄帝、岐伯、扁鹊、华佗苏死更生醒魂夺命之术，以至三代而降，学是者疏莽聊略，不致精元，时时有贼夫人者，何也？盖于阴也而体之以阳，阳也而拟之以阴，虚也而推之以实，实也而度之以虚，外也而揣之以内，内也而像之以外，急也而料之以缓，缓也而亿之以急，进也而窥之以退，退也而探之以进，孟浪以诊其脉，浮浅以察其证，苍黄以稽其声，恍惚以征其色，所以颠倒用蒙聩之工，舛差施聋盲之药，斩绵绵未艾之年，绝婉婉方增之齿，俾含枉而下世，抱屈而归泉，天下之夭折者，诚为庶哉。嗟嗟！医本活人，学之不精，反为夭折。[143p]

The practice of medicine, when not profound, leads to premature death.

What people in the world value most is life; the means to sustain life is medicine, and the foundation of medicine lies in its principles. In ancient times, figures like the Yellow Emperor, Qi Bo, Bian Que, and Hua Tuo mastered the art of reviving the dead and restoring consciousness, carrying these skills down through the three dynasties. However, those who study these arts today are often superficial and lack thorough understanding. Consequently, there are those who mislead others. Why is this so? It is because they interpret the yin with yang, and the yang with yin; they push the void with the solid, and measure the solid with the void; they consider the external in relation to the internal, and the internal in relation to the external; they assess urgency with slowness, and slowness with urgency; they advance with a retreating eye, and probe forward with a retreating stance.

They are reckless in diagnosing pulses, shallow in observing symptoms, confused in judging sounds, and hazy in assessing colors. This leads to a reversal of techniques that blind and confuse, misapplying remedies to the deaf and blind. They cut short lives yet to blossom, and extinguish souls that should thrive. Such practitioners send lives into this world burdened and return them to the grave in torment. Indeed, premature death in the world is common. Alas! The purpose of medicine is to save lives; a lack of mastery leads instead to their demise.[143p]

　　　　　用药活法

　　用药之法，不可辟执古方。当因证轻重，加减药味，冷热元微，务合其理。切勿妄施，误投丸散，顷刻伤残性命，天理不容，为医者可不自慎？如雄黄、牙硝、石英、硼砂、丹砂之类不可火煎，川岛、附子、郁金、南星、半夏不可生用，务须精细依法修制，斯药无弗效矣。
　　　　证治准绳 【明　王肯堂】

　　　　　通论

　　罗谦甫曰：一小儿，五月间因食伤冷粉，腹中作痛，遂于市药铺中，购得神芎丸服之，脐腹渐加冷痛，时发时止，逾七八年不已。因思古人云，寒者热之，治寒以热，良医不能废其绳墨而更其道也。据所伤之物寒也，所攻之药亦寒也，重寒伤胃，其为冷痛可知矣。凡人之脾胃，喜温而恶冷，况小儿气血尚弱，不能任其寒，故阳气潜伏，寒毒留连，久而不除也。治病必先其本，当用和中养气之药，以救前失，服之月余方愈。[144p]

Method of Using Medicines

When it comes to the method of using medicines, one must not rigidly adhere to ancient formulas. Instead, adjustments should be made based on the severity of symptoms, modifying the ingredients according to the varying properties of heat and cold to ensure they align with the principles of treatment. One must not apply recklessly; misguidedly administering pills or powders can, in an instant, harm or endanger life. Is there not a natural order that physicians must heed carefully? Substances like realgar, chaulmoogra, quartz, borax, and cinnabar must not be fire-cooked. Herbs such as Sichuan pepper, aconite, turmeric, southern star, and pinellia should not be used raw; they must be processed diligently according to proper methods for medicines to be effective.

General Discussion

Luo Qianfu stated: A child, during the fifth month, suffered abdominal pain after consuming cold powder. He subsequently purchased Shenqi Pills from a pharmacy, which led to an increase in cold pain around the navel and abdomen, intermittently for seven to eight years without relief. Reflecting on the ancients' saying, "Cold should be treated with heat," one must adhere to established principles; a competent physician cannot abandon their essential guidelines to change methods. The harmful substance was cold, and the medicine used was also cold; heavy cold injures the stomach, which explains the cold pain. The human spleen and stomach prefer warmth and reject cold, particularly for children whose vital energy and blood are still weak and cannot tolerate cold. Thus, the yang energy becomes dormant, and cold toxins linger, remaining unresolved over time. To treat illness, one must first address the root cause, using medicines that harmonize and nourish the vital energy to rectify earlier mistakes; only after one month of such treatment can recovery be expected.[144p]

呜呼！康子馈药，孔子拜而受之，以未达不敢尝，此保生之重者也。奈何常人拱默而令切脉，以谓能知病否。且脉者人之血气附行经络之间，热胜则脉疾，寒胜则脉迟，实则有力，虚则无力。至于所伤何物，岂能别其形象乎？医者不可不审其病源，而主家亦不可不说其病源。且此子之父，不以病源告医，而求药于市铺中，发药者亦不审其病，而以药付之，以致七八年之病，皆昧此理也。孙真人云：未诊先问，最为有准。东坡云：只图愈疾，不欲困医。二公之语，其有功于世也大矣。
　　　　医学入门 【明　李梴】

　　　　　习医规格

　　医司人命，非质实而无伪，性静而有恒，真知阴功之趣者。未可轻易以习医。志既立矣，却可商量用工。每早对先天图静坐，玩读《孝经》、《论语》、《小学》；大有资力者，次及全部四书古易白文，及《书经》、《洪范》、《无逸》、《尧典》。　　理会大意，不必强记。[145p]

Alas! When Kang Zi offered medicine, Confucius bowed and accepted it, and due to his lack of understanding, he did not dare to taste it; this is the essence of preserving life. How is it that ordinary people merely sit silently and pulse-check, claiming to know the illness? The pulse is the blood and energy of a person flowing between the meridians; if heat prevails, the pulse is rapid; if cold prevails, the pulse is slow; when it is robust, it indicates strength; when it is weak, it indicates deficiency. As for what is injured, how can one distinguish its form? Physicians must thoroughly examine the source of illness, and the patient must also disclose the source of their ailment. Yet this man's father did not inform the doctor of the illness' source and sought medicine from the marketplace, while the dispenser of medicine also failed to assess the condition, resulting in a sickness lasting seven or eight years, all missing this principle. The real master Sun said: “Inquiring before diagnosis is most accurate.” Dongpo said: “One should just seek to recover from illness and not burden the physician.” The words of these two gentlemen greatly benefit the world.

Entry to the Study of Medicine
【Ming Dynasty – Li Chen】

Standards for Practicing Medicine

The practice of medicine involves human life; it must be genuine and unpretentious, characterized by calmness and constancy, possessing true knowledge of the nuances of Yin and virtue. One cannot recklessly pursue the study of medicine. Once the resolve is established, then one may deliberate on the application of efforts. Every morning, sit quietly facing the diagram of the primordial, studying the "Classic of Filial Piety," "Analects," and "Elementary Learning"; those with substantial resources should also study all the Four Books, the ancient texts of the I Ching, the "Classic of Documents," "Great Plan," "Unfettered," and the "Classic of Yao." Grasp the general meaning without the need for forced memorization.[145p]

　　盖医出于儒，非读书明理，终是庸俗昏昧，不能疏通变化。每午将入门大字，从头至尾，逐段诵读，必一字不遗，若出诸口。　　如欲专小科，则亦不可不读大科；欲专外科，亦不可不读内科。盖因此识彼则有之，未有通于彼而塞于此者。惟经涉浅深生熟，故有分科不同。　　熟读后，潜思默想，究竟其间意义。稍有疑难，检阅古今名家方书，以广闻见；或就有德高明之士，委曲请问。陶节庵云：但不与俗人言耳。盖方药不外于《本草》、《素》、《难》，及张、刘、李、朱，纵有小方捷法，终不是大家数，慎不可为其诬惑。入门书既融会贯通，而后可成一小医。愈加静坐，玩读儒书，稍知阴阳消长，以己验人，由亲及疏，自料作车于室，天下合辙，然后可以应人之求。及其行持，尤不可无定规。每五鼓清心静坐，及早起仍玩儒书一二，以雪心源。　　时时不失平旦之气为妙。[146p]

Indeed, the practice of medicine originates from Confucianism. Without studying and understanding principles, one remains ignorant and unable to grasp the complexities of change. Every noon, upon entering, one should read aloud from the beginning to the end, methodically, ensuring that no word is missed, as if it flows from the mouth.

If one desires to specialize in minor subjects, it is also essential to study major subjects; if one wishes to focus on surgery, one must not neglect internal medicine. Understanding one requires knowledge of the other; there has never been a case of mastering one while ignoring the other. It is precisely due to the varied levels of depth and familiarity that there are divisions in specialties.

After thorough reading, one should contemplate quietly, investigating the meanings within. If there are doubts or difficulties, one should consult ancient and modern renowned texts to broaden one’s knowledge. Alternatively, seeking advice from virtuous and wise individuals is advisable. Tao Jie'an stated: "Just do not engage in conversation with the ignorant." This is because formulas and remedies do not stray from the “Ben Cao,” “Su,” and “Nan,” as well as works by Zhang, Liu, Li, and Zhu. Even if there are minor treatments and quick methods, they are ultimately not the work of the masters, and one must be cautious to avoid being misled by them.

Once the fundamental texts have been thoroughly comprehended, one can then qualify as a minor physician. With greater dedication to quiet meditation, one should revisit Confucian texts, gaining a basic understanding of the dynamics of yin and yang, learning from both close relations and distant ones, thus preparing to offer assistance effectively in various situations.

Particularly in one’s conduct, it is crucial to establish consistent guidelines. Each dawn, one should purify the heart through quiet meditation and rise early to engage with Confucian texts, in order to refresh the source of one’s heart.

At all times, one should not lose the vitality of early morning, which is wondrous.[146p]

　　及其为人诊视，先问证起何日。从头至足，照依伤寒初证杂证及内外伤辨法，逐一详问。证虽重而门类明白者，不须诊脉，亦可议方。证虽轻而题目未定音，必须仔细察脉。　　男必先左后右，女必先右后左，所以顺阴阳升降也。　　先单看，以知各经隐曲。次总看，以决虚实死生。既诊后，挂病家言必以实，或虚或实，可治易治难治，说出几分证候，以验自己精神。如有察未及者，值令说明，不可牵强文饰。务宜从容拟议，不可急迫激切，以至恐吓。如诊妇女，须托其至亲先问证色与舌，及所饮食；然后随其所便，或证重而就床隔帐诊之，或证轻而就门隔帷诊之，亦必以薄纱罩手。　　贫家不便，医者自袖薄纱。[147p]

When examining a patient, one must first inquire about the onset of symptoms and the date they began. From head to toe, one should reference the initial signs of typhoid fever, mixed symptoms, and methods of distinguishing internal and external injuries, questioning each detail thoroughly. If the symptoms are severe but clearly categorized, one need not check the pulse and can discuss treatment options. If the symptoms are mild yet not clearly defined, pulse examination is necessary for careful assessment.

For men, the left side is examined first, followed by the right; for women, the right side is examined first, followed by the left, in accordance with the principles of yin and yang. First, assess each channel individually to understand the hidden curves. Then, take a comprehensive look to determine the state of deficiency or excess, life or death. After the diagnosis, any remarks about the patient's condition must be truthful, whether they indicate excess or deficiency, and one should clarify the treatability and difficulty of the case, sharing the details of symptoms to evaluate one’s own state of mind. If there are aspects that were overlooked, they should be openly clarified without embellishment.

It is crucial to remain calm and consider the options carefully, avoiding hasty or intense actions that may lead to fear. When examining women, it is essential to first consult their close relatives about the symptoms, appearance, and dietary habits; then, proceed according to convenience. For severe cases, one may conduct the examination from a bedside screen, while in less severe cases, examine at the door behind a curtain, always ensuring to cover one's hands with a thin gauze.

In cases involving poor families, the physician should provide their own thin gauze.[147p]

　　寡妇室女，愈加敬谨，此非小节。及其论病，须明白开论辨析，断其为内伤外感，或属杂病，或属阴虚，或内伤而兼外感几分，或外感而兼内伤几分，论方据脉，指下所定，不可少有隐秘。依古成法，参酌时宜年纪，与所处顺逆，及曾服某药否。　　女人经水胎产，男子房室劳逸。
　　虽本于古而不泥于古，真如见其脏腑，然后此心无疑，于人亦不枉误。用药之际，尤宜仔细。　　某经病以某药为君，某为监制，某为引使。　　凡剂料本当出自医家，庶乎新陈炮灸，一一合则，况紧急丸散，岂病家所卒办？但有病寒必欲自制者，听其意向，须依本草注下古法修合，不可逞巧以伤药力。病机稍有疑滞，而药不甚效者，姑待五鼓静坐，潜心推究其源，再为诊察改方，必无不愈。治病既愈，亦医家分内事也。纵守清素，借此治生，亦不可过取重索，但当听其所酬。如病家赤贫，一毫不取，尤见其仁且廉也。[148p]

Widows and unmarried women should be even more respectful and careful, as this is not a trivial matter. When discussing illness, one must clearly analyze and discern whether it is an internal injury or an external affliction; whether it pertains to miscellaneous diseases, or whether it shows signs of Yin deficiency; whether it is primarily an internal injury with some external influence, or primarily an external affliction with some internal injury. Discussing prescriptions should be based on the pulse, following what has been established and avoiding any secrecy.

According to ancient methods, one should adjust based on the patient's age, the circumstances of their situation, and whether they have taken any medications before. Women experience menstrual cycles and childbirth, while men deal with the labor and leisure of sexual activity.

Although based on ancient wisdom, one should not be bound by it. Understanding the organs and functions leads to certainty and prevents misjudgment. Care must be taken when administering medicine. For any specific condition, designate one herb as the sovereign, another as the minister, and others as assistants.

All treatment should stem from qualified practitioners; certainly, the application and formulation of medicines must align with established practices, especially in urgent situations — how can those suffering from ailments manage this themselves? If someone insists on self-administering remedies, their intentions should be respected, but they must adhere to the classical methods of preparation without risking the efficacy of the medicine through clever tricks. Should the pathological conditions remain somewhat unclear, and if the medicine does not yield strong results, it is advisable to wait quietly and reflect deeply on the underlying causes, then reassess and adjust the prescription; recovery is assured.

Once the illness is treated, that remains the task of the physician. Even while adhering to simplicity, when using this to sustain one’s livelihood, one should not overly demand compensation but should accept what is offered. If a patient is extremely poor and does not provide anything, it particularly demonstrates the physician’s compassion and integrity.[148p]

盖人不能报，天必报之。如是而立心，而术有不明不行者哉！　　或问一言为约，曰：不欺而已矣。读入门书而不从头至尾，零星熟得一方一轮，而便谓医者，欺也；熟读而不思悟，融会贯通者，欺也；悟后而不早起，静坐调息，以为诊视之地者，欺也；诊脉而不以实告者，欺也；论方用药，潦草而不精详者，欺也；病愈后而希望贪求，不脱市井风味者，欺也。　　盖不患医之无利，特患医之不明耳。　　屡用屡验而心有所得，不纂集以补报天地，公于人人者，亦欺也。欺则天良日以蔽塞而医道终失，不欺则良知日益发扬而医道愈昌，欺不欺之间，非人之所能与也。[149p]

If a person cannot repay, then heaven will repay them. Thus, with a steadfast heart, is there a technique that is unclear or ineffective? Someone asked about the essence of a single word, and the answer was: merely to not deceive. One who reads medical books but does not go through them from beginning to end, only partially understanding one method or another, is deceiving themselves; one who reads thoroughly but does not comprehend or integrate the knowledge is also deceiving themselves; one who understands but does not rise early to calmly sit and regulate their breath, thinking this suffices for diagnosis, is deceiving themselves; one who diagnoses a pulse but does not accurately convey the truth is deceiving themselves; one who discusses prescriptions and medications carelessly and without detail is deceiving themselves; one who hopes for gain after recovery, yet remains entangled in worldly ways, is deceiving themselves.

It is not the lack of profit that is concerning with a physician, but rather the lack of clarity. Repeatedly using methods that yield results while gaining understanding, yet not compiling this knowledge to repay heaven and earth for the public good, is also deceitful. Deceit clouds one's conscience and causes the medical way to ultimately fail; honesty, on the other hand, allows for the cultivation of one’s moral sense and the flourishing of medicinal practices. The distinction between honesty and deceit is beyond human manipulation.[149p]

　　　　万病回春 【明　龚廷贤】

　　　　　医家十要

　　一存仁心，乃是良箴；博施济众，惠泽斯深。二通儒道，儒医世宝；道理贵明，群书当考。三情脉理，宜分表里；指下既明，沉疴可起。四识病原，坐死敢言；医家至此，始称专门。五知气运，以明岁序；补泻温凉，按时处治。六明经络，认病不错；脏腑洞然，今之扁鹊。七识药性，立方应病；不辨温凉，恐伤性病。八会炮制，火候详细；太过不及，安危所系。九莫嫉妒，因人好恶；天理昭然，速当悔悟。十勿重利，当存仁义；贫富虽殊，药施无二。[150p]

Ten Essentials for Medical Practitioners

  1. Maintain a compassionate heart, which is the best advice; generously help the masses, and your kindness will be profound.
  2. Understand Confucian and Daoist principles, as they are treasures in the world of medicine; clarity in reasoning is valuable, and one must study various texts thoroughly.
  3. Differentiate between emotional and physical conditions; when your understanding is clear, even chronic illnesses can be addressed.
  4. Recognize the origins of diseases; only when a doctor can confidently discuss these matters can they truly be considered specialized.
  5. Understand the flow of Qi to comprehend seasonal changes; adapt treatments of warming or cooling according to the time.
  6. Be familiar with the meridians, and correctly identify diseases; like the famous physician Bian Que, have a complete understanding of internal organs.
  7. Know the properties of medicines and tailor prescriptions to diseases; failing to distinguish between warming and cooling might harm patients with specific conditions.
  8. Master the preparation of medicines, paying attention to the correct heat; too much or too little can determine safety and risk.
  9. Do not be jealous or let personal preferences influence you; the principles of nature are evident, so quickly repent of such thoughts.
  10. Do not prioritize profit; instead, uphold righteousness and benevolence; regardless of wealth or poverty, the use of medicine should be equitable.[150p]

　　　　　病家十要

　　一择明医，于病有裨；不可不慎，生死相随。二肯服药，诸病可却；有等愚人，自家担阁。三宜早治，始则容易；履霜不谨，坚冰即至。四绝空房，自然无疾；倘若犯之，神医无术。五戒恼怒，必须省悟；怒则火起，难以救护。六息妄想，须当静养；念虑一除，精神自爽。七节饮食，调理有则；过则伤神，太饱难克。八慎起居，交际当袪；稍若劳役，元气愈虚。九莫信邪，信之则差；异端诳诱，惑乱人家。十勿惜费，惜之何谓！请问君家，命财孰贵？[151p]

Ten Essentials for the Ill

  1. Choose a capable physician; it benefits the illness. One must be cautious, as life and death are at stake.
  2. Be willing to take medicine; many ailments can be dispelled. There are foolish people who resist treatment themselves.
  3. Seek early treatment; it is easier at the outset. If one is not careful in frosty conditions, hard ice will suddenly arrive.
  4. Avoid empty rooms; naturally, illness will not arise. If one violates this, even the best doctor cannot help.
  5. Avoid anger; self-reflection is necessary. Anger ignites like fire, making rescue difficult.
  6. Cease idle thoughts; one must rest in tranquility. When worries are cleared, the spirit naturally rejuvenates.
  7. Regulate diet and nutrition; there must be principles. Excess damages the spirit; overeating is hard to manage.
  8. Be careful with daily routines and social interactions; even minor labor can deplete vital energy.
  9. Do not trust the misguided; believing them leads to trouble. Heretical notions and deceptions disturb families.
  10. Do not be frugal with expenses; what does saving mean in this context? I ask you, is life or wealth more precious?[151p]

　　　　　医家病家通病

　　一、南方人有患病者，每延医至家，诊视后，止索一方，命人购药于市，不论药之真伪，有无炮制，辄用服之。不敬，不责己之非，惟责医之庸，明日遂易一医，如是者数日，致使病证愈增，而医人亦惑乱，莫知其所以悞也。吁！此由病家之过欤？亦医家之不明欤？　　一、北方人有患病者，每延医至家，不论病之轻重，乃授一二金而索一方剂，刻时奏效，否则即复他求，朝秦暮楚。殊不知人禀有虚实，病感有浅深。[152p]

Doctors and Patients Share Common Ills

  1. In the south, when a person is ill, they often invite a doctor to their home. After the examination, the doctor simply prescribes a remedy and instructs someone to purchase the medicine from the market, without regard for the authenticity or preparation of the medicine, which they then take without question. They do not show reverence, nor do they reflect on their own faults, but only blame the doctor for incompetence. The next day, they simply switch doctors, and this continues for several days, resulting in an increase in symptoms, while the doctors themselves are confused and do not understand the cause. Alas! Is this the fault of the patients? Or the ignorance of the doctors?

  2. In the north, when a person is ill, they invite a doctor to their home, and regardless of the severity of the illness, they hand over a sum of money and request a prescription. They expect immediate results; if not, they quickly seek out another doctor, changing their minds as easily as switching allegiances. They do not realize that each person has their own unique constitution and that illnesses vary in depth and severity.[152p]

且夫感冒腠理之疾，一二剂可愈；至于内伤劳瘵之证，岂投一二剂可愈哉？此习俗之弊，悞于人者多矣，惟智者辨之！　　一、医道，古称仙道也，原为活人。今世之医，多不知此义，每于富者用心，贫者忽略，此固医者之恒情，殆非仁术也。以余论之，医乃生死所寄，责任匪轻，岂可因其贫富而我为厚薄哉？告我同志者，当以太上好生之德为心，慎勿论贫富，均是活人，是亦阴功也。　　一、凡病家延医，乃寄之以生死，理当敬重，慎勿轻藐，贫富不在论财，自尽其诚，稍亵之则非重命者耳。[153p]

The affliction of cold and its symptoms can be cured with one or two doses; however, as for injuries and ailments from exhaustion, can they be cured with just a dose or two? This is a common misunderstanding, and many are misled by it; only the wise can discern!

Firstly, the path of medicine, which was once called the way of immortals, is fundamentally about preserving life. Many modern physicians do not understand this meaning; they often focus on the wealthy while neglecting the poor. This is a habitual sentiment among doctors and is hardly a compassionate practice. In my view, medicine concerns life and death, and the responsibility is grave. How can one discriminate based on wealth? Those who claim to be my peers should regard the virtue of cherishing life highly and should be careful not to discuss wealth, as all are living beings; this too is a hidden merit.

Secondly, when a patient seeks medical aid, they are entrusting their life and death; thus, they ought to be respected. One should not treat them lightly; wealth should not be a factor in assessing their situation. One ought to sincerely dedicate oneself, for any slight disrespect would imply a disregard for life itself.[153p]

更有一等背义之徒，本得医人之力，病愈思财，假言昨作何福，易某人药，所为吝财之计，不归功于一人。吁！使不得其利，又不得其名，此辈之心，亦不仁之甚。　　一、常见今时之人，每求医治，令患者卧于暗室帷帐之中，并不告以所患，止令切脉，至于妇人多不之见，岂能察其声色？更以锦帕之类护其手，而医者又不便亵于问，纵使问之亦不说，此非欲求愈病，将以难医。[154p]

Moreover, there are even more despicable traitors, who, having the power of medicine, upon recovering from illness, seek financial gain with false claims of blessings received yesterday. They exchange medicine for another’s wealth, and do not give credit to anyone. Alas! They cannot reap benefits nor gain reputation; such individuals are truly devoid of compassion.

Firstly, it is often seen in contemporary times that when people seek medical treatment, they are instructed to lie in dark rooms behind curtains, without being informed of their afflictions. They simply allow pulse-taking, and many women are not even seen. How can they perceive their own condition? Furthermore, they cover their hands with silk cloths, making it inconvenient for the physician to inquire. Even if they do ask, the patients do not disclose the truth; this is not a desire for recovery but rather a means of complicating the treatment.[154p]

殊不知古之神医，尚且以望闻问切，四者缺一不可，况今之医未必如古之神，安得以一切脉而洞知脏腑也耶？余书此奉告世之患者，延医至家，罄告所患，今医者对证切脉，了然无疑，则用药无不效矣。昔东坡云：吾求愈疾而已，岂以困医为事哉？　　一、吾道中有等无行之徒，专一夸己之长，形人之短，每至病家，不问疾疴，惟毁前医之过以骇患者。设使前医用药尽是，何复他求？盖为一时或有所偏，未能奏效，岂可概将前药为庸耶？夫医乃仁道，况授受相传，原系一体，同道虽有毫末之差，彼此亦当护庇，慎勿訾毁，斯不失忠厚之心也，戒之戒之！[155p]

Little do they know that even the divine physicians of old relied on observing, listening, asking, and palpating; none of these four can be neglected. How can today's doctors, who may not be as remarkable as those of the past, expect to thoroughly comprehend the viscera by merely feeling the pulse? I write this to inform patients of the world: when a doctor is called to your home, explain your ailments in detail. If the physician then examines your pulse and understands your condition without doubt, the prescribed medicine will surely be effective.

In the past, Dongpo said: "I seek to cure my illness; how can I be a burden to the doctor?"

Firstly, among those in my field, there are individuals without virtue who only boast of their own strengths and expose the weaknesses of others. When they visit a patient, they do not inquire about the illness but instead criticize the previous doctor's mistakes to frighten the patient. Even if the previous physician's medicines were entirely appropriate, what more could one seek? Just because there may have been a temporary oversight that failed to produce results, one cannot generalize that the previous medicine was inept. Medicine is fundamentally a compassionate practice, and since sharing and passing down knowledge is part of this continuum, colleagues must protect and support one another despite minor differences. They should avoid slandering each other, as this reflects a lack of loyalty and integrity. Beware, beware![155p]

　　　　外科正宗 【明　陈实功】

　　　　　医家五戒十要

　　一戒：凡病家大小贫富人等，请观者便可往之，勿得迟延厌弃，欲往而不往，不为平易；药金毋论轻重有无，当尽力一例施与，自然阴隲日增，无伤方寸。　　二戒：凡视妇女及孀妇尼僧人等，必候侍者在傍，然后入房诊视，倘傍无伴，不可自看。假有不便之患，更宜真诚窥睹，虽对内人不可谈，此因闺阃故也。　　三戒：不得出脱病家珠珀珍贵等送家合药，以虚存假换。如果该用，令彼自制入之，倘服不效，自无疑谤。亦不得称赞彼家物色之好。凡此等非君子也。　　四戒：凡救世者，不可行乐登山，携酒游玩：又不可非时离去家中。[156p]

Five Precepts and Ten Essentials of Medical Practitioners

  1. First Precept: Regardless of the patient's size, wealth, or status, if someone seeks your help, do not delay or dismiss them. If you wish to go but do not, it is not easy; do not consider the cost of medicine lightly. You should do your best with each case, and naturally, your reputation will grow, without harming your conscience.

  2. Second Precept: When examining women, widows, or nuns, always wait for an attendant to be present before entering the room for diagnosis. If there is no companion, do not examine them alone. If there are inconvenient issues at hand, you should earnestly observe, but even to close relatives, do not speak of it, due to the nature of the domestic environment.

  3. Third Precept: Do not accept precious gifts or items from patients as a means of barter. If it is truly necessary, have them prepare it themselves. If the treatment does not work, there will be no suspicion of blame. Moreover, do not praise the quality of their possessions; such behavior is unworthy of a gentleman.

  4. Fourth Precept: As someone who saves lives, do not engage in leisure activities like climbing mountains, carrying wine, or traveling for pleasure. Additionally, do not leave your home at inappropriate times.[156p]

凡有抱病至者，必当亲视，用意发药，又要依经写出药帖。必不可杜撰药方，受人驳问。　　五戒：凡娼妓及私伙家请看，亦当正己视如良家子女，不可他意见戏，以取不正，视毕便回。贫窘者药金可璧，看回只可与药，不可再去，以希邪淫之报。　　一要：先知儒理，然后方知医理，或内或外，勤读先古明医确论之书，须旦夕手不释卷，一一参明，融化机变，印之在心，慧之于目，凡临证时自无差谬矣。　　二要：选买药品，必遵雷公炮炙。药有依方修合者，又有因病随时加减者。汤散宜近备，丸丹须预制。常药愈久愈灵，线药越陈越异。药不吝珍，终久必济。　　三要：凡乡井同道之士，不可生轻侮傲慢之心，切要谦和谨慎。[157p]

Whenever someone arrives with an illness, one must personally examine them, carefully prepare the medicine, and write the prescription according to the texts. One must not fabricate prescriptions or be subject to questioning by others.

The five prohibitions: As for courtesans and private practitioners requesting to see patients, one should treat them as one would the children of respectable families, refraining from inappropriate remarks to derive improper benefits, and upon completing the consultation, they should leave. For the impoverished, the cost of medicine may be returned, but one should only dispense medicine and not allow further visits, to avoid temptations of immorality.

First imperative: One must first understand Confucian principles to then grasp medical theories, whether internal or external. It is essential to diligently read the ancient texts of distinguished medical theories, never letting go of them day or night, and to consult each one thoroughly, absorbing the intricate changes, imprinting them in the heart, and discerning them with the eyes so that one will not err when diagnosing patients.

Second imperative: When selecting and purchasing medicines, one must strictly adhere to the methods of Lei Gong (the Thunder God). Medicines should be prepared according to established prescriptions, while some may be adjusted according to the illness as needed. Decoctions and powders should be readily prepared, while pills must be made in advance. Medicines become more effective with age, and those that age well will differ in nature. One should not be stingy with valuable medicines; in the end, they will surely bring benefit.

Third imperative: Among fellow practitioners in the countryside, one must not harbor feelings of contempt or arrogance; it is crucial to remain humble and cautious.[157p]

年尊者恭敬之，有学者师事之，骄傲者逊让之，不及者荐拔之。如此自无谤怨，信和为贵也。　　四要：治家与治病同。人之不惜元气，斫丧太过。百病生焉，轻则支离身体，重则丧命。治家若不固根本而奢华，费用太过，轻则无积，重则贫窘。　　五要：人之受命于天，不可负天之命。凡欲进取，当知彼心顺否，体认天道顺逆。凡顺取，人缘相庆；逆取，子孙不吉。为人何不轻利远害，以防还报之业也？　　六要：凡里中亲友人情，除婚丧疾病庆贺外，其余家务，至于馈送来往之礼，不可求奇好胜。凡飧只可一鱼一菜，一则省费，二则惜禄，谓广求不如俭用。[158p]

Respect for elders fosters deference among students and humility among the proud, while those who fall short benefit from recommendations. In this way, there are no slanders or grievances, and trust is cherished.

Four essentials: Managing a household is akin to treating illness. When people neglect their vital energy and overindulge, numerous ailments arise; mildly, their bodies become weak, severely, they risk death. If a household lacks a solid foundation and indulges in luxury, expenses become excessive; mildly, there are no savings, severely, there is poverty and distress.

Five essentials: Humans receive their mandate from heaven and must not go against it. Those who seek to advance must understand if intentions align with heaven’s will, recognizing what is in accordance with or against nature. When one acts in accordance, relationships flourish; when one acts against, descendants face misfortune. Why not lightly pursue profit and avoid long-term harm, as a precaution against repercussions?

Six essentials: In local relationships with friends and relatives, apart from weddings, funerals, sickness, and congratulations, other household matters including exchanges and gifts should not seek extravagance or rivalry. Meals should consist of one fish and one vegetable: this not only saves expenses but also cherishes resources, implying that frugality is preferable to excessive seeking.[158p]

　　七要：贫穷之家及游食僧道衙门差役人等，凡来看病，不可要他药钱，只当奉药。再遇贫难者，当量力微赠，方为仁术。不然有药而无火食者，命亦难保也。　　八要：凡有所蓄，随其大小，便当置买产业以为根本。不可收买玩器及不紧物件，浪费钱财。又不可做银会酒会，有妨生意。必当一例禁之，自绝谤怨。　　九要：凡室中所用各样物具，俱要精备齐整，不得临时缺少。又古今前贤书籍，及近时明公新刊医理词说，必寻参看，以资学问。此诚为医家之本务也。　　十要：凡奉官衙所请，必要速去，无得怠缓。要诚意恭敬，告明病源，开俱方药。病愈之后，不得图求扁礼，亦不得言说民情，至生罪戾。闲不近公，自当守法。[159p]

Seven Essentials: For those from poor families and traveling monks, when they come for treatment, do not ask them for payment for medicine; simply consider it as providing medicine. If others also encounter poverty, offer small tokens of help according to your ability, as this is the true practice of benevolent medicine. Otherwise, those with medicine but without firewood or food may find their lives difficult to preserve.

Eight Essentials: For all that one possesses, regardless of its size, it is necessary to invest in property as a foundation. Do not purchase trivial items or non-essential objects that waste money. Additionally, do not hold gatherings for silver or alcohol, as this is detrimental to business. This should be strictly prohibited in order to eliminate gossip and resentment.

Nine Essentials: All items used in the household should be well-prepared and organized, with nothing lacking at the last moment. Furthermore, one should seek out ancient and modern respected texts, as well as recently published medical terms and theories, to enhance knowledge. This is indeed the fundamental duty of a physician.

Ten Essentials: Whenever requested by an official office, one must be prompt in response and not delay. It is important to maintain sincerity and respect, clearly state the source of the illness, and prescribe appropriate medicines. After recovery, one should not seek gifts or speak of personal matters, as this may lead to fault or crime. Avoid unnecessary interaction with the government; one should naturally abide by the law.[159p]

　　　　小儿卫生总微论方【宋　撰人不详】
　　　　　医工论

　　凡为医之道，必先正己，然后正物。正己者，谓能明理以尽术也。正物者，谓能用药以对病也。如此，然后事必济而功必着矣。若不能正己，岂能正物？不能正物，岂能愈疾？今冠于篇首，以劝学者。　　凡为医者，性存温雅，志必谦恭，动须礼节，举乃和柔，无自妄尊，不可矫饰。广收方论，博通义理，明运气，晓阴阳，善诊切，精察视，辨真伪，分寒热，审标本，识轻重。疾小不可言大，事易不可去难。贫富用心皆一，贵贱使药无别。苟能如此，于道几希。反是者，为生灵之巨寇。　　凡为医者，遇有请召，不择高下，远近必赴。如到其家，须先问曾请医未曾？又问曾进是何汤药？已未经下？乃可得知虚实也。如已曾经下即虚矣。更可消息参详，则可无悞。又治小儿之法，必明南北禀受之殊，必察土地寒温之异，不可一同施治，古人最为慎耳。[160p]

Discussion on Children's Health

In the practice of medicine, one must first rectify oneself, and then address the ailments of others. To rectify oneself means to understand principles and fully utilize techniques. To rectify others means to accurately apply remedies to cure diseases. Only then will success be assured. If one cannot rectify oneself, how can one rectify others? If one cannot rectify others, how can one cure diseases? This is mentioned at the beginning to encourage learners.

As for those who practice medicine, they should possess a warm and gentle temperament, remain humble in aspirations, observe etiquette in actions, and be harmonious and gentle in demeanor, without self-aggrandizement or pretentiousness. They should broadly gather medicinal knowledge, deeply understand principles, be clear about the movements of Qi, grasp yin and yang, skillfully diagnose through pulse and sight, distinguish between truth and falsehood, differentiate cold and heat, examine root and branch, and identify severity and lightness. Minor illnesses should not be treated as major, and easy matters should not be neglected for difficult ones. The attention to detail should be the same for the poor and rich alike; there should be no distinction in the usage of medicines between nobility and commoners. If one can do so, they are close to the true path. Conversely, failing to do so makes one a great bane to living beings.

When a physician is summoned, they should not discriminate by status or distance, and must attend indiscriminately. Upon arriving at the home, they should first inquire whether medical help has been previously sought, and what medicines were administered. If there has been treatment already, then one can discern the truth of the situation. Furthermore, when treating children, one must be aware of the distinct characteristics based on geographical differences and must carefully consider the varying temperatures of the locality; treatments should not be uniform, as the ancients were particularly cautious in this regard.[160p]

　　　　医宗必读 【明　李中梓】

　　　　　不失人情论

　　尝读《内经》至方盛衰论而殿之曰：不失人情。未尝不瞿然起，喟然叹轩岐之人人深也。夫不失人情，医家所甚急，然戛戛乎难之矣。大约人情之类有三：一曰病人之情，二曰傍人之情，三曰医人之情。　　所谓病人之情者：五脏各有所偏，七情各有所胜，阳脏者宜凉，阴脏者宜热，耐毒者缓剂无功，不耐毒者竣剂有害，此脏气之不同也。动静各有欣厌，饮食各有爱憎；性好吉者危言见非，意多忧者慰安云伪；未信者忠告难行，善疑者深言则忌，此好恶之不同也。富者多任性而禁戒勿遵，贵者多自尊而骄恣悖理，此交际之不同也。贫者衣食不周，况乎药饵？贱者焦劳不适，怀抱可知，此调治之不同也。[161p]

Essential Readings in Medicine
(Ming - Li Zhongzi)

On Not Losing Human Feelings

I once read the "Inner Classic" and came across the discussion on the variations of treatments, which concludes with the phrase: "Not losing human feelings." I couldn't help but be struck by it, sighing deeply at how profound this understanding is for practitioners. The notion of not losing human feelings is something that physicians must urgently consider, yet it proves to be quite difficult. Generally, human feelings can be categorized into three types: the feelings of the patient, the feelings of others, and the feelings of the physician.

The so-called feelings of the patient are characterized by the distinct conditions of the five organs and the varying responses of the seven emotions. Yang organs require coolness, whereas Yin organs require warmth. Those who can tolerate toxins may find mild treatments ineffective, while those who cannot tolerate them may suffer harm from strong treatments; this reflects the differences in the energies of the organs. Each person's preferences and aversions vary with activity and rest, as well as with food and drink. Those who are inclined to good fortune often dismiss warnings, while those who are more anxious may find comfort in false reassurances. For the untrusting, sincere advice is hard to accept, and those who are skeptical may reject deeper insights out of caution.

Moreover, the wealthy often indulge their desires and disregard restrictions, while the noble may act with pride and defy reason; these depict the differences in social interactions. The poor struggle to meet basic needs, making it difficult to consider medicine and remedies. The lowly labor excessively with little remedy, and their burdens are evident; these illustrate the variations in treatment approaches.[161p]

有良言甫信，谬说更新，多歧亡羊，终成画饼，此无主之为害也。有最畏出奇，惟求稳当，车薪杯水，难免败亡，此过慎之为害也。有境缘不偶，营求未遂，深情牵挂，良药难医，此得失之为害也。有性急者遭迟病，更医而致杂投；有性缓者遭急病，濡滞而成难挽，此缓急之为害也。有参、朮沾唇惧补，心先痞塞；硝、黄入口畏攻，神即飘扬，此成心之为害也。有讳疾不言，有隐情难告，甚而故隐病状，试医以脉，不知自古神圣，未有舍望闻问而独凭一脉者；且如气口脉盛则知伤食，至于何日受伤，所伤何物，岂能以脉知哉？此皆病人之情，不可不察者也。　　所谓傍人之情者：或执有据之论，而病倩未必相符；或兴无本之言，而医理何曾梦见。[162p]

There are sound words that, once believed, can lead to misunderstandings and misinterpretations—many paths lead to losing one’s way, ultimately resulting in empty promises; this is the harm of lack of guidance. Some are overly cautious, seeking only safety, but with limited resources, they inevitably face failure; this is the harm of excessive caution. There are circumstances that do not align, leading to unfulfilled desires, deep emotional ties, and even the best remedies may be ineffective; this is the harm of gains and losses. Some act too hastily and face delayed ailments, complicating their treatment; others are too slow and encounter urgent problems, leading to a situation that is hard to rectify; this is the harm of haste and delay. Some fear the remedies due to their bitter taste, leading to mental blockages; others fear aggressive treatments, causing their spirit to become unsettled; this is the harm of the mind. Some avoid discussing their ailments, harbor hidden troubles, and even conceal symptoms, merely relying on pulse diagnosis without understanding that since ancient times, wise figures have never depended solely on pulse without considering other symptoms; for instance, if the pulse shows fullness, one knows it could be due to food damage, but the exact time and nature of the injury cannot be discerned solely through the pulse. These are emotional aspects of patients, which must not be overlooked.

As for the feelings of others: some may adhere to opinions grounded in evidence, while the illness may not correspond; others may speak without basis, and the medical principles have never actually appeared in dreams.[162p]

或操是非之柄，同我者是之，异己者非之，而真是真非莫辨；或执肤浅之见，头痛者救头，脚痛者救脚，而孰标孰本谁知？或尊贵执言难抗，或密戚偏见难回。又若荐医动关生死，有意气之私厚而荐者，有庸浅之偶效而荐者，有信其利口而荐者，有贪其酬报而荐者，甚至熏犹不辨，妄肆品评，誉之则跖可为舜，毁之则凤可作鸮，致怀奇之士，拂衣而去，使深危之病，坐而待亡。此皆傍人之情，不可不察者也。　　所谓医人之情者：或巧语诳人，或甘言悦听，或强辩相欺，或危言相恐，此便侫之流也。或结纳亲知，或修好僮仆，或营求卜荐，或不邀自赴，此阿謟之流也。有腹无藏墨，诡言神授，目不识丁，假托秘传，此欺诈之流也。有望闻问切，漫不关心，枳朴归苓，到手便撮，妄谓人愚我明，人生我熟，此孟浪之流也。[163p]

Some manipulate the truth, siding with those who agree with them and denying those who do not, making it difficult to discern true from false; some cling to shallow views, treating headaches with head remedies and foot pain with foot remedies, but who can distinguish what is fundamental? Some hold on to prestigious opinions that are hard to counter, while others have biases that are hard to correct. It is like recommending a doctor on matters of life and death, where some recommend out of personal affection, others for superficial effects, some trust their reputation, and others for greedy rewards. Even when they are confused, they carelessly critique; if praised, someone like Zhi can become Shun, while if criticized, a phoenix can become an owl. This leads to talented individuals withdrawing, leaving serious illnesses to wait for demise. These are all human sentiments that ought to be scrutinized.

As for the emotions involved in healing: some use crafty language to deceive, some sweeten their words to please, others argue passionately to mislead, and some use alarmist speech to intimidate—these belong to the deceitful category. Some build connections with acquaintances, some improve relations with servants, some seek recommendations, and some show up uninvited—these belong to the sycophants. There are those who express empty ideas without substance, making claims of divine inspiration while being illiterate, pretending to possess secret teachings—these are the frauds. Others observe and inquire without real concern, gathering what they can without thought and mistakenly believing that they are wise while others are foolish—these are the reckless.[163p]

有嫉妒性成，排挤为事，阳若同心，阴为浸润，是非颠倒，朱紫混淆，此谗妒之流也。有贪得无知，轻忽人命，如病在危疑，良医难必，极其详慎，犹冀回春，若辈贪功，妄轻投剂，至于败坏，嫁谤自文，此贪幸之流也。有意见各持，异同不决，曲高者和寡，道高者谤多，一齐之傅几何，众楚之咻易乱，此庸浅之流也。有素所相知，苟且图功；有素不相识，偶延辨证。病家既不识医，则倏赵倏钱，医家莫肯任怨，则惟苓惟梗。或延医众多，互相观望；或利害攸系，彼此避嫌。惟求免怨，诚然得矣。坐失机宜，谁之咎乎？此由知医不真而任医不专也。　　凡若此者，孰非人情？而人情之详，尚多难尽。圣人以不失人情为戒，欲令学者思之慎之，勿为陋习所中耳。虽然，必期不失，未免迁就，但迁就既碍于病情，不迁就又碍于人情，有必不可迁就之病情，而复有不得不迁就之人情，且奈之何哉！故曰：戛戛乎难之矣。[164p]

There exists a jealousy that leads to exclusion, where the sun may share a common heart while the shadows permeate; right and wrong are reversed, and distinctions are blurred. This is the flow of slander and jealousy. There are those who are greedy and ignorant, dismissing human life lightly; when illness is precarious, even a good doctor cannot be certain. Despite thorough care, there is still hope for recovery, yet the greedy, in their eagerness for achievement, irresponsibly administer treatments that lead to ruin, thus spreading slander to disguise their failures. This is the flow of greed and fortune.

There are opinions held individually, leading to indecision over similarities and differences. Those who aspire to high ideals often have few allies, while those who strive for lofty paths frequently face slander. How much can be collectively shared, and how easily can disturbances arise from many voices? This is the flow of superficiality.

Some, who are longtime acquaintances, work opportunistically for gain; others, who are strangers, engage in ad hoc debates. If the patient does not recognize the physician, they hastily seek financial help, while physicians, unwilling to bear grudges, can only rely on superficial remedies. Some engage many doctors, watching each other; others, with vested interests, avoid suspicion. They only seek to avoid blame; indeed, they may succeed. But who is to blame for missing opportunities? This results from a lack of true understanding of the physician and reliance on unqualified doctors.

In all these cases, is there not a depth to human emotions? Yet, the complexities of human sentiment are too numerous to exhaust fully. The sage warns against losing sight of human feelings, urging learners to reflect and be cautious, lest they fall into bad habits. Nevertheless, to avoid loss is not without its compromises. Compromises may exacerbate the illness, while not compromising can strain human relations. There are inevitable conditions where one must compromise on illness, and yet there are also unavoidable social dynamics that require concessions. How, then, can one navigate this? Hence it is said: “How difficult it is!”[164p]

　　　　　行方智圆心小胆大论

　　孙思邈之祝医者曰：行欲方而智欲圆，心欲小而胆欲大。嗟乎！医之神良，尽乎此矣。宅心醇谨，举动安和，言无轻吐，目无乱观，忌心勿起，贪念罔生，毋忽贫贱，毋惮疲劳，检医典而精求，对疾苦而悲悯，如是者谓之行方。禀赋有厚薄，年岁有老少，身形有肥瘦，性情有缓急，境地有贵贱，风气有柔强，天时有寒热，昼夜有轻重，气色有吉凶，声音有高下，受病有久新，运气有太过不及，知常知变，能神能明，如是者谓之智圆。[165p]

A Discussion on the Conduct of Healing: Straight in Action, Round in Wisdom, Small in Heart, and Great in Courage

Sun Simiao's advice to physicians states: "Actions should be straight, and wisdom should be round; the heart should be small, but the courage should be great." Truly! The essence of medical skill resides in this. Maintain a sincere and cautious heart, act with tranquility and harmony, speak without frivolous words, observe without chaotic gazes, avoid letting unwholesome thoughts arise, never give in to greed, be not dismissive of poverty, do not shy away from fatigue, diligently examine medical texts, and seek to understand suffering with compassion. Such practice is called "straight in action."

Understand that talents vary in depth, ages differ in youth and old age, body types range from fat to thin, temperaments can be slow or quick, circumstances can be rich or poor, social climates may be gentle or strong, climatic conditions shift between cold and heat, day and night have their light and heavy moments, appearances reflect good or ill fortune, voices have their highs and lows, illnesses may be longstanding or new, and life circumstances may be excessive or deficient. Knowing the constant and the variable, possessing spirit and clarity, such qualities are referred to as "round in wisdom."[165p]

望闻问切宜详，补泻寒热须辨，尝思人命至重，冥报难逃，一旦差讹，永劫莫忏，乌容不慎？如是者谓之心小。补即补而泻即泻，热斯热而寒斯寒，抵当承气，时用回春，姜附理中，恒投起死，析理详明，勿持两可，如是者谓之胆大。四者似分而实合也。世未有详谨之士，执成法以伤人，灵变之人，败名节以损己。行方者智必圆也，心小则惟惧或失，胆大则药知其证。或大攻，或小补，似乎胆大，不知不如是则病不解，是胆大适所以行其小心也。故心小胆大者合而成智圆，心小胆大智圆者合而成行方也。世皆疑方则有碍乎圆，小则有妨乎大，故表而出之。[166p]

The principles of observation, listening, questioning, and examining should be thorough. One must distinguish between tonifying and purging, and recognize the variations of cold and heat. Life is incredibly precious, and the consequences of error are inescapable; if one makes a mistake, there can be no atonement, no matter how long one may seek it. How can one afford to be careless? Such is called a little heart. To tonify is to tonify, and to purge is to purge; heat is heat, and cold is cold. Use appropriate methods according to the situation, apply recovery techniques, and administrate ginger in moderation. Constantly strive to restore life; analyze principles thoroughly and avoid indecision. Those who can act thus are called bold. The four aspects may seem separate but are fundamentally connected.

In the world, there has not been a meticulous scholar who uses established methods to harm others, nor a resourceful person who damages their reputation and integrity for personal loss. Those who are skilled in treatments will have complete wisdom. A little heart fears mistakes, while a bold heart understands the diagnosis of medicines. Sometimes, a powerful remedy or a minor supplement may seem brash, but ignorance of the balance can lead to unresolved ailments; thus, boldness allows one to exercise caution effectively.

Hence, a small heart paired with a bold spirit leads to complete wisdom, while a small heart and bold spirit together cultivate effective medical practices. In this way, suspicion of methods may disrupt wisdom, and caution may hinder boldness; therefore, these concepts must be articulated and expressed.[166p]

　　　　本草经疏 【明　缪希雍】

　　　　　祝医五则

　　凡人疾病，皆由不惜众生身命，竭用人财，好杀禽兽昆虫，好棰楚下贱，甚则枉用毒刑，加诸无罪，种种业因，感此苦报。业作医师，为人司命，见诸苦恼，当兴悲悯，详检方书，精求医道，谛察深思，务期协中。常自思惟，药不对病，病不对机，二旨或乖，则下咽不返。人命至重，冥报难逃，勿为一时衣食，自贻莫忏之罪于千百劫，戒之哉！宜惧不宜喜也。　　凡为医师，当先读书；凡欲读书，当先识字。字者，文之始也。不识字义，宁解文理？文理不通，动成窒碍。[167p]

The Treatise on Herbal Medicine: Commentary by Miao Xiyong in the Ming Dynasty

Five Principles for Healers

All human ailments arise from a disregard for the lives of countless beings, exhausting human and material resources, and a tendency to kill birds, beasts, and insects. They may even unjustly impose punishment on the innocent. These various actions generate karma that leads to suffering in return. Those who practice medicine and serve as healers must feel compassion when witnessing suffering. They should carefully examine medical texts, diligently seek the path of healing, and thoroughly contemplate their practices, aiming for harmony with the natural order.

One must constantly reflect: if the remedy does not match the illness, and the illness does not connect with the right treatment, it could lead to irreversible consequences. Human life is of utmost importance, and retribution is inescapable. Do not sacrifice your conscience for momentary gain, as it may result in untold suffering in future lives—beware of this! Fear the repercussions rather than rejoice in temporary comforts.

For anyone aspiring to be a healer, they must first engage in scholarly study; and for those wishing to study, they must first learn to read. Literacy is the beginning of learning. Without understanding the meaning of characters, how can one grasp the texts? If the meanings are unclear, it becomes an obstacle to understanding.[167p]

虽诗书满目，于神不染，触途成滞，何由省入？譬诸面墙，亦同木偶，望其拯生民之疾苦，顾不难哉？故昔称太医，今曰儒医。太医者读书穷理，本之身心，验之事物，战战兢兢，求中于道，造次之际，罔敢或肆者也。外此则俗工耳，不可以言医矣。　　凡为医师，先当识药。药之所产，方隅不同则精粗顿异，收采不时则力用全乖；又或市肆饰伪，足以混真。苟非确认形质，精尝气味，鲜有不为其误者。譬诸将不知兵，立功何自？医之于药，亦犹是其。既识药矣，宜习修事。[168p]

Although one may be well-versed in poetry and prose, if one's spirit remains untainted, and if one becomes stagnant upon encountering the path, how can one achieve enlightenment? It is like facing a wall or being akin to a wooden puppet—desiring to alleviate the suffering of the people, yet finding it not difficult? Hence, in ancient times, they were called great physicians; today, we refer to them as Confucian physicians. Great physicians study deeply to understand principles, focusing on both body and mind, observing the world with cautious vigilance, seeking the middle path, and in moments of urgency, dare not act recklessly. Anything less would merely be considered common craft and not worthy of being called medicine.

For anyone who wishes to be a physician, it is essential to first understand medicine. The origin of medicine varies by region, affecting its quality; if it is harvested at the wrong time, its efficacy is entirely disrupted. Furthermore, markets may present counterfeit products that can easily confuse the genuine. Unless one recognizes the true qualities and diligently tests the properties and flavors, it is rare to avoid being misled. Just as a general who does not understand military strategy cannot achieve victories, a physician who is ignorant of medicine is in a similar plight. Having understood the medicines, one should then practice diligently in this field.[168p]

雷公炮炙固为大法，或有未尽，可以意通，必期躬亲，勿图苟且。譬诸饮食，烹调失度，尚不益人，反能增害，何况药物关于躯命者也？可不慎诸！　　凡作医师，宜先虚怀，灵知空洞，本无一物。苟执我见，便与物对，我见坚固，势必轻人。我是人非，与境角立，一灵空窍，动为所塞。虽日亲至人，终不获益，白首故吾，良可悲已。执而不化，害加于人，清夜深思，宜生愧耻。况人之才识，自非生知，必假问学。[169p]

The thunder god's cannon is indeed a great method; however, there may be aspects that are not fully expressed. One must understand the essence and work diligently, without seeking superficial gains. This can be likened to food: if cooking is poorly done, it will not benefit people and may even cause harm. How much more cautious one must be with medicines that relate to life and death!

One must be careful about this! When becoming a physician, it is essential to remain humble, with an open mind, empty of preconceived notions. If one stubbornly clings to their own views, they will confront the world, becoming rigid and dismissive of others. This attitude will block the flow of wisdom. Even if one is close to others, they will ultimately gain no benefit. Growing old without gaining knowledge is indeed a sorrowful fate. If one holds onto rigid ideas and fails to transform, they may bring harm to others. In the quietness of the night, one ought to reflect deeply and feel shame. Moreover, one’s talents and insights are not innate; they must be acquired through learning and inquiry.[169p]

问学之益，广博难量，脱不虚怀，何由纳受？不耻无学，而耻下问，师心自圣，于道何益！苟非至愚，能不儆省乎？　　医师不患道术不精，而患取金不多。舍其本业，专事旁求，假宠贵人，冀其口吻，以希世重，纵得多金，无拔苦力，念当来世，岂不酬偿？作是思惟，是苦非乐，故当勤求道术，以济物命，纵有功效，任其自酬，勿责厚报，等心施治，勿轻贫贱，如此则德植厥躬，鬼神幽赞矣。　　上来所祝五条，皆关切医师，才品道术，利济功过。仰愿来学，腑从吾祝，则进乎道而不囿于技矣。讵非生人之至幸，斯道之大光也哉！[170p]

The benefits of studying are broad and difficult to measure; without a humble heart, how can one accept knowledge? It is shameful to lack learning, yet shameful to ask someone of lower status. A teacher's heart is sacred, but what benefit does it bring to the path? If one is not utterly foolish, can they not reflect upon this?

A physician does not worry about their skills being inadequate, but rather about not earning enough money. By abandoning their true vocation and seeking external favors, hoping for the approval of the influential to gain worldly esteem, even if they acquire great wealth, they cannot escape the toil. Considering the next life, is there not a recompense for this?

Such thoughts are painful rather than joyful, which is why one should diligently seek knowledge and skills to benefit others' lives. Even if one achieves effectiveness, let it reward itself rather than seeking excessive returns. Treat all with an equal heart, and do not look down on the poor. By doing so, virtue will take root in oneself, and even the spirits will silently approve.

The five blessings just mentioned are all connected to physicians: their talents and skills, and the benefits and contributions they make. I earnestly hope that you will learn and follow my blessings, advancing in the path without being confined to mere techniques. Is it not the greatest fortune for living beings and the greatest illumination of this path?[170p]

　　　　医门法律 【清　喻昌】

　　　　　治病

　　昌按春秋时左氏谭医理甚悉，汉儒已不习医。太史公作仓公等列传鲜所发明，况其他乎！其后如华元化传，寝涉妖妄，医脉之断，实儒者先断之也。有唐列方技，无足怪矣。《九灵山房集》所论医者当博极群书，求圣贤之意旨，明造化之会归，其属望顾不大欤！　　医之为道，非精不能明其理，非博不能至其约。是故前人立教，必使之先读儒书，明《易》理、《素问》，虽《本草》、《脉经》而不少略者何？盖非四书无以通义理之精微，非《易》无以知阴阳之消长，非《素问》无以识病，非《本草》无以识药，非《脉经》无从诊候而知寒热虚实之证。圣贤示人，略举其端而已。后学必须会群书之长，参所见而施治之，然后为可。[171p]

Medical Laws of the Medical Field
(Qing Dynasty, Yu Chang)

Treating Illness

Chang notes that during the Spring and Autumn period, Zuo's commentary was well-versed in medical principles, while Han scholars had ceased to study medicine. The Grand Historian has little to mention regarding those like Cang Gong, let alone others! Later, works such as "Hua Yuanhua's Biography" delve into the chaotic and superstitious, where it was indeed scholars who first determined the pulse diagnosis. There are many prescriptions from the Tang dynasty, which are not surprising. Those who discuss medicine in the "Jiu Ling Shan Fang Ji" should broadly read a variety of texts, seek the intentions of sages, and understand the return of natural laws. Is their ambition not vast?

The path of medicine requires precision to illuminate its principles and breadth to grasp its essentials. Therefore, previous scholars established teachings that required students to first read Confucian texts, understanding the principles of the "Yi," the "Suwen," and not to neglect the "Bencao" and "Mai Jing." Without the Four Books, it is impossible to comprehend the subtlety of meanings; without the "Yi," one cannot understand the changes of yin and yang; without the "Suwen," one cannot recognize illnesses; without the "Bencao," one cannot identify medicines; and without the "Mai Jing," one has no method to diagnose and understand symptoms of cold, heat, deficiency, or excess. Sages have provided only a brief outline for people. Later scholars must integrate the strengths of various texts, apply what they observe in practice, and only then can they be deemed competent.[171p]

　　病有六大失：失于不审，失于不信，失于过时，失于不择医，失于不知病，失于不知药。又《史记》云：骄恣不伦于理，一不治；轻身重财，二不治；衣食不能适，三不治；阴阳并脏气不定，四不治；形羸不能服药，五不治；信巫不信医，六不治。今时病家，此其通弊矣。　　孙思邈曰：世间多有病人亲友故旧交游来问疾，其人曾不经事，未读方书，自骋了了，诈作明能，谭说异端，或言是虚，或道是实，或云是风，或云是气，纷纷谬说，种种不同，破坏病人心意，不知孰是，迁延未就，时不待人，歘然至祸，各自散走，此种情态，今时尤甚。是须好人及好名医，识病深浅，探赜方书，博览古今，方可倚任，不尔大误人事。[172p]

There are six major failures in dealing with illness: failing to examine properly, failing to trust, being out of date, failing to choose the right physician, not understanding the disease, and not knowing the medicine. The "Records of the Grand Historian" states: Those who are arrogant and disregard reason will not be treated; those who value wealth over life will not be treated; those whose clothing and food are unsuitable will not be treated; those whose yin and yang and internal qi are unsettled will not be treated; those whose bodies are weak and cannot take medicine will not be treated; those who believe in shamans rather than physicians will not be treated. In today's society, these are the common issues faced by patients.

Sun Simiao said: Many sick people have friends and old acquaintances come to inquire about their illnesses, yet these individuals have no experience, have not read medical texts, and self-pretend to be knowledgeable. They speak nonconventional views, some say it is illusory, some say it is real, some say it is wind, some say it is qi, with a multitude of erroneous claims, all differing, which confuse the patient’s thoughts, leaving them unsure of what is true. Time does not wait for anyone, and disaster suddenly arrives, causing everyone to scatter. This kind of situation is particularly concerning nowadays. Therefore, it is crucial to rely on good people and reputable physicians who understand the depth of diseases, thoroughly explore medical texts, and have broad knowledge of both ancient and modern practices; otherwise, it will lead to great misunderstandings and errors in treatment.[172p]

　　　　寓意草 【清　喻昌】

　　　　　先议病后议药

　　从上古以至今时，一代有一代之医，虽神圣贤明，分量不同，然必不能舍规矩准绳以为方圆平直也。故治病必先识病，识病然后讲药。药者所以胜病者也。识病则千百药中，任举一二种用之且通神；不识病则歧多而用眩。凡药皆可伤人，况于性最偏驳者乎？迩来习医者众，医学愈荒，遂成一议药不讲病之世界，其夭枉不可胜悼。或以为杀运使然，不知天道岂好杀恶生耶？每见仕宦家，诊毕即令定方，以示慎重，初不论病从何起，药以何应，致医师以模棱迎合之术，妄为议论。迨药之不效，诿于无药，非无药也，可以胜病之药，以不识病情而未敢议用也。厄哉！《灵枢》、《素问》、《难经》、《甲乙》无方之书，全不考究，而后来一切有方之书，奉为灵宝。如朱丹溪一家之言，其《脉因证治》一书，先论脉，次因，次证，后乃论治，其书反不行；而《心法》一书，群方错杂，则共宗之。[173p]

The Meaning of Herbs 【Qing Dynasty, Yu Chang】

First Discuss the Illness, Then Discuss the Medicine

From ancient times to the present, each generation has its physicians, though they may differ in wisdom and stature, they must not abandon rules and standards to seek balance and clarity. Therefore, in treating illness, one must first understand the illness; only after understanding the illness can one discuss the medicine. Medicine is the means to overcome illness. If one understands the illness, they can choose one or two appropriate remedies from thousands of medicines, and these remedies will work effectively; if one does not understand the illness, there are many different approaches which may confuse the treatment. All medicines can harm a person, especially those that are the most obstinate.

Recently, there has been an increase in those studying medicine, yet medical practice has grown more chaotic, resulting in a world where medicine is discussed without consideration of the illness, which leads to countless tragedies. Some attribute this to fate, not knowing that the natural order does not favor killing or harming life. I often see families in officialdom, after a diagnosis, hastily prescribing a treatment to show their caution while failing to consider the source of the illness or how the medicine aligns with it; this leads physicians to resort to ambiguous discussions. When the medicine fails, they blame the lack of effective medicine, when in fact, effective medicines exist, but they do not dare to use them due to their ignorance of the disease.

Alas! Treatises like Ling Shu, Su Wen, Nan Jing, and Jia Yi, which contain fundamental principles, are seldom studied, while later works that do have prescriptions are venerated as treasures. For instance, Zhu Danxi's writing, Pulse Diagnosis and Treatment, discusses pulse first, then the cause, followed by symptoms, and lastly, treatment; yet this approach has fallen out of favor. Meanwhile, the book Xing Fa is filled with assorted prescriptions, forming a common doctrine.[173p]

又《本草》止述药性之功能，人不加嗜，及缪氏《经疏》，兼述药性之过劣，则必悬之肘后。不知草木之性，亦取其偏，以通人之用，其过劣不必言也，言之而弃置者众矣。曷不将《本草》诸药尽行删抹，独留无过之药五七十种而用之乎？其于《周礼》令医人采毒药以供医事之旨，及历代帝王恐本草为未备，而博采增益之意，不大刺谬乎？欲破此惑，无如议病精详。病经议明，则有是病即有是药，病千变药亦千变。且勿论造化生心之妙，即某病之以某药为良，某药为劫者，至是始有定名。若不论病，则药之良毒善恶，何从定之哉？可见药性所谓良毒善恶，与病体所谓良毒善恶不同也。而不知者，必欲执药性为去取，何其陋耶？故昌之议病，非得已也。昔人登坛指顾，后效不爽前言；聚米如山，先事已饶硕画。医虽小道，何独不然？昌即不能变俗，实欲借此榜样，阐发病机，其能用不能用何计焉？[174p]

The "Compendium of Materia Medica" only describes the functions of medicinal properties, without addressing people's cravings. Additionally, the commentary by Miao includes both the virtues and flaws of these properties, which should necessarily be neglected.
